Subject Matter of Logic: Place and State
Fragmentary discussion on the subject matter of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter (mawḍūʿ) of a discipline and distinguish the categories of place (makān) and state (ḥāl) in logical discussions.

Core Idea
The subject matter of logic includes categories like place and state, defining the scope of rational inquiry.

This lesson clarifies what logic studies, focusing on its subject matter and the categories of place and state as foundational concepts in Islamic logic. Even through a corrupt passage, we can glimpse how early logicians framed the domain of logical analysis. Understanding these categories helps us see how logic organises knowledge in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognise 'mawḍūʿ' as the subject matter
The term 'mawḍūʿ' (موضوع) refers to the primary topic of a discipline. In logic, the subject matter is the focus of investigation—here, the categories of place and state.
Later works specify mawḍūʿ more precisely; here it is used broadly.
2
Identify the categories 'makān' and 'ḥāl'
The passage mentions 'makān' (مكان) and 'ḥāl' (حال) as categories. In Aristotelian logic, these are among the ten categories that classify beings. Place and state qualify how something exists.
3
Connect categories to definitions in Islamic sciences
In Islamic logic, these categories help define terms precisely. For example, in fiqh, the ruling on an action may depend on the place (e.g., mosque) or state (e.g., travel). Recognising them sharpens definitions.

Source Text

ان موضوع المكان والحال معطيه احمد وحاله فيكول ین انسان عامر استان سم بالمال له لاله اسم و طرف من الطلاق و اللي عده ای به بان انه مساو عام و انسان بطلان العالم ان امن علی میان والا کون سماء مال مول لو انت في المنام العاملونات الية الأفراد الك سارع يطلق اسماء ما عليها مال افراد الانسان يمان لما اسم مدولار على الم
Indeed, the subject of place and state is given by Ahmad and his condition says: 'Is there a flourishing human? A standard name with property for him? For him a name and a side of divorce and what counts as such? That it is equal to general and human vainness of the world? If you trust between? And if not be a sky property? If you were in sleep, the workers ... of the individuals ... quickly gives names upon what property individuals of human ... for what name?'

Commentary
This passage appears to be defining or discussing the subject matter of logic with respect to categories like place (مكان) and state (حال). The text is highly corrupted, but it seems to be an attempt to enumerate aspects of definition or predication. The mention of 'human' (انسان), 'general' (عام), and 'equal' (مساو) suggests a discussion of universals and their application. The fragmentary nature prevents a clear logical analysis, but the intent seems to be definitional, possibly outlining the domains of logical investigation. Due to the poor OCR, the commentary is tentative.

General and Specific Possibility in Islamic Logic
Author defines and contrasts general and specific possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) and specific possibility (al-imkan al-khass), and explain why the necessary being is included in general possibility but excluded from specific possibility.

Core Idea
General possibility covers everything not impossible; specific possibility covers only contingent beings.

Islamic logicians refined the concept of possibility (imkan) to avoid confusion in theological arguments. This passage from a commentary on Ibn Sina's al-Isharat clarifies the two senses of possibility and corrects a common misinterpretation that would narrow the general sense too much.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
General possibility is the negation of necessity in one side (existence or non-existence). Specific possibility is the negation of necessity in both sides simultaneously.
The passage states: 'general possible is that to which applies the absence of necessity in one of the two sides; specific possible is that to which applies the absence of necessity on both sides together.'
2
Apply to the necessary being
The necessary being (wajib) is possible in the general sense because it is not impossible (i.e., not necessarily non-existent). But it is not possible in the specific sense because it cannot not exist (i.e., its non-existence is impossible).
The passage explicitly says 'the necessary is outside this possible' when speaking of specific possibility.
3
Understand the corrective
The author criticizes Ibn Sina's phrasing that limits possibility to 'what accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence', which is only the possibility of existence. He argues that possibility also includes the side of non-existence, so general possibility must be broader.
The passage says: 'So possibility is more general than possibility in existence or possibility in non-existence.'
4
Recognize the relation
General possibility includes specific possibility as a subset, and also includes the necessary (which lacks necessity in non-existence) and the impossible (which lacks necessity in existence).
The passage notes that general possible applies to both sides of the specific possible, hence the name 'general'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول صراحة: [ واعلم أنَّ في لفظ الشَّيخ هي هنا نوع إشكال؛ لأنَّه قال: الإمكان إما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، فقد خصَّص هذا الإمكان بما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، وليس كذلك؛ فإن هذا الإمكان حاصل أيضًا في سلب ضرورة الوجود، ألا ترى أنَّ إمكان العدم بهذا المعنى محمول على الممتنع والممكن الخاص؛ لأن قولك يمكن أن لا يكون بهذا المعنى محمول على الممتنع والممكن الخاص، فالإمكان إذن أعم من الإمكان في الوجود أو الإمكان في العدم، والذي يلزم سلب ضرورة العدم هو إمكان الوجود لا الإمكان المطلق، فكأنَّه فسَّر العام بالخاص. فالواجب أن يقال: الإمكان ما يلازم سلب الضَّرورة في أحد جانبي الوجود والعدم، فإن كان في جانب الوجود فهو الذي يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم، وإنْ كان في جانب العدم فهو الذى يلازم سلب ضرورة الوجود. قال: وإما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب الضرورة في العدم والوجود إلى آخره؛ أقول: قد عرفت أن الممكن العام هو الذي يصدق عليه عدم الضَّرورة في أحد طرفي الوجود أو العدم، والممكن الخاص هو الذى يصدق عدم الضّرورة على طرفيه معًا، فحينئذ يكون الممكن العام صادقًا على طرفي الممكن الخاص، فلأجل ذلك نقلوا اسم الممكن إليه من المعنى الأوّل والواجب خارج عن هذا الممكن والقسمة بحسبه ثلاثة: إما واجبة، أو ممكنة، أو ممتنعة. ولفظ الكتاب هي هنا أيضًا مشعر بأنَّ الإمكان الخاص ليس هو نفس سلب الضّرورتين، بل هو أمر يلازم ذلك السلب، ونحن قد بيّنا فى كتابنا الكبير أنَّه يمتنع أن يكون أمرًا ثبوتيّا.
وقد بحث نفس هذه المسائل في المنطق الكبير (و ۱۹ ظ): فإنَّه إما أن يُعنى به ما يلازم سلب الضرورة العدم وهي الامتناع على ما هو موضوع له في الوضع الأوَّل . وهنالك كلّ ما ليس بممكن فهو ممتنع، وهذا الإمكان من جملة ما يحمل على الواجب. وإمَّا أَنْ يُعنى به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم والوجود جميعًا على ما هو موضوع له بحسب النقل حتَّى أنَّ الشَّيء يصدق عليه الإمكان الأوّل في جانبي النَّفي والإثبات، فيقال ممكن أن يكون وممكن أن لا يكون أي غير ممتنع أن يكون وغير ممتنع أن لا يكون. فلما كان الإمكان بالمعنى الأوَّل قد يصدق في الجانبين موافقا ومخالفًا خصه الخاص باسم الإمكان وخرج الواجب عن البين فصارت الأشياء بحسبه إما ممكنة وإما ممتنعة وإما واجبة لا من حيث هي الأشياء بل من حيث إنها قابلة للوجود أو لا قابلة. وكانت بحسب المفهوم الأوَّل إما ممكنة وإمَّا ممتنعة فيكون غير الممكن بحسب هذا المفهوم بمعنى غير ما ليس بضروري فيكون الواجب ليس بممكن بهذا المعنى إذن. ثم الإمكان الأوَّل يسمى الإمكان العام أو العامي منسوبًا إلى العامة [ كما هو عند البعض وإلى العموم، والثاني بالخاص أو الخاصي.
وقد قيل في قولهم: أما ما يعني به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم أنَّه مشعر بأنَّ الإمكان عاما أو خاصا لا يكون عبارة عن سلب الضَّرورة التي مرَّ ذكرها. فإن من المحال أن يكون الشَّيء يلازم نفسه وإنَّه هو سلب الضّرورة لا غير؛ غير أنَّه لا يكون كذلك فإن الإمكان العام لا يكون عبارة عن سلب ضرورة العدم من حيث هو السَّلب بل هو عبارة عمَّا يلازم هذا السلب وغيره أعني الوجوب والإمكان الخاص ولا يقال لو كان كما ذكرتم لكان الواجب متركبًا منه ومن...
He says explicitly: [Know that in the expression of the Shaykh here there is a kind of problem; because he said: 'Possibility is either intended by it what accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence,' he has specified this possibility to what accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence, but it is not so; for this possibility also occurs in the negation of the necessity of existence. Do you not see that the possibility of non-existence in this sense is predicated of the impossible and the specific possible? Because your saying 'it can not be' in this sense is predicated of the impossible and the specific possible. So possibility is more general than possibility in existence or possibility in non-existence. And what necessitates the negation of the necessity of non-existence is the possibility of existence, not absolute possibility. So it is as if he interpreted the general by the specific. So it is necessary to say: possibility is what accompanies the negation of necessity in one of the two sides of existence and non-existence. If it is in the side of existence, it is what accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence; and if it is in the side of non-existence, it is what accompanies the negation of the necessity of existence. He said: 'And either it is intended by it what accompanies the negation of necessity in non-existence and existence to the end.' I say: You have known that the general possible is that to which applies the absence of necessity in one of the two sides of existence or non-existence, and the specific possible is that to which applies the absence of necessity on both its sides together. Then the general possible applies to the two sides of the specific possible. For that reason they transferred the name 'possible' to it from the first meaning. And the necessary is outside this possible, and the division according to it is three: either necessary, possible, or impossible. And the wording of the book here also indicates that the specific possibility is not itself the negation of the two necessities, but rather something that accompanies that negation. And we have explained in our great book that it is impossible for it to be a positive thing.
And these very issues have been investigated in the Great Logic (folio 19 verso): For it is either intended by it what accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence — and that is impossibility according to what it is posited for in the first positing. And there, everything that is not possible is impossible, and this possibility is among what is predicated of the necessary. Or it is intended by it what accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence and existence together, according to what it is posited for by way of transference, such that a thing is predicated of it the first possibility in both the negative and affirmative sides. So it is said: 'possible that it is' and 'possible that it is not,' i.e., not impossible that it is and not impossible that it is not. And since possibility in the first sense may apply in both sides, agreeing and differing, the specific one distinguished it with the name 'possibility' and the necessary came out from between, so things according to it become either possible, impossible, or necessary — not insofar as they are things, but insofar as they are receptive of existence or not receptive. And they were according to the first concept either possible or impossible, so what is not possible according to this concept means something other than what is not necessary. Therefore the necessary is not possible in this sense. Then the first possibility is called general possibility or common possibility, attributed to the common people [as some hold] or to generality, and the second is called specific or special.
And it has been said in their statement: 'As for what is meant by it what accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence' — that it indicates that possibility, general or specific, is not an expression for the negation of necessity that was mentioned. For it is impossible for a thing to accompany itself, and it is the negation of necessity itself, nothing else; however it is not so, for general possibility is not an expression for the negation of the necessity of non-existence insofar as it is negation, but rather it is an expression for what accompanies this negation and other things, namely necessity and specific possibility. And it is not said: if it were as you mentioned, the necessary would be composed of it and of...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and clarificatory commentary on the concept of 'imkan' (possibility) in the context of Avicenna's logic, as interpreted by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The author begins by identifying an ambiguity in Ibn Sina's phrasing: when he says 'possibility is what accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence,' he seems to define general possibility too narrowly, because the same phrase can also apply to the negation of the necessity of existence. For example, the statement 'it can not be' (imkan al-'adam) in this sense applies both to the impossible (which necessarily does not exist) and to the contingent (which may or may not exist). Thus, general possibility is broader than what he specified. The author then offers a corrected definition: possibility is what accompanies the negation of necessity in one of the two sides (existence or non-existence). If it is on the side of existence, it is the possibility of existence (negation of non-existence necessity); if on the side of non-existence, it is the possibility of non-existence (negation of existence necessity).

Next, the author distinguishes between general possible (al-mumkin al-'amm) and specific possible (al-mumkin al-khass). The general possible is what lacks necessity in one side; the specific possible lacks necessity in both sides simultaneously. Therefore, the general possible is wider in scope and includes the specific possible as a subset. The term 'possible' in the first sense was then transferred to the specific sense, and the necessary (wajib) is excluded from this specific possibility. Thus, under the specific meaning, things divide into three: necessary, possible, and impossible. The author also notes that the wording of the text ('al-kitab') suggests that specific possibility is not identical to the mere negation of the two necessities, but rather something that accompanies that negation — a point he argues in his larger work.

The commentary then reproduces an investigation from the 'Great Logic' (al-Mantiq al-Kabir) that further clarifies the two meanings. In the first sense, possibility accompanies the negation of the necessity of non-existence only; even the necessary (wajib) is possible in this sense because it is not impossible. In the second sense, possibility accompanies the negation of both necessities of non-existence and existence together, i.e., something can be said to be possible in the sense that it may exist or not exist. This specific possibility does not apply to the necessary being, because the necessary cannot not exist. Thus, according to this second sense, things are divided into possible, impossible, and necessary. The first type is called 'general possibility' (al-imkan al-'amm) and the second 'specific possibility' (al-imkan al-khass).

Finally, the author addresses a subtle point: when one says 'possibility is what accompanies the negation of necessity,' the phrase 'accompanies' indicates that possibility is not itself the negation, but something that implies it. This is because it would be impossible for a thing to accompany itself. The negation of necessity is one thing, and possibility is a state that necessarily goes with that negation. The author cautions against thinking that possibility is synonymous with the negation. This clarifies the logical relationship between possibility and necessity, a key distinction in modal logic.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
سلب ضرورة العدم
الواجب
الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct relationship between general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm) and specific possibility (al-imkan al-khass)?
A) General possibility is narrower and applies only to the necessary.
B) Specific possibility is a subset of general possibility.
C) They are synonymous terms.
D) General possibility does not apply to the impossible.

Key Takeaway
General possibility is broader and includes the necessary and impossible; specific possibility is narrower and applies only to contingent beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement: 'Everything that is not necessary is possible.' Which sense of 'possible' is being used? Explain using the criteria from the passage.
?
Test the definition: Is the necessary being (wajib) 'possible' in the general sense? In the specific sense? Provide the logical justification from the passage.

Critique of Defining Tasawwur as Perception Without Judgment
Author refutes the definition of tasawwur as perception of quiddity without judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradiction in defining tasawwur as perception without judgment and explain the distinction between absence of judgment and judgment of absence.

Core Idea
The definition of tasawwur as 'perception without judgment' is itself a judgment — a contradiction in terms.

In this passage, the author critically examines a common definition of conception (taṣawwur) as 'the perception of a quiddity without any judgment.' He argues that this definition is self-contradictory because the phrase 'without judgment' is itself a judgment. Through this critique, we will see how the act of conceptualizing inherently involves an implicit judgment, and we will learn the crucial distinction between the absence of judgment and the judgment of absence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique
Locate the definition of tasawwur as 'perception of the quiddity from the aspect that it is the quiddity' and note the claim that it is without judgment.
The author quotes this definition and immediately challenges it.
2
Find the contradiction claimed
The author says that perceiving a quiddity inherently involves a judgment that the quiddity is perceived. So defining it as 'without judgment' is contradictory.
The phrase 'without judgment' is itself a judgment about the quiddity.
3
Understand the subtle distinction
Distinguish between 'absence of judgment' (no mental act of affirming/denying) and 'judgment of absence' (asserting that there is no judgment). The definition mistakenly uses the latter.
The intended meaning is the absence of further judgment beyond mere apprehension.
4
Recognize the correct priority
The author affirms that perception of the quiddity qua quiddity is logically prior to any additional attribution (like existence or non-existence).
This preserves the possibility of a simple conception that is not yet a full propositional judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال في تعريف التصور»: إنه لا يكون عبارة عن إدراك الماهية من حيث هي الماهية، فإن إدراك الماهية لا يكون إلا وأن تكون الماهية مدركة. وهذا هو الحكم على الماهية فلا يكون إدراكها من حيث هي هي، بل يكون مع الحكم عليها، وإنه مما تحقق التناقض في الكلام. وعلى هذا في قولهم: إنه عبارة عن إدراك الماهية من غير أن يحكم عليها». فإن قولهم: «من غير أن يحكم عليها هو الحكم عليها وفيه من التناقض أيضًا غير أنه ضعيف جدا. فإن الفرق بين بين عدم الحكم وبين الحكم بالعدم كما مر من قبل؛ وما أشرتم إليه فذلك عدم الحكم لا الحكم بالعدم. ولا يستراب في أن إدراك الماهية من حيث هي الماهية متقدم في رتبة العقل على إسناد أمر آخر إليها.
And it may be said regarding the definition of conception: it is not that it is perception of the quiddity from the aspect that it is the quiddity, for perception of the quiddity only occurs when the quiddity is perceived. This is a judgment upon the quiddity, so its perception is not from the aspect that it is it, but rather with judgment upon it, and that is a verified contradiction in speech. And in accordance with this, their saying: 'It is perception of the quiddity without judging it' — for their saying 'without judging it' is itself a judgment upon it, and in it there is contradiction, though it is very weak. Indeed the difference between the absence of judgment and the judgment of absence is as was previously; and what you have indicated is the absence of judgment, not the judgment of absence. And there is no doubt that perception of the quiddity from the aspect that it is the quiddity is prior in the order of the intellect to the attribution of another thing to it.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage critically examines a common definition of conception (taṣawwur) as 'the perception of a quiddity (māhiyya) from the aspect that it is that quiddity' without any judgment. The author argues that this definition is contradictory. The act of perceiving a quiddity inevitably involves a judgment that the quiddity is being perceived — that is, the mind asserts the existence of the quiddity in perception. Hence, saying 'perception without judgment' is self-contradictory: the phrase 'without judgment' is itself a judgment (that there is no judgment). The author then clarifies the distinction between 'absence of judgment' (ʿadam al-ḥukm) and 'judgment of absence' (al-ḥukm bi al-ʿadam) — a subtle but crucial point. The intended meaning is the absence of a further affirmative or negative judgment beyond the mere apprehension. The author concludes that the perception of the quiddity qua quiddity is logically prior to any additional attribution (like existence or non-existence). An example: In conceiving 'human', the mind grasps the quiddity of humanity; this grasp is a kind of judgment (that 'human' is present in the mind), but it is not a judgment about external existence. The definition's flaw is treating tasawwur as free from any judgment, while even the minimal cognitive grasp is implicitly a judgment.

Key Terms
التصور
إدراك الماهية
الحكم
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main criticism of defining tasawwur as 'perception of quiddity without judgment'?
A) The phrase 'without judgment' is ambiguous.
B) The phrase 'without judgment' is itself a judgment, causing a contradiction.
C) Tasawwur cannot be defined at all.
D) Perception cannot occur without prior judgment.

Key Takeaway
The act of conceiving a quiddity implicitly involves a judgment that the quiddity is perceived, so defining tasawwur as 'perception without judgment' is contradictory; the intended meaning is the absence of further judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the definition of 'tasawwur' as 'mere apprehension' (al-idrak al-mujarrad) avoids the contradiction discussed. Explain your reasoning.
?
Using the distinction between absence of judgment and judgment of absence, explain why the definition of 'tasdiq' as 'judgment' is not subject to the same critique.

Critique of Defining Tasawwur as Form in the Soul
Author rejects definition of tasawwur as form in the soul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a definition by example and explain why it fails the condition of universality (muṭṭarid).

Core Idea
A definition must be universal; an example does not define.

In this passage, the author critiques a common definition of taṣawwur: 'the acquisition of the form of a thing in the soul.' He dismisses it as a definition by example, which is not truly universal. Understanding why this definition fails will sharpen your ability to evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target definition
Locate the phrase defining taṣawwur: 'acquisition of the form of a thing in the soul.' This is the definition under critique.
2
Determine the author's objection
The author says this is a definition by example (taʿrīf bi al-mithāl). Why? Because it refers specifically to the mental form, not to all instances of conception.
3
Analyze the condition of universality (muṭṭarid)
A proper definition must be consistent (muṭṭarid), covering every instance of the concept. This definition fails because it only applies to cases where a form exists in the mind. For example, conceiving a nonexistent entity like a phoenix does not involve an existing form.
The marginal note clarifies that this phrase is actually an explanation (bayān), not a definition.
4
Consider the marginal note's clarification
The marginal note says the author earlier argued that knowledge (ʿilm) is self-evident and needs no definition. Thus, this phrase is not a real definition but an explanation of the technical term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول من قال: «إنه عبارة عن حصول صورة الشيء في النفس فذلك هو التعريف بالمثال [٤] لما أنه بالنسبة إلى الصورة الذهنية (۱) / ولا خفاء في أنه لا يكون مطردًا. (۱) وفي هامش النسخة وقوله التصور هو حصول صورة الشيء في الذهن ليس تعريفا لما مرَّ في العلم بأنه غني عن التعريف، بل بيان مفهوم التصور الاصطلاحي عندهم.
As for the saying of whoever said: 'It is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the soul' — that is a definition by example, because it is in relation to the mental form. There is no concealment of the fact that it is not universal. (Marginal note: His saying that tasawwur is the acquisition of the form of a thing in the mind is not a definition, because of what preceded regarding knowledge being independent of definition, but rather an explanation of the technical concept of tasawwur according to them.)
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage critiques another attempted definition of conception (taṣawwur): 'the acquisition of the form of a thing in the soul.' The author dismisses this as a definition by example (taʿrīf bi al-mithāl), not a true logical definition. It is not universal (muṭṭarid) because it applies only to mental forms, not to all instances of conception. For example, conceiving a nonexistent thing like a phoenix does not involve an existing form in the soul in the same sense. The marginal note further clarifies that this phrase is not actually a definition, since the author earlier argued that knowledge is self-evident and needs no definition; rather, it is an explanation (bayān) of the technical notion of tasawwur as used by scholars. In usul al-fiqh, the form of a ruling in the mind is the conception, but the definition fails to cover all cases.

التعريف بالمثال
مطرد

Checkpoint

A) It is too vague.
B) It is a definition by example and not universal.
C) The soul cannot acquire forms.
D) It contradicts the definition of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
A proper definition must be universal, covering all instances; a definition by example fails this condition.

Simple Conception vs. Conception with Assent
Author distinguishes simple conception from conception with assent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple conception from conception with assent and identify examples of each in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Simple conception is bare meaning; assent adds judgment.

In this passage, the author clarifies a foundational distinction in logic: a bare concept (tasawwur saadhaj) versus a concept accompanied by a judgment (tasdiq). Without this distinction, you might think you know something when you only have the name, not the reality. This distinction is crucial for understanding how logic builds from definitions to proofs.

Guided Path
1
Find the key terms
Locate the Arabic terms for 'simple conception' and 'conception with assent' in the passage. Notice how the author uses 'tasawwur saadhaj' and 'tasawwur ma'ahu tasdiq'.
2
Analyze the triangle example
The author gives two examples involving 'triangle'. The first is simple conception: knowing the meaning of the name 'triangle' as a shape with three sides. The second involves assent: knowing that every triangle has angles equal to two right angles. Identify which is which.
3
Identify the role of assent
Note that simple conception is 'bare' ('ari) of any assent. It is merely understanding the meaning of a term. The author says such a conception is not according to reality (bi-hasabi al-haqiqa) because true conception of a thing includes its existence in the mind, which is a judgment.
4
Connect to ignorance
The passage ends by mentioning ignorance in terms of conception: one may not be able to conceive a meaning until they learn, like ambiguous terms. This shows the necessity of definitions in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الفرق بين العلم والمعرفة ومكانة المنطق منهما ] ثم الشيء قد يعلم تصورًا ساذجا مثل علمنا بمعنى اسم المثلث، فهو شكل يحيط به ثلاثة أضلع؛ إذ هو تصور ساذج ؛ والتصور الساذج هو التصور العاري عن التصديق. ومثل هذا التصور لا يكون تصور الشيء بحسب الحقيقة، مثل علمنا بمعنى المثلث؛ إذ ذلك لا يتعرى عن التصديق وهو كونه موجودا في الذهن بخلاف علمنا بمعنى اسم المثلث. وقد يعلم تصورا معه تصديق مثل علمنا إن كل مثلث فإن زواياه مساوية لقائمتين. فكذلك وقد يجهل من جهة التصوُّر فلا يتصور معناه إلى أن يتعرف مثل ذي الاسمين والمنفصل وقد
Section: On the difference between knowledge and cognition and the status of logic among them. Then a thing may be known by a simple conception, like our knowledge of the meaning of the name 'triangle': it is a shape enclosed by three sides; for it is a simple conception. And simple conception is conception stripped of assent. And such a conception is not a conception of the thing according to reality, like our knowledge of the meaning of 'triangle'; for that is not stripped of assent, namely its existence in the mind, unlike our knowledge of the meaning of the name 'triangle'. And it may be known by a conception with assent, like our knowledge that every triangle has its angles equal to two right angles. Likewise, and one may be ignorant with respect to conception, so he cannot conceive its meaning until he learns, like [the case of] the one with two names and the discrete...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section (faṣl) that distinguishes simple conception (al-taṣawwur al-sādhaj) from conception accompanied by assent (taṣawwur maʿahu taṣdīq), and relates this to the terms 'ilm (knowledge) and maʿrifa (cognition). The author gives examples from geometry, but the lesson is general for logic. Simple conception is bare apprehension of a meaning without any judgment, such as knowing the meaning of the name 'triangle' as 'a shape enclosed by three sides'. This is a conception without affirming or denying anything about it. In contrast, conception according to reality (bi-ḥasabi al-ḥaqīqa) involves assent: for example, knowing the meaning of 'triangle' as a mental existent (that it exists in the mind) is not stripped of assent because it involves a judgment of existence. The passage then gives an example of conception with assent: the knowledge that every triangle has angles equal to two right angles, which is a proposition (taṣdīq). The author also mentions ignorance in terms of conception: not being able to conceive a meaning until one comes to know it, like the case of ambiguous terms (dhū al-ismayn) or discrete entities (al-munfaṣil). This section sets the stage for the place of logic, which deals with these types of knowledge and the tools to move from conception to assent correctly.

Key Terms
التصور الساذج
التصديق
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Knowledge that fire is hot

Key Takeaway
Simple conception is the first step in knowledge, but full knowledge (ilm) often requires assent (tasdiq).

Explanatory Statements (Qawl Sharh) — The Path to Conception
Author defines qawl sharh and lists its subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of qawl sharh and list its subtypes as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Qawl sharh is the logical tool for conception — the first aim of logic.

In this passage, the author introduces the two fundamental products of logic: qawl sharh and hujja. The first, qawl sharh, is the explanatory statement that leads to tasawwur (conception). Understanding its structure and subtypes is essential for grasping how definitions work in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'qawl sharh'. The passage tells us it is composed from specific known things in a specific form and leads to conception.
The definiendum is 'qawl sharh'.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'ta'lif' (composition) from specific known things in a specific form. The differentia is 'leading to conception' (tasawwur).
This is a hadd (definition) of qawl sharh.
3
List the subtypes
The passage mentions four subtypes: hadd (definition), rasm (description), mithal (example), and 'alama (sign). Note that the list is open-ended with 'mithala'.
These are all types of qawl sharh.
4
Understand the error possibilities
Errors can occur in the information used, the composition, or both. The goal of logic is to distinguish correct from incorrect qawl sharh.
This motivates the need for logical scrutiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد جرت العادة بأن يسمى المؤلف من معلومات خاصة على هيئة خاصة موصلة إلى التصور «قولا شارحًا؛ فمنه حد ومنه رسم، ومنه مثال ومنه علامة مثلا، وأن يسمي المؤلف من معلومات خاصة على هيئة خاصة موصلة إلى التصديق «حجة»؛ فمنه قياس ومنه استقراء ومنه تمثيل، ومنه ما يعرف من بعد على مثال هذه الأمور. وكثيرًا ما يكون الغلط فيه شبيها بالصواب في كل فرد من الأفراد المذكورة من القول الشارح والحجة تارة من جهة المعلومات التي منها يتألف كل واحد منهما، وتارة من جهة التأليف، وأخرى من جهتهما فقصارى علم المنطق أن يكون عند الذهن معرفة هذين الطريقين أن القول الشارح مثلا كيف يجب حتى يكون معرفًا ماهية الشيء، وكيف يكون حتى دالا عليه، وكيف يكون حتى لا يكون معرفًا ولا دالا بل يكون فاسدا يظن أنه على وجه الصحة، وأن الحجة كيف تجب حتى تكون موصلة إلى التصديق
And the custom is that the composition from specific known things in a specific form leading to conception is called an 'explanatory statement' (qawl sharh); among it are definition (hadd), description (rasm), example (mithal), and sign ('alama) for example. And the composition from specific known things in a specific form leading to assent is called a 'proof' (hujja); among it are syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil), and what will be known later on the model of these things. And often error in it resembles correctness in each of the mentioned individuals from the explanatory statement and the proof, sometimes from the aspect of the information from which each of them is composed, sometimes from the aspect of composition, and other times from both aspects. So the utmost of the science of logic is that the mind should have knowledge of these two paths: that the explanatory statement, for example, how it must be to be a definer of the essence of a thing, and how it must be to be indicative of it, and how it must be to be neither a definer nor indicative but rather corrupt that is thought to be correct; and that the proof, how it must be to be leading to assent.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines the two principal products of logic: the 'explanatory statement' (qawl sharh) which leads to tasawwur (conception), and the 'proof' (hujja) which leads to tasdiq (assent). The author explains that qawl sharh is composed of specific known things in a specific form, and its subtypes include hadd (definition), rasm (description), mithal (example), and 'alama (sign). The hujja also is composed of specific known things in a specific form, with subtypes including qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). The author then notes that errors can occur in these, resembling correct forms, due to problems in the information used, the composition, or both. Therefore the goal of logic is to enable the mind to recognize how each of these must be to be correct (e.g., a true definition, a valid proof) and to distinguish them from invalid ones that appear correct.

Key Takeaway
Qawl sharh is a composition from known information in a specific form leading to conception; it includes hadd, rasm, mithal, and 'alama.

Proofs (Hujja) — The Path to Assent
Author defines hujja and lists its subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of hujja and list its subtypes as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Hujja is the logical tool for assent (tasdiq) — the second aim of logic.

In the same passage, the author defines the second product of logic: hujja (proof). Like qawl sharh, it is composed from specific known information in a specific form, but its purpose is to lead to tasdiq (assent). Understanding hujja is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum

The definiendum is 'hujja'.

Extract the genus and differentia

List the subtypes
The passage mentions three explicit subtypes: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). It also says 'and what will be known later' indicating more types.
These are all types of hujja.
4
Understand the error possibilities
Similar to qawl sharh, errors can arise from the information, composition, or both. The goal is to distinguish valid proofs from invalid ones.
This motivates the study of logic.

The Two Pillars of Discourse: Explanatory Statements and Arguments
Author classifies the two types of discourse and their subcategories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explanatory statements and arguments in Islamic logic and identify their subcategories and threefold statuses.

Core Idea
Logic safeguards the mind by teaching how to construct correct definitions and arguments, and to spot corrupt ones.

In this passage, the author lays out the fundamental division of discourse in logic. He defines two types: the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) which leads to conceptualization, and the argument (hujja) which leads to assent. He also notes that error can creep in from the information, the composition, or both. The ultimate aim of logic is to equip the mind to distinguish the sound from the corrupt in both types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Read the first sentence. The author defines two categories: qawl sharh (explanatory statement) and hujja (argument). Note their purposes: tasawwur (conceptualization) vs tasdiq (assent).
2
List the subcategories
Under qawl sharh, the author includes hadd (definition), rasm (description), mithal (example), and alama (sign). Under hujja, he includes qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy), plus others to be introduced later.
3
Locate the three sources of error
The author says error can be from the information (al-malumat), from the composition (al-talif), or from both. This applies to each category.
4
Understand the three statuses for each
For explanatory statements, they can be truly defining, merely indicative, or corrupt yet deceptively sound. For arguments, they can lead to certain assent, probable assent, or be corrupt (fallacious).
5
Recognize the aim of logic
The passage concludes that the purpose of the science of logic is for the mind to know these two paths—how to produce correct versions and how to avoid corrupt ones that resemble truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد جرت العادة بأن يسمى المؤلف من معلومات خاصة على هيئة خاصة موصلة إلى التصور «قولا شارحًا؛ فمنه حد ومنه رسم، ومنه مثال ومنه علامة مثلا، وأن يسمي المؤلف من معلومات خاصة على هيئة خاصة موصلة إلى التصديق «حجة»؛ فمنه قياس ومنه استقراء ومنه تمثيل، ومنه ما يعرف من بعد على مثال هذه الأمور. وكثيرًا ما يكون الغلط فيه شبيها بالصواب في كل فرد من الأفراد المذكورة من القول الشارح والحجة تارة من جهة المعلومات التي منها يتألف كل واحد منهما، وتارة من جهة التأليف، وأخرى من جهتهما فقصارى علم المنطق أن يكون عند الذهن معرفة هذين الطريقين أن القول الشارح مثلا كيف يجب حتى يكون معرفًا ماهية الشيء، وكيف يكون حتى يكون دالا عليه، وكيف يكون حتى لا يكون معرفًا ولا دالا بل يكون فاسدا يظن أنه على وجه الصحة، وأن الحجة كيف تجب حتى تكون موصلة إلى التصديق القطعي، وكيف تكون حتى تكون موصلة إلى التصديق الظني، وكيف تكون حتى تكون من الحجج الفاسدة.
And customarily, a composition of specific information in a specific form leading to conceptualization is called an 'explanatory statement'; among it are definition, description, example, and sign, for instance. And a composition of specific information in a specific form leading to assent is called an 'argument'; among it are syllogism, induction, analogy, and what will be known later on the pattern of these matters. And often error in it resembles truth in every one of the mentioned individuals of the explanatory statement and the argument, sometimes from the perspective of the information from which each is composed, sometimes from the perspective of composition, and other times from both. So the aim of the science of logic is that the mind has knowledge of these two paths: how the explanatory statement, for example, must be in order to define the essence of a thing, how it must be in order to be indicative of it, and how it must be so that it neither defines nor indicates but is corrupt while thought to be sound; and how the argument must be in order to lead to certain assent, how it must be in order to lead to probable assent, and how it must be in order to be among corrupt arguments.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definitional enumeration of the two main types of discourse in logic: the explanatory statement (qawl sharh) and the argument (hujja). The author states that by convention, a composition of specific information in a specific form that leads to conceptualization (tasawwur) is called an explanatory statement. Its subtypes include definition (hadd), description (rasm), example (mithal), and sign (alama). Conversely, a composition leading to assent (tasdiq) is called an argument, with subtypes: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), analogy (tamthil), and others. The author then notes that error can occur in each type—either from the constituent information, the composition, or both. Accordingly, the purpose of logic is to learn how to produce correct explanatory statements and arguments, distinguishing them from their corrupt counterparts. The passage highlights three statuses for each: for the explanatory statement, it can be truly defining, merely indicative, or corrupt yet deceptively sound; for the argument, it can lead to certainty, probability, or be fallacious. This establishes the scope of logic as safeguarding the mind from error in these essential cognitive tools.

Key Terms
قول شارح
حجة
حد
رسم
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To memorize the types of explanatory statements.

C) To define all things.
D) To produce certain assent only.

Key Takeaway
Logic teaches the mind to distinguish correct definitions and arguments from those that are corrupt but resemble truth.

Logic as a Legalistic Tool for Thought
Author explains human nature's insufficiency and defines logic as a legalistic tool to safeguard thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why unaided human reasoning is insufficient and define logic as a legalistic tool that protects thought from error.

Core Idea
Logic is the legalistic tool that safeguards the mind from error, akin to grammar for speech.

This passage establishes the foundational need for logic. Human nature, despite its innate ability to discern good from bad, is not sufficient to guarantee correct reasoning. The author argues that even skilled practitioners can err, necessitating a normative tool—logic—that, when observed, protects the mind. Understanding this purpose is essential for appreciating why logic is a required science in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the insufficiency of human nature
The author states that human nature with its levels is not sufficient to distinguish sound from corrupt reasoning. This includes even experts, as the craft itself does not guarantee correctness.
2
Recognize the need for a legalistic tool
Because of this insufficiency, a tool (آلة) that provides rules (قانونية) is necessary to prevent error in thought.
3
Understand logic as that tool
The author explicitly names logic as this legalistic tool, whose observance protects the user from straying in thought.
4
Grasp the analogy with grammar
Logic is to the rational faculty as grammar is to external speech—both are sets of rules that correct and regulate their respective domains.
5
Examine the definitions given
The author gives two complementary definitions: logic as a legalistic tool (purpose-based) and logic as the science of transitions from known to unknown (process-based).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فإن منها من المراتب والفطرة البشرية وهي القوة التي بها يقع التمييز بين الأمور الحسنة والقبيحة غير وافية في التفرقة بين الصحيح منها والفاسد وغير ذلك نحو القريب من هذا ومن ذلك كما هو حقها. والإنسان الواحد قد يناقض قوله وقتا بعد وقت؛ اللهم إلا أن يكون ذكيا كاملا في الصناعة وغاية في الفكر والتأمل؛ إذ الصناعة من حيث هي الصناعة غير كافية من كل وجه حتى لا يقع صاحبها في الغلط أصلا. وقد كان من الجائز أن يذهب عنها لأن الصناعة في نفسها غير ضابطة لكنه قد تعرّض هنالك من الانهماك والعجز عن الاشتغال كما ينبغي. ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللوازم أن يطلع على آلة قانونية - أي أصلية - تعصمه مراعاتها من أن يضل في فكره وهي «المنطق». وهذه الآلة من جملة ما لو أمكن استعمالها على وجه الصحة كان المستعمل في العصمة عن الغلط في التركيبات الموصلة إلى المطالب. ثم للإنسان في معتقداته أمور مهمة وأمور تليها في الاهتمام كذلك؛ فصاحب هذه الآلة التي مر ذكرها يتأتى له أن يجتهد في تأكيد الأمر وتمهيد الطرق إلى تلك المهمات بمرافعات صناعية ليزول عنها الشبهة. ونسبة هذه الصناعة - وهي المنطق- إلى الروية الناطقة كنسبة صناعة النحو إلى العبارة الظاهرة. وتعريفها بالآلة القانونية كما هو تعريف متضمن لبيان الغرض منه؛ وهذا ظاهر. ومنهم من عرف علم المنطق وقال: «إنه علم يتعلم منه ضروب الانتقالات من الأمور الحاضرة في الذهن إلى أمور غير حاضرة».
And on this, [human nature] with its levels and innate disposition—which is the power by which discrimination between good and bad things occurs—is not sufficient to distinguish between the sound and corrupt [among statements] and other such things, as is its due. And one individual may contradict his own statement time after time; that is, unless he is extremely intelligent, perfect in the craft, and utmost in thought and reflection. For the craft as such is not sufficient in every aspect to prevent its practitioner from falling into error at all. And it was possible that one might abandon it because the craft itself is not a guarantor, but he may be exposed therein to engrossment and inability to engage as he should. And if it were like that, then it would be necessary to look into a legalistic tool—i.e. an original one—whose observance protects him from straying in his thought, and that is 'logic'. And this tool, if it could be used correctly, would make the user immune from error in compositions that lead to desired ends. Then, for man, in his beliefs there are important matters and matters that follow in importance likewise; so the possessor of this aforementioned tool can strive to strengthen the matter and pave the ways to those important matters through technical appeals to remove doubt. And the relationship of this craft—logic—to the speaking faculty is like the relationship of the craft of grammar to external speech. And its definition as the 'legalistic tool' is a definition that includes an explanation of its purpose; and this is clear. And some have defined the science of logic, saying: 'It is a science by which one learns the various transitions from matters present in the mind to matters not present.'
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition on the necessity of logic as a normative tool. The author begins by stating that human nature, with its innate capacity to distinguish good from bad, is insufficient for reliably distinguishing correct from incorrect reasoning. Humans can contradict themselves over time. Even a skilled practitioner, unless exceptionally intelligent and reflective, can err because the craft itself does not guarantee correctness. Therefore, there is a need for a 'legalistic tool' (ala qanuniyya) that, when followed, protects the user from error in reasoning. This tool is logic. The author then highlights its utility: it helps in strengthening important beliefs and removing doubt through technical arguments. The analogy is drawn: logic is to the rational faculty (al-ru'ya al-natiqa) as grammar is to external speech. The purpose of logic is thus to regulate the internal mental discourse. Finally, a definition is given: logic is the science that teaches the various transitions from present to absent mental matters. This definition aligns with the earlier description of thought as a transition from known to unknown.

روية ناطقة

Key Takeaway
The author establishes logic as an indispensable legalistic tool that corrects the natural shortcomings of human reasoning, defined both by its purpose (error prevention) and its function (transitioning from known to unknown).

The Complete Rasm Definition of Logic and Defense of Its Wording
Definition of logic as a science for learning transitions and defense of its wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and properties in the complete rasm definition of logic and argue for the appropriateness of the phrase 'yuta'allamu' using the 'ilm/ma'rifa distinction.

Core Idea
The definition of logic uses 'science' as genus and 'transitions' as properties; the term 'learning' reflects its universal nature.

In this lesson, we examine how Islamic logicians defined their subject. The passage presents a definition of logic as a 'science from which are learned the types of transitions' and then defends the choice of the word 'yuta'allamu' (is learned) over alternatives like 'yuta'arrafu' (is known). Understanding this distinction requires Ibn Sina's differentiation between 'ilm (knowledge of universals) and ma'rifa (knowledge of particulars). By the end, you will appreciate how careful terminological choices reflect deep philosophical commitments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the definition
Find what is being defined (logic) and the exact wording of the definition in Arabic: 'علم يتعلم منه ضروب الانتقالات...'.
2
Break down the definition into genus and properties
Recognize that 'science' (علم) is the proximate genus, and 'from which are learned the types of transitions...' are the properties that distinguish logic from other sciences.
This is a complete rasm because genus and unique property are given.
3
Understand the nature of rasm
Note the author's comment that this is a definition by complete rasm. Consider why it is not a hadd (essential definition) – it describes what logic does, not its essence.
The author says it is a rasm according to essence, meaning it captures the essential function.
4
Examine the objection
Look at the hypothetical objection: why use 'yuta'allamu' instead of 'yuta'arrafu', as in medicine? Locate the objection in the Arabic.
5
Apply Ibn Sina's distinction
Use the 'ilm/ma'rifa distinction (from al-Shifa') to explain: logic deals with universals, requiring effort (ta'allum); medicine deals with particulars, which can be known (ma'rifa) through experience.
Ta'arruf implies effort too, but it is more suitable for particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من عرّف علم المنطق وقال: إنه علم يتعلم منه ضروب الانتقالات من الأمور الحاضرة في الذهن إلى أمور غير حاضرة. وإنه هو التعريف بالرسم الكامل؛ إذ العلم جنس قريب والباقي هو من الخواص. وقد يقال في هذا الرسم، إنه رسم بحسب الذات على خلاف ما مر من قبل؛ وهو أنه آلة قانونية إذ ذاك لا يكون بمعزل عن القياس إلى الغير. ثم الضروب هي الجزئيات المتعلقة بالمراد على ما هي مستعملة في سائر العلوم. ولقائل أن يقول لماذا كان يقول إنه علم يتعلم منه ولم يقل إنه علم يتعرف منه، كما قيل في الطب : «علم يتعرَّف منه أحوال بدن الإنسان من جهة كذا وكذا». لكنا نقول: إنما قال: «علم يتعلم منه»، فإن الجزئيات التي يستعمل فيها المنطق كليات بخلاف الجزئيات التي يستعمل فيها الطب. وقد ذكر الشيخ أبو علي ابن سينا في الشفاء: إن المعرفة إدراك الجزئيات، والعلم إدراك الكليات. ولأن المنطق كالطبيعي للإنسان بالنسبة إلى الطب؛ فلا يبعد أن يحصل من المنطق ما يحصل منه إلا بالتكلُّف، ولفظ التعرف التكلُّف فلا يليق بالمنطق.
And among them is one who defined the science of logic and said: 'It is a science from which are learned the types of transitions from matters present in the mind to matters not present.' And that it is the definition by complete rasm; since 'science' is the proximate genus and the rest is from the properties. And it is said in this rasm that it is a rasm according to the essence, contrary to what passed previously; that is, it is a legalistic tool then that is not separate from analogy to another. Then the 'types' are the particulars related to the intended meaning as they are used in the other sciences. And it may be said by an objector: 'Why did he say it is a science from which is learned, and did not say it is a science from which is known, as was said in medicine: "a science from which are known the states of the human body from such-and-such aspect"?' But we say: 'He only said "science from which is learned" because the particulars in which logic is used are universals, unlike the particulars used in medicine. And the Shaykh Abu Ali Ibn Sina has mentioned in al-Shifa': "Verily, ma'rifa is the perception of particulars, and 'ilm is the perception of universals." And because logic is like the natural for man in relation to medicine; so it is not far that what is obtained from logic is obtained only with effort (takalluf), and the word ta'arruf implies effort, so it does not befit logic.'
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage focuses on the definition of logic. It begins by presenting a definition from some scholars: logic is a science from which are learned the types of transitions from present to absent mental contents. The author notes that this is a complete definition by rasm (description), where 'science' is the genus and the rest are distinguishing properties. He then addresses an objection: why use 'learned' (yuta'allamu) instead of 'known' (yuta'arrafu), as done in medicine? The response invokes the distinction made by Ibn Sina between 'ilm (knowledge of universals) and ma'rifa (knowledge of particulars). Since logic deals with universal rules of thought, it requires effort and learning, unlike medicine which deals with particular bodies and may be known through experience. Therefore, the term 'learned' is more appropriate for logic.

معرفة
رسم
آلة قانونية
تعلّم

Checkpoint

A) Because logic is a tool for particulars.
B) Because logic deals with universals and requires effort.
C) Because logic is innate and needs no learning.
D) Because medicine uses 'يتعرف' so logic should follow suit.

Key Takeaway
The definition of logic as a complete rasm highlights its genus (science) and property (learning transitions), and the choice of 'yuta'allamu' underscores that logic is the study of universal rules requiring deliberate effort.

The Foundational Dilemma: Does Logic Need a Legalistic Tool?
Author raises a dilemma challenging logic's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dilemma that logic's necessity faces and identify the disjunctive structure of the argument.

Core Idea
If logic is a tool, its own tool leads to regress or circle—a master dilemma.

The passage presents a powerful objection to the very enterprise of logic. It argues that intellectual reasoning (nazar 'aqli) either needs a formal tool (qanun sina'i) or not. If not, logic is superfluous. If it does, that tool must be either self-evident or theoretical, and either way faces problems. This dilemma is a classic example of a compound argument that challenges the foundation of the discipline. Understanding it is crucial for appreciating later responses by logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main disjunction
Locate the two alternatives in the Arabic: either intellectual reflection requires a legalistic tool or it does not. This is the foundational fork in the argument.
The Arabic phrase 'إما أن يكون مفتقرًا إلى قانون صناعي أو لا يكون' presents the disjunction.
2
Trace the consequences of each branch
If it does not require a tool, then logic is unnecessary and there is no need to study it. If it does require a tool, we must examine the nature of that tool.
3
Analyze the sub-dilemma for the tool
The required tool is either self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). If self-evident, studying it is wasteful. If theoretical, it itself requires another tool.
The text explicitly distinguishes between بديهيات and نظريات.
4
Recognize the regress or circularity
If the tool is theoretical, it needs another tool for its justification, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul) or circular reasoning (dawr). Thus both paths of the tool lead to problems.
The passage concludes 'يُفضي إلى الدور أو التسلسل'.
5
Formalize as a compound argument
The dilemma can be seen as a series of conditional and disjunctive steps: first a disjunction, then conditionals for each branch, then a sub-disjunction. This shows the logical power of the objection.
We will use reasoning_form to capture this structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في المنطق ما هو؟ ] وقد يقال فيما نحن بصدده وهو المنطق إن تعرف المجهولات من المعلومات بالنظر العقلي إما أن يكون مفتقرًا إلى قانون صناعي يقايس به أو لا يكون؛ فإن كان مفتقرًا وإن لم يكن مفتقرًا فلا حاجة إلى التعرض للمنطق فذلك القانون في نفسه لا يخلو من أن يكون من البديهيات؛ وحينئذ يكون التعرض لتحصيله ضايعا، أو لا من البديهيات؛ وحينئذ يكون من النظريات لا محالة، فيكون مفتقرًا إلى قانون آخر وإنه يُفضي إلى الدور أو التسلسل.
Section: [On what logic is?] And it may be said concerning what we are discussing, namely logic, that knowing unknown things from known things by intellectual reflection either requires a legalistic tool by which to measure or not. If it requires such, and if it does not require, then there is no need to engage in logic. That tool itself is either from self-evidents; then engaging in acquiring it is wasteful, or it is not from self-evidents; then it is necessarily from theoretical matters, and thus it requires another tool, and that leads to circularity or infinite regress.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a critical dilemma about the necessity of logic. The author questions whether intellectual reflection (nazar 'aqli) requires a formal legalistic tool (qanun sina'i) to move from known to unknown. If such a tool is not needed, then logic is superfluous. If it is needed, then that tool must be either self-evident (badihi) or theoretical (nazari). If self-evident, studying it is pointless. If theoretical, then it itself requires another tool to be justified, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul) or circular reasoning (dawr). This is a foundational challenge to the discipline, likely posed to then be resolved in subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
نظر عقلي
قانون صناعي
بديهيات
نظريات
دور
تسلسل

Checkpoint

A) It becomes self-evident and needs no study.
B) It requires another tool, leading to infinite regress.
C) It is unnecessary because logic is innate.
D) It leads to the rejection of all knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma uses a compound disjunctive argument to show that studying logic is either unnecessary, wasteful, or leads to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Defining Logic: Why 'Learn' Instead of 'Know'
Author defines logic as 'science from which we learn' and contrasts it with medicine's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is defined as 'a science from which one learns' rather than 'a science by which one knows', using the distinction between 'ilm and ma'rifa and the nature of universal particulars.

Core Idea
Logic's subject matter is universal particulars, requiring learning, not mere recognition.

In this passage, the author defines logic through a complete description (rasm kāmil) and addresses a key objection about the wording 'science from which one learns.' Why not say 'science by which one knows,' as in medicine? The answer reveals a fundamental distinction between 'ilm (science of universals) and ma'rifa (knowledge of particulars). This lesson will show you how to unpack this definition and defend its phrasing using Ibn Sina's framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and genus
Find what is being defined (logic) and the broad category it belongs to. The passage states the genus is 'knowledge' (al-‘ilm). This is the first component of the rasm.
The genus is 'proximate' (qarīb), meaning immediately above the definiendum.
2
Determine the type of definition
The author explicitly says this is a 'complete description' (rasm kāmil). Why isn't it a real definition (hadd)? Because it uses a genus and properties (khavāṣṣ) rather than an essential differentia (fasl).
A hadd would require a fasl that expresses the essence; a rasm uses accidental properties.
3
Locate the objection
Someone objects: why use 'yuta‘allam' (from which one learns) instead of 'yuta‘arraf' (by which one knows), as is done in medicine? This challenges the phrasing of the differentia.
The objector presumes both sciences should use similar wording.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author cites Ibn Sina: 'ilm is perception of universals, ma'rifa is perception of particulars. Medicine deals with particulars; logic deals with universal particulars. The word 'ta‘arruf' implies effort, but logic is natural to man, so 'ta‘allum' (learning) is more appropriate.
The author also notes logic is an instrument (āla) inseparable from syllogism.
5
Summarize the logical point
The definition's phrasing is justified by the subject matter: universal particulars require a learning process (ta‘allum), not just a recognition (ta‘arruf). This also aligns with logic's role as a natural but learned skill.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعريف بالرسم الكامل؛ إذ العلم جنس قريب والباقي هو من الخواص. وقد يقال في هذا الرسم، إنه رسم بحسب الذات على خلاف ما مر من قبل؛ وهو أنه آلة قانونية إذ ذاك لا يكون بمعزل عن القياس إلى الغير. ثم الضروب هي الجزئيات المتعلقة بالمراد على ما هي مستعملة في سائر العلوم. ولقائل أن يقول لماذا كان يقول إنه علم يتعلم منه ولم يقل إنه علم يتعرف منه، كما قيل في الطب : «علم يتعرَّف منه أحوال بدن الإنسان من جهة كذا وكذا». لكنا نقول: إنما قال: «علم يتعلم منه»، فإن الجزئيات التي يستعمل فيها المنطق كليات بخلاف الجزئيات التي يستعمل فيها الطب. وقد ذكر الشيخ [أبو علي ابن سينا ] في الشفاء: إن المعرفة إدراك الجزئيات، والعلم إدراك الكليات» (١). ولأن المنطق كالطبيعي للإنسان بالنسبة إلى الطب؛ فلا يبعد أن يحصل من المنطق ما يحصل منه إلا بالتكلُّف، ولفظ التعرف التكلُّف فلا يليق بالمنطق.
Definition by complete description; because knowledge is the proximate genus and the remainder are properties. And it might be said regarding this description, that it is a description according to the essence contrary to what has passed before; and that is that it is a legal (law-giving) instrument, since then it would not be isolated from syllogism to something else. Then the specific parts (durūb) are the particulars related to what is intended according to how they are used in the other sciences. And one might say: why did he say that it is 'a science from which one learns' and did not say 'a science by which one knows', as was said in medicine: 'a science by which one knows the states of the human body from such and such aspect.' But we say: he only said 'a science from which one learns', because the particulars used in logic are universals, unlike the particulars used in medicine. And the Shaykh [Abu Ali Ibn Sina] mentioned in al-Shifa': 'Knowledge (ma'rifa) is perception of particulars, and science ('ilm) is perception of universals.' And because logic is like a natural thing for man relative to medicine; thus it is not far that what is obtained from logic is obtained from it except with effort, and the word 'knowing' (ta'arruf) implies effort, so it is not befitting for logic.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines logic through a complete description (rasm kāmil). It states that knowledge is the proximate genus, and the remainder are properties. The definition is considered a description according to essence, contrary to earlier claims, and it identifies logic as a legal instrument inseparable from syllogism. The author then addresses a potential objection: why define logic as 'science from which one learns' (yuta'allam) rather than 'science by which one knows' (yuta'arraf), as medicine is defined? The answer is that the particulars used in logic are universals, unlike medicine's particulars. The author cites Ibn Sina's distinction between ma'rifa (perception of particulars) and 'ilm (perception of universals). Since logic deals with universals and requires effort (takalluf), the word 'ta'arruf' (implying effort) is actually suitable, but the author argues that logic is more like a natural faculty for man, so the effort is inherent, and the word 'yuta'allam' (learned) better captures the acquisition of a skill.

Key Terms
العلم
المعرفة
الرسم
الجزئيات

A) 'ilm is perception of particulars; ma'rifa is perception of universals.
B) 'ilm is perception of universals; ma'rifa is perception of particulars.
C) 'ilm is innate; ma'rifa is acquired.
D) 'ilm is certain; ma'rifa is probable.

Key Takeaway
Logic is defined through a complete description because its subject matter is universal particulars, and the word 'yuta'allam' emphasizes the effort required to master this universal instrument, unlike medicine which deals with concrete particulars.

Resolving Verbal Disputes about the Status of Logic
Author resolves verbal disputes on logic's status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify verbal disputes in discussions about logic's status and resolve them by clarifying definitions.

Core Idea
Verbal disputes in logic dissolve when definitions are made explicit.

In this passage, the author tackles two apparent disagreements: whether logic is a science and whether it is the chief or servant of sciences. He argues that these disagreements are not substantive but verbal, arising from different definitions of 'science' and 'chief'. This lesson will teach you how to recognize such verbal disputes and resolve them by clarifying the key terms involved.

Guided Path
1
Locate the apparent disagreements
The passage mentions two disputes: (a) whether logic is a science, and (b) whether it is chief or servant of sciences. Identify the two positions and the scholars associated with each.
2
Analyze the definitions at play
For the first dispute, note that whether logic is a science depends on the definition of 'science': does it require investigating things with external existence, or does it include things with mental existence? For the second, the meaning of 'chief' depends on whether it involves being sought for its own sake or being needed by others without being dependent on them.
3
Resolve by specifying definitions
The author shows that if you adopt one definition, logic is not a science; if another, it is. Similarly for chief/servant. Thus the disagreements are verbal – both sides can be right once the definitions are fixed. The same method applies to many debates in Islamic sciences.
This method is generalizable to many areas of Islamic scholarship where terms are used ambiguously.
4
Apply to other contexts
Think of similar disputes in fiqh or usul al-fiqh, e.g., whether qiyas is a source of law. The apparent disagreement often hinges on how 'source' is defined. Practice identifying the key term and showing how clarifying it resolves the dispute.
This step connects the logical tool to real Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلوب مشعورًا به من كل وجه ولا يكون مشعورًا به من كل وجه أيضًا. ولا كلام فيه بل الكلام فيما يكون مشعورًا به على سبيل الإجمال وذلك من جملة ما يحتمله.
وقد يقال أيضًا، هب أنه يلزم من كل علم علم آخر من غير كسب وهو الفكر على ما عرف. لكن لا يبعد في بعض النفوس أن يتوقف استعدادها لقبول ما يلزم منه غيره على تعلم هذه القوانين المنطقية. وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون المنطق ضايعا.
ثم الاختلاف بأنه من العلوم أو لا اختلاف لفظي؛ إذ البحث عما به وجود في الخارج إن كان معتبراً في العلم، فالمنطق ليس بعلم. وإن لم يكن معتبراً فيه ذلك بل المعتبر هو البحث عن كل ما له وجود سواءً كان في الذهن أو في الخارج فإنه علم.
وقد نقل عن الشيخ أنه قال إنه رئيس العلوم) وعن الفارابي (إنه خادم العلوم)، وهذا بحث لفظي أيضًا. فإن المفهوم من «الرئيس» إذا كان مشروطًا بشرط أن يكون مقصودًا بنفسه؛ والمنطق ليس كذلك؛ إذ هو مقصود لغيره فلا يكون رئيسا . وإن لم يكن مشروطًا بذلك الشرط، بل الرئيس هو الذي يحتاج إليه غيره من غير عكس، والمنطق كذلك فهو رئيس. وعلى هذا كونه «خادما»؛ إذ البحث فيه بحث لفظي أيضًا؛ فنبحث عن مفهومه بأنه ما هو وننظر فيه أنه هل يوجد في نفس المنطق ذلك أم لا يوجد، ولا يبعد أن يكون خادمًا ورئيسًا. وقد قيل: سيد القوم خادمهم»، والسيد هو الرئيس.
What is sought is known in every respect, but also it is not known in every respect. There is no dispute about that, rather the dispute is about what is known in an overall manner, and that is among the things that it admits. It may also be said: Grant that from every knowledge another knowledge follows without acquisition, which is discursive thought (al-fikr) as defined. But it is not improbable for some souls that their preparedness to accept what leads to something else depends on learning these logical laws. On this basis, logic is not wasted. Then the disagreement about whether it is among the sciences or not is a verbal disagreement; because if the investigation of what is its existence in the outside is considered in a science, then logic is not a science. And if that is not considered, rather what is considered is the investigation of everything that has existence, whether in the mind or in the outside, then it is a science. It has been narrated from the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) that he said it is the chief of the sciences, and from al-Farabi that it is the servant of the sciences. This is also a verbal dispute. For the meaning of 'chief' if it is conditioned by being sought for its own sake, logic is not so; because it is sought for the sake of something else, so it is not chief. And if not conditioned by that, rather the chief is that which is needed by others without the reverse, then logic is so, so it is chief. On this basis it is 'servant'; since the discussion about it is also a verbal dispute; so we investigate its concept: what it is, and we examine whether that exists in logic itself or not, and it is not unlikely that it is both servant and chief. It has been said: 'The master of a people is their servant' and the master is the chief.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage addresses two interrelated critical discussions about the nature and status of logic. First, the author responds to an objection: if knowledge leads to other knowledge without any acquisition, then learning logic seems useless. The author counters that some souls may require the logical laws as a preparation to receive that knowledge, so logic is not wasted. Second, the author examines the dispute over whether logic is a science ('ilm). He argues that this dispute is merely verbal: if a science is defined as investigating things with external existence, logic (which deals with mental concepts) is not a science; but if science includes anything with existence, whether mental or external, then logic qualifies as a science. He then turns to the famous characterizations by Ibn Sina (logic as the 'chief of sciences') and al-Farabi (logic as the 'servant of sciences'). Again, the author shows the conflict is verbal: if 'chief' means something sought for its own sake, logic is not chief because it is a tool; but if 'chief' means something on which others depend without the reverse, logic qualifies. Thus logic can be both servant and chief. The passage concludes with a saying: 'The master of a people is their servant', reinforcing that these labels are not contradictory. The key takeaway is that many apparent disagreements in logic arise from differing definitions and are resolved by clarifying terms.

Key Terms

علم
رئيس العلوم
خادم العلوم

A) Because logic is neither a science nor an art.
B) Because it depends on whether science is defined as investigating external existence only or any existence.
C) Because all scholars agree that logic is a science.
D) Because logic has no subject matter.

Key Takeaway
Many disagreements in logic and Islamic sciences are verbal; they can be resolved by making the definitions of key terms explicit.

What Is Mantiq?
