The Purpose and Ultimate Goal of Logic
Author defines the goal and purpose of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the intended purpose (maqsud) and the ultimate goal (ghaya) of logic as defined in the classical Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Logic's ultimate goal is eternal happiness; its immediate purpose is to provide universal rules for correct reasoning.

This passage opens by recalling that logic is the tool for distinguishing sound from corrupt reasoning. It then distinguishes two levels of aim: the immediate purpose (maqsud) of providing universal rules for conceptual and assertive knowledge, and the ultimate goal (ghaya) of attaining knowledge of the means to eternal happiness, which belongs to the divine sciences. Understanding this hierarchy helps you see why the Islamic logicians valued logic not just as a formal system but as a path to spiritual fulfillment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct aims
The passage explicitly mentions 'al-maqsud' (intended purpose) and 'al-ghaya' (ultimate goal). Locate these terms in the Arabic and note how they are defined differently.
Maqsud is about the benefit in this life; ghaya is about the afterlife.
2
Analyze the definition of maqsud
The maqsud is the benefit of logic: it leads to conceptual (tasawwur) and assertive (tasdiq) aims through explication and argument, universally applicable to all things.
This emphasizes logic's generality—it is not limited to any subject.
3
Analyze the definition of ghaya
The ghaya is knowledge of the means to eternal happiness (al-sa'ada al-abadiyya), which comes from the divine sciences. The author mentions that some even say eternal happiness itself is the goal.
This connects logic to theology and eschatology.
4
Distinguish the two levels
Contrast the two: maqsud is an intermediate, worldly benefit (correct reasoning here and now); ghaya is the ultimate, otherworldly goal (eternal happiness). The former serves the latter.
The passage says ghaya is 'al-ghayat al-quswa' (the utmost goal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في غاية المنطق ]
أما المنطق، فقد مرَّ من قبل أنه ما هو أي التمييز بين الصحيح من الأحوال اللازمة، والحجج بين الصحيح والفاسد. وكذلك المقصود منه، وهو وجه الانتفاع به فيما هو الموصل إلى المقاصد التصورية والتصديقية من القول الشارح والحجة على وجه كلي غير مخصوص بالبعض من الأشياء دون البعض. وكذلك الغاية وهي الاطلاع على ما هو من الوسائل إلى السعادة الأبدية وذلك من العلوم الإلهية وما يتعلق بها. وقد قيل: بأنها هي السعادة الأبدية؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا مناقشة فيه؛ إذ هي الغاية القصوى بالنسبة إلى ذلك.
وأما موضوعه فإنه عبارة عن المعقولات الثانية؛ لما أن موضوع العلم أي علم كان عبارة عما يبحث في ذلك العلم عن أحواله العارضة له لذاته؛ لأن الوسائل متقدّمة. وتسمى تلك الأحوال أعراضا ذاتية»؛ وذلك هو المعقولات الثانية فيما نحن فيه. فإنه إذا تصورنا حقائق الأشياء، كالأرض والسماء والماء والهواء وغير ذلك، فلا يمكننا أن نتصرف في فرد من هذه الأفراد بأنه كذا وكذا. وكيف يمكن ؟! والذهن لا يمكنه أن ينتقل من معنى مفرد إلى تصديق شيء البتة، بل يمكن ذلك عند مقارنة ذلك المعنى وجوده أو عدمه. وحينئذ يضاف إليه معنى آخر فيوجد في الذهن من المفردات ما يمكن أن يحكم بالبعض منها على البعض حكمًا تقييديًا أو خبريًا.
Section [On the Goal of Logic]
As for logic, it has passed before what it is, i.e., the discrimination between the sound and corrupt of the necessary states and arguments. And likewise, its intended purpose (maqsud), which is the benefit of using it as that which leads to the conceptual and assertive aims from the explicatory statement and argument in a universal manner not specific to some things rather than others. And likewise the goal (ghaya), which is the knowledge of what is among the means to eternal happiness, and that is from the divine sciences and what pertains to them. It has been said that it is eternal happiness; and it is among that about which there is no objection, since it is the ultimate goal with respect to that.
As for its subject matter (mawdu'), it is the secondary intelligibles; because the subject matter of any science is that about which it investigates its accidental states that attach to it essentially; because the means are prior. Those states are called 'essential accidents' (a'radh dhatiyya); and that is the secondary intelligibles in our case. For if we conceptualize the realities of things, like earth and sky and water and air and so on, we cannot manipulate any individual of these individuals as being such and such. How could that be?! The mind cannot transition from a single meaning to a judgment of something at all, but that is possible when that meaning is accompanied by its existence or non-existence. Then another meaning is added to it, and among simple concepts that exist in the mind, some can be predicated of others in a restrictive or declarative judgment.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage formally defines the goal and subject matter of logic. It opens with a recap: logic is the instrument that distinguishes correct from incorrect reasoning (in necessary states and arguments). Then it clarifies the intended purpose (maqsud): to provide a universal method for reaching conceptual (tasawwur) and assertive (tasdiq) ends via explicatory statements and arguments. The ultimate goal (ghaya) of logic is knowledge of the means to eternal happiness, which lies in the divine sciences—some even say eternal happiness itself is the goal, and that is undisputed. The core of the passage is the definition of logic's subject matter (mawdu') as the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). This is explained by the general principle that every science investigates the essential accidents of its subject. The secondary intelligibles are those concepts that arise after primary concepts (like earth, sky) and are used to combine and judge them. For example, when we apprehend 'earth', we cannot directly judge 'earth is a substance' without the secondary concept 'substance'. The mind needs an additional meaning (like existence or non-existence) to form a judgment. Thus logic studies these secondary intelligibles—concepts like subject, predicate, genus, species—that enable reasoning and judgment.

Key Terms
الغاية
المقصود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ultimate goal (ghaya) of logic?
A) To learn how to construct any argument.
B) To gain knowledge of the means to eternal happiness.
C) To distinguish between sound and corrupt arguments.
D) To study the secondary intelligibles.

Key Takeaway
Logic serves both an immediate intellectual purpose (universal rules for reasoning) and an ultimate spiritual goal (eternal happiness through divine knowledge).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Subject Matter of Logic: Secondary Intelligibles
Author defines the subject matter of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the subject matter of logic is the secondary intelligibles and identify their role in forming judgments.

Core Idea
Logic studies not things themselves (like earth or sky) but the conceptual tools (secondary intelligibles) we use to reason about things.

In this passage, the author defines the subject matter (mawdu') of logic as the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya) and their essential accidents. He explains that when we think of 'earth' or 'sky', we cannot directly judge them; we need additional concepts like 'substance' or 'existence' to form propositions. These additional concepts are what logic investigates. This lesson will guide you through the reasoning behind this choice of subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of mawdu'
The passage states that the subject matter of any science is what it investigates regarding its essential accidents. For logic, that is the secondary intelligibles.
Essential accidents are properties that necessarily belong to the subject.
2
Distinguish primary from secondary intelligibles
Primary intelligibles are concepts of real things like earth, sky, water. Secondary intelligibles are concepts like genus, species, subject, predicate—used to organize and judge primary concepts.
The passage says: 'if we conceptualize the realities of things... we cannot manipulate any individual... without another meaning.'
3
Understand why logic does not study primary concepts
The mind cannot transition from a single meaning to a judgment (tasdiq) without an additional meaning. Logic explores how these additional meanings (secondary intelligibles) combine to form judgments.
Example: 'Zayd is standing' requires the secondary concept 'is' (existence) and 'standing' (predicate).
4
Apply the principle to an Islamic science
In fiqh, when a jurist defines 'salah', he uses secondary intelligibles like genus (act of worship), differentia (specific conditions), etc. Logic provides the rules for such definitions.
Thus logic's subject is not salah itself but the logical tools used to analyze it.

Source Text

فصل [ في غاية المنطق ]
أما المنطق، فقد مرَّ من قبل أنه ما هو أي التمييز بين الصحيح من الأحوال اللازمة، والحجج بين الصحيح والفاسد. وكذلك المقصود منه، وهو وجه الانتفاع به فيما هو الموصل إلى المقاصد التصورية والتصديقية من القول الشارح والحجة على وجه كلي غير مخصوص بالبعض من الأشياء دون البعض. وكذلك الغاية وهي الاطلاع على ما هو من الوسائل إلى السعادة الأبدية وذلك من العلوم الإلهية وما يتعلق بها. وقد قيل: بأنها هي السعادة الأبدية؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا مناقشة فيه؛ إذ هي الغاية القصوى بالنسبة إلى ذلك.

Section [On the Goal of Logic]
As for logic, it has passed before what it is, i.e., the discrimination between the sound and corrupt of the necessary states and arguments. And likewise, its intended purpose (maqsud), which is the benefit of using it as that which leads to the conceptual and assertive aims from the explicatory statement and argument in a universal manner not specific to some things rather than others. And likewise the goal (ghaya), which is the knowledge of what is among the means to eternal happiness, and that is from the divine sciences and what pertains to them. It has been said that it is eternal happiness; and it is among that about which there is no objection, since it is the ultimate goal with respect to that.

Those states are called 'essential accidents' (a'radh dhatiyya); and that is the secondary intelligibles in our case. For if we conceptualize the realities of things, like earth and sky and water and air and so on, we cannot manipulate any individual of these individuals as being such and such. How could that be?! Then another meaning is added to it, and among simple concepts that exist in the mind, some can be predicated of others in a restrictive or declarative judgment.

Commentary
This passage formally defines the goal and subject matter of logic. It opens with a recap: logic is the instrument that distinguishes correct from incorrect reasoning (in necessary states and arguments). For example, when we apprehend 'earth', we cannot directly judge 'earth is a substance' without the secondary concept 'substance'. The mind needs an additional meaning (like existence or non-existence) to form a judgment. Thus logic studies these secondary intelligibles—concepts like subject, predicate, genus, species—that enable reasoning and judgment.

The Ultimate Aim of Logic: Eternal Happiness
Author defines logic's aim and its ultimate goal of eternal happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three components of logic—its essence, its intended purpose, and its ultimate aim—as defined in this passage.

Core Idea
Logic's ultimate goal is not mere formalism but eternal happiness through divine sciences.

This passage from a classical logic text clarifies that logic is not just a technical tool for argumentation; it has a higher spiritual purpose. The author carefully distinguishes between what logic is (its essence), what it does (its purpose), and why it ultimately matters (its aim). Understanding this three-part framework will help you see how the Islamic tradition integrates rational inquiry with the quest for salvation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three-part structure
The passage describes logic under three headings: its definition (ma huwa), its intended purpose (maqsud), and its ultimate aim (ghaya). Read the Arabic and translate each part to confirm.
Notice the progression from instrument to objective to final goal.
2
Locate the definition of logic
The author says logic is 'the distinction between correct and incorrect among the necessary states and arguments.' This is the essential function of logic.
This definition is recalled from earlier in the text.
3
Extract the intended purpose (maqsud)
The maqsud is the universal benefit of logic: it provides explanatory statements and arguments that lead to conceptual and assent-based knowledge without being restricted to any specific subject.
Logic is a universal instrument, not limited to one science.
4
Recognize the ultimate aim (ghaya)
The ghaya is 'the attainment of that which is among the means to eternal happiness, and that from the divine sciences.' The author states there is no dispute that eternal happiness is the ultimate end.
This connects logic directly to religious and metaphysical knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ في غاية المنطق ]
أما المنطق، فقد مرَّ من قبل أنه ما هو أي التمييز بين الصحيح من الأحوال اللازمة، والحجج بين الصحيح والفاسد. وكذلك المقصود منه، وهو وجه الانتفاع به فيما هو الموصل إلى المقاصد التصورية والتصديقية من القول الشارح والحجة على وجه كلي غير مخصوص بالبعض من الأشياء دون البعض. وكذلك الغاية وهي الاطلاع على ما هو من الوسائل إلى السعادة الأبدية وذلك من العلوم الإلهية وما يتعلق بها. وقد قيل: بأنها هي السعادة الأبدية؛ وإنه من جملة ما لا مناقشة فيه؛ إذ هي الغاية القصوى بالنسبة إلى ذلك.
Section on the aim of logic. As for logic, it has already passed that it is what distinguishes the correct from the incorrect among the necessary states and arguments between the correct and the corrupt. Likewise, its intended purpose is the manner of benefiting from it in what leads to conceptual and assent-based aims from the explanatory statement and the argument in a universal manner not restricted to some things over others. Likewise, the aim is the attainment of that which is among the means to eternal happiness, and that from the divine sciences and what pertains to them. It has been said: that it is eternal happiness; and it is among that about which there is no dispute; for it is the ultimate end relative to that.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the aim (ghaya) of logic. The author begins by recalling the earlier definition of logic as a tool for distinguishing correct reasoning from incorrect. He then states the intended purpose (maqsud) of logic: it is the universal instrument for obtaining conceptual and assent-based knowledge through explanatory statements (al-qawl al-sharih) and arguments (al-hujja), not restricted to any particular subject. Finally, he gives the ultimate goal (al-ghaya): the attainment of eternal happiness (al-sa'ada al-abadiyya), which comes from the divine sciences (al-'ulum al-ilahiyya). He notes that there is no debate that eternal happiness is the ultimate end. This passage establishes that logic is not merely a formal discipline but serves a higher religious purpose, leading to knowledge of God and salvation.

المنطق
الغاية
السعادة الأبدية
العلوم الإلهية

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ultimate goal (ghaya) of logic?
A) Distinguishing correct from incorrect arguments

Logic is not an end in itself; its ultimate purpose is to guide humans to eternal happiness through the divine sciences.

The Subject of Logic: Secondary Intelligibles
Author identifies secondary intelligibles as the subject matter of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of logic as secondary intelligibles and reconstruct the author's argument for why this is the case.

Core Idea
Logic studies not things themselves but the mental attributes that make reasoning possible.

Every science has a subject matter. But what exactly does logic study? The author argues it is not external realities nor their quiddities, but the secondary intelligibles — the attributes that attach to concepts when we use them in reasoning. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle about subject matter
The passage states: the subject matter of any science is that whose essential accidental states are investigated in that science. Understand what 'essential accidents' (al-'arad al-dhati) means here — they are the properties that belong to the subject by virtue of itself.
2
Apply the principle to logic
For logic, the essential accidents are the secondary intelligibles. Why? Because when we conceptualize things like earth or sky, we cannot make judgments until we consider them as subjects, predicates, universals, etc. These are the secondary attributes that enable logical composition.
Secondary intelligibles are called 'secondary' because they are the second level of understanding after primary quiddities.
3
Trace the mental process described
The author explains: we first know simples (mufradat), then we combine them into composites. The mind cannot move from a single meaning to a judgment without adding a second meaning like existence. Logic deals with the conditions for this composition.
This process shows why secondary intelligibles are needed for reasoning.
4
Conclude the subject matter
Thus, logic's subject is the secondary intelligibles — not the quiddities themselves, but their relational attributes (subject/predicate, universal/particular, etc.) that allow us to compose arguments and move from knowns to unknowns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما موضوعه فإنه عبارة عن المعقولات الثانية؛ لما أن موضوع العلم أي علم كان عبارة عما يبحث في ذلك العلم عن أحواله العارضة له لذاته؛ لأن الوسائل متقدمة. وتسمى تلك الأحوال أعراضا ذاتية؛ وذلك هو المعقولات الثانية فيما نحن فيه. فإنه إذا تصورنا حقائق الأشياء، كالأرض والسماء والماء والهواء وغير ذلك، فلا يمكننا أن نتصرف في فرد من هذه الأفراد بأنه كذا وكذا. وكيف يمكن ؟! والذهن لا يمكنه أن ينتقل من معنى مفرد إلى تصديق شيء البتة، بل يمكن ذلك عند مقارنة ذلك المعنى وجوده أو عدمه. وحينئذ يضاف إليه معنى آخر فيوجد في الذهن من المفردات ما يمكن أن يحكم بالبعض منها على البعض حكمًا تقييديًا أو خبريًا. والعلم بالمفردات قبل العلم بالمركبات لا محالة. وإنه إما من حيث هي مستعدة للتأليف، وإما من حيث هي الطبائع وأمور يعرض لها ذلك. فانظر إلى بسائط المركبات، فإن المؤلف منها يحتاج إلى معرفتها؛ لكنها وإن كانت هي من الجواهر مثلا، فلها أحوال تصلح للتأليف وأحوال أخرى خارجة عن ذلك، وإنها من جملة ما لا حاجة إليه أصلا فكذلك فيما نحن بصدده وهو صناعة المنطق، فإنه ليس ينظر في ماهيات الأشياء من حيث هي ماهيات، ولا أيضًا فيما يعرض لها من حيث إنها في الأعيان أو في الأذهان، بل من حيث هي موضوعات ومحمولات وكليات وجزئيات وغير ذلك مما يعرض لها من جهة أنها مستعدة لتأليف ما يلزم المنطقي تأليفه. فإذا بحث عن هذه الاعتبارات لا مطلقا، بل من حيث إنه كيف يمكن أن نتأدَّى بوسائطها من المعلومات إلى المجهولات. فذلك هو البحث في المعقولات الثانية؛ لما أنها في الدرجة الثانية بالنسبة إلى الماهيات التي تعرض لها هذه الاعتبارات . فإذن الموضوع فيما نحن فيه - وهو المنطق - هو المعقولات الثانية. وبالجملة فالاعتبارات التي مرّ ذكرها من قبل نبحث عنها أنها هل هي موجودة أم لا؛ وهذا البحث مما ينظر فيه العلم الناظر ، يعني يكون من موضوع هذا العلم في الوجود ولواحقه، وهو «علم ما بعد الطبيعة». وأيضًا قد نبحث عنها أن وجودها في الذهن أو فيها أو في الخارج، وهذا هو ممَّا ينظر فيه علم النفس؛ وقد نبحث عنها أيضًا من حيث إنها كيف يمكن تركيبها حتى نتأدى منها إلى تعريف المجهولات، وهذا هو مما ينظر فيه علم المنطق.
As for its subject matter, it consists of the secondary intelligibles; because the subject matter of any science is that whose accidental states are investigated in that science due to itself; because the means are prior. Those states are called essential accidents; and that is the secondary intelligibles in what we are dealing with. For if we conceptualize the realities of things, like earth, sky, water, air, and others, we cannot act upon any individual of these individuals by saying it is such and such. How could we?! The mind cannot at all move from a single meaning to assent to something, but that is possible when existence or non-existence of that meaning is compared. Then another meaning is added to it, and there arises in the mind from the simples that which can judge some of them about others by a restrictive or predicative judgment. Knowledge of simples before knowledge of composites is inevitable. And it is either in terms of their being ready for composition, or in terms of their being natures and matters that have that occur to them. Look at the simples of composites: the composite of them needs knowledge of them; but even if they are, for example, substances, they have states suitable for composition and other states outside that, and they are among that which is not needed at all. Similarly in what we are dealing with, which is the art of logic: it does not look at the quiddities of things in terms of being quiddities, nor at what occurs to them in terms of being in external objects or in minds, but rather in terms of their being subjects and predicates, universals and particulars, and other than that which occurs to them from the aspect that they are ready for the composition which the logician must compose. So if we investigate these considerations not absolutely, but in terms of how we can arrive through their means from knowns to unknowns, that is the investigation into secondary intelligibles; because they are in the second degree relative to the quiddities that have these considerations occur to them. Therefore the subject matter in what we are dealing with — which is logic — is the secondary intelligibles. In summary, the considerations mentioned before are investigated by us: whether they exist or not; and this investigation is that which the examining science looks into, meaning it belongs to the subject of that science concerning existence and its concomitants, and it is 'the science of metaphysics'. Also we may investigate them: whether their existence is in the mind, or in themselves, or in external objects; and this is that which the science of psychology looks into. And we may also investigate them in terms of how they can be composed so that we arrive through them to the definition of unknowns, and this is that which the science of logic looks into.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter (mawdu') of logic as the secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). The author first establishes a general principle: the subject of any science is that whose essential accidental states are investigated. For logic, these 'essential accidents' are the secondary intelligibles. He explains why: when we conceptualize external realities (like earth, sky), the mind cannot immediately make judgments about them until a second meaning (like existence or non-existence) is added. Thus, compositeness is required for knowledge. The mind deals with simples (mufradat) that are prepared for composition. Logic does not study quiddities as such (that is metaphysics), nor their existence in the mind or externally (that is psychology), but rather their relation as subjects and predicates, universals and particulars, and other attributes that enable logical composition. These attributes are 'secondary' because they attach to primary quiddities. The author then distinguishes logic from metaphysics (which investigates existence and concomitants) and psychology (which investigates mental existence). Logic specifically investigates how to compose these secondary intelligibles to arrive from knowns to unknowns. Thus, the subject of logic is the secondary intelligibles.

How Logic Differs from Metaphysics and Psychology
Author distinguishes the investigations of logic, metaphysics, and psychology regarding secondary intelligibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the domains of logic, metaphysics, and psychology regarding their treatment of secondary intelligibles.

Core Idea
The same concepts can be studied by different sciences from different aspects.

Secondary intelligibles can be investigated from multiple angles. This lesson clarifies the specific perspective of logic versus that of metaphysics and psychology. The author shows that each science asks distinct questions about secondary intelligibles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three questions about secondary intelligibles
The passage lists three ways to investigate secondary intelligibles: (1) whether they exist or not, (2) whether their existence is in the mind or externally, and (3) how they can be composed to define unknowns.
These correspond to different sciences.
2
Match each question to its science
The first question (existence) belongs to metaphysics ('ilm ma ba'd al-tabi'a). The second question (mode of existence) belongs to psychology ('ilm al-nafs). The third question (composition) belongs to logic.
Logic's focus is the compositional aspect.
3
Understand why these distinctions matter
This division prevents confusion: a logician does not need to decide whether secondary intelligibles really exist; he only cares how they function in reasoning. Similarly, a psychologist does not analyze their logical structure.
This relates to the independence of scientific domains.

Logic's Subject: Secondary Intelligibles
Defining the subject of logic as secondary intelligibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the subject matter of logic from that of metaphysics and psychology by identifying secondary intelligibles.

Core Idea
Logic studies how we reason, not what things are—its subject is secondary intelligibles.

In this passage, the author clarifies what logic does and does not study. He distinguishes logic from the study of essences (metaphysics) and from the study of mental existence (psychology). Instead, logic examines concepts like 'subject,' 'predicate,' 'universal,' 'particular'—the tools we use to compose arguments. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the scope and purpose of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sciences distinguished
The passage distinguishes logic from metaphysics and psychology. Find the sentences that describe what each science investigates: metaphysics asks whether considerations exist, psychology asks about existence in the mind, logic asks how to compose to reach unknowns.
2
Extract the definition of secondary intelligibles
Locate the term 'المعقولات الثانية' (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). The passage defines them as the attributes that attach to essences, like universality and particularity, and are studied from the aspect of their use in reasoning.
They are called 'secondary' because they are in the second degree relative to primary essences.
3
Parse the Arabic phrase for secondary intelligibles
Break down the Arabic: 'المعقولات' (intelligibles) + 'الثانية' (second/ secondary). Understand that they are concepts derived from primary intelligibles.
This term is standard in post-Avicennan logic.
4
Apply the distinction to a concrete example
Take the concept 'universal' (كلي). Which science studies whether it exists? That is metaphysics. Which studies its mental existence? Psychology. Which studies how it functions in a syllogism? That is logic.
This exercise reinforces the three perspectives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعلم بالمفردات قبل العلم بالمركبات لا محالة. وإنه إما من حيث هي مستعدة للتأليف، وإما من حيث هي الطبائع وأمور يعرض لها ذلك. فانظر إلى بسائط المركبات، فإن المؤلف منها يحتاج إلى معرفتها؛ لكنها وإن كانت هي من الجواهر مثلا، فلها أحوال تصلح للتأليف وأحوال أخرى خارجة عن ذلك، وإنها من جملة ما لا حاجة إليه أصلا فكذلك فيما نحن بصدده وهو صناعة المنطق، فإنه ليس ينظر في ماهيات الأشياء من حيث هي ماهيات، ولا أيضًا فيما يعرض لها من حيث إنها في الأعيان أو في الأذهان، بل من حيث هي موضوعات ومحمولات وكليات وجزئيات وغير ذلك مما يعرض لها من جهة أنها مستعدة لتأليف ما يلزم المنطقي تأليفه. فإذا بحث عن هذه الاعتبارات لا مطلقا، بل من حيث إنه كيف يمكن أن نتأدَّى بوسائطها من المعلومات إلى المجهولات. فذلك هو البحث في المعقولات الثانية؛ لما أنها في الدرجة الثانية بالنسبة إلى الماهيات التي تعرض لها هذه الاعتبارات . فإذن الموضوع فيما نحن فيه - وهو المنطق - هو المعقولات الثانية. وبالجملة فالاعتبارات التي مرّ ذكرها من قبل نبحث عنها أنها هل هي موجودة أم لا؛ وهذا البحث مما ينظر فيه العلم الناظر ، يعني يكون من موضوع هذا العلم في الوجود ولواحقه، وهو «علم ما بعد الطبيعة». وأيضًا قد نبحث عنها أن وجودها في الذهن أو فيها أو في الخارج، وهذا هو ممَّا ينظر فيه علم النفس؛ وقد نبحث عنها أيضًا من حيث إنها كيف يمكن تركيبها حتى نتأدى منها إلى تعريف المجهولات، وهذا هو مما ينظر فيه علم المنطق.
Knowledge of simples necessarily precedes knowledge of compounds. And it is either from the aspect that they are prepared for composition, or from the aspect that they are natures and things that happen to them. Consider the simples of compounds, for the one who composes them needs to know them; but even if they are, for example, substances, they have states suitable for composition and other states outside of that, and they are among what is not needed at all. Similarly in what we are concerned with, namely the art of logic, it does not consider the essences of things insofar as they are essences, nor does it consider what happens to them insofar as they are in external existence or in minds, but rather insofar as they are subjects and predicates, universals and particulars, and other things that happen to them from the aspect that they are prepared for the composition that the logician is required to compose. So if it investigates these considerations not absolutely, but from the aspect of how we can arrive by means of them from known things to unknown things, that is the investigation of secondary intelligibles; because they are in the second degree relative to the essences to which these considerations occur. So the subject in what we are dealing with — namely logic — is secondary intelligibles. In summary, the considerations mentioned earlier we investigate whether they exist or not; this investigation belongs to the master science, meaning it is part of the subject of that science concerning existence and its concomitants, which is 'metaphysics'. Also, we may investigate whether their existence is in the mind, in themselves, or in external reality, and this is what psychology investigates; and we may also investigate them from the aspect of how they can be composed so that we may arrive by means of them at the definition of unknowns, and this is what logic investigates.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of logic by clarifying what logic does and does not study. The author begins with a general principle: knowledge of simples precedes knowledge of compounds. Then he applies this to logic. Logic does not investigate essences (mahiyyat) as such, nor their existence in mind or external world; rather, it considers them in terms of being subjects, predicates, universals, particulars, etc.—precisely those aspects that make them fit for composition into arguments. The investigation of how to reason from known to unknown via these considerations is what yields 'secondary intelligibles' (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). These are called secondary because they are attributes (like universality, particularity) that attach to primary essences. The author then distinguishes logic from metaphysics (which asks whether these considerations exist) and psychology (which asks about their mental existence), while logic specifically asks how to combine them to attain definitions and proofs. Thus, the subject of logic is secondary intelligibles from the perspective of their use in reasoning.

According to this passage, what distinguishes the subject of logic from that of metaphysics?
A) Logic studies secondary intelligibles as they exist in the mind; metaphysics studies them as they exist in reality.
B) Logic studies how secondary intelligibles can be combined to reach unknowns; metaphysics studies whether they exist.
C) Logic studies primary essences; metaphysics studies their attributes.
D) There is no distinction; both study the same things.

Key Takeaway
Logic's subject is secondary intelligibles—concepts like subject, predicate, universal, particular—studied not for their existence but for their role in reasoning from known to unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Identify a secondary intelligible in the following statement: 'The world is temporally originated.' Which secondary intelligible is at work, and how does it serve the logician?

The Necessity of Language in Logic
Author argues the necessity of words in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two reasons the author gives for the necessity of words in logic and explain why pure intellectual intuition is insufficient.

Core Idea
Words are indispensable tools for logical reasoning and communication, even though logic is about meanings.

The author begins by acknowledging that words are not the essence of logic, but they are necessary tools. He explores a hypothetical scenario where pure intellection could suffice, but concludes that in reality, words are unavoidable for both teaching and thinking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the necessity claim
Find the phrase 'من جملة ما تدعو إليه الضرورة' – the author states that words are considered out of necessity (darura).
This is the thesis.
2
Examine the counterfactual
Look at 'لو أمكنه أن يطلع على صناعة المنطق بفكره الساذج' – the author imagines a scenario where pure intellect suffices.
This shows the ideal scenario.
3
Assess why the counterfactual fails
Note 'كان من المتعذر على الروية أن ترتيب المعاني من غير أن يتخيل معها الألفاظ' – the mind cannot arrange meanings without imagining words.
This is the key reason.
4
Conclude the necessity
The last sentence shows that without words, meanings would not have certain rulings, so logic must consider words.
This justifies including words in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النظر إلى الألفاظ فإنه من جملة ما تدعو إليه الضرورة؛ إذ المنطقي لا يشتغل بها إلا من جملة المخاطبة أو المحاورة. ولو أمكنه أن يطلع على صناعة المنطق بفكره الساذج دون الاستعانة بتعلّم مخاطبة أو محاورة، وأمكنه أن يطلع المحاور فيه على ما في ذهنه من غير أن يتعرض للألفاظ، فلا حاجة له إلى اللفظ بلفظ ما البتة. لكن لما كانت الضرورة تدعو إلى استعمال الألفاظ وقد كان من المتعذر أيضًا على الرّويّة أن ترتيب المعاني من غير أن يتخيل معها الألفاظ الدالة عليها، بل كانت الرّويّة بينة في الذهن بألفاظ متخيلة مطابقة لزم أن يكون لألفاظ أحوال مختلفة تختلف لأجلها أحوال ما يطابقها في الذهن من المعاني، حتى تصير لها أحكام لولا الألفاظ لم تكن. فتكون الألفاظ بل أحوالها منظورة إليها في صناعة المنطق لا محالة وحينئذ تكون الألفاظ محتاجة إليها، وتلك الألفاظ لا تكون مختصة بلغة قوم دون قوم أصلا؛ لما أن المقصود منها هو الاطلاع على المعاني وذلك من جملة ما لا اختصاص له بقوم على ما عرّف، ولما تعرفت على حقيقة الموضوع وعلى ما يتعلق به من الموجودات والجهات التي بها هي الموضوع، فلا تلتفت إلى قول من يقول: إن موضوع المنطق هو النظر في الألفاظ من حيث إنها تدل على المعاني، وإن المنطقي صناعته أن يتكلم على الألفاظ من حيث إنها تدل على معانيها بالمطابقة أو بغيرها، فإن ذلك من قبيل ما لا يلتفت وهذا ظاهر ولمَّا أشرتُ إلى المقدمات اللازمة معرفتها في هذا الباب إشارة مشتملة على تعريف المنطق وموضوعه و مقصوده و منتهاه على وجه الصحة
As for considering words, it is among the things that necessity calls for; for the logician does not occupy himself with them except as part of address or discussion. If it were possible for him to gain knowledge of the art of logic through his pure intellect without the aid of learning address or discussion, and if it were possible for him to make the interlocutor understand what is in his mind without resorting to words, then he would have no need for words at all. But since necessity calls for the use of words, and it was also impossible for the mind to arrange meanings without imagining with them the words that indicate them—rather, the mind's meaning is clear in the intellect only through imagined corresponding words—then necessarily words have various states, and because of them the states of the meanings that correspond to them in the mind vary, so that they acquire rulings that without words would not exist. Thus words, and their states, are necessarily considered in the art of logic, and then words are needed for it. And those words are not restricted to the language of one people rather than another at all, because what is intended by them is the comprehension of meanings, and that is among what is not specific to any people as was defined. And since you have known the reality of the subject and what pertains to it of existents and the aspects by which it is the subject, do not pay attention to the saying of someone who says: 'The subject of logic is the consideration of words inasmuch as they indicate meanings, and the logician's craft is to discuss words inasmuch as they indicate their meanings by correspondence or otherwise.' For that is of the sort not to be heeded, and this is clear. And I have indicated to the necessary premises to be known in this chapter an indication including the definition of logic, its subject, its aim, and its end in a correct manner.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage explains why words are discussed in logic. The author first states that the logician deals with words only out of necessity, since reasoning requires communication and the mind cannot arrange meanings without linguistic symbols. Ideally, if direct intellectual intuition were possible, words would be unnecessary. But in reality, words are indispensable for teaching and learning logic. The author emphasizes that words themselves are not the subject of logic; rather, they are tools that acquire specific roles because of their correspondence to meanings. Moreover, words are not tied to any particular language, because the goal is universal meaning. The author then warns against the mistaken view that logic is about words and their indicative relations (correspondence, etc.). This view is dismissed as untenable given the correct understanding of logic's subject (secondary intelligibles). The passage ends by noting that the author has correctly indicated the definition, subject, aim, and end of logic.

Key Terms
الألفاظ (al-alfaz): Words as necessary vehicles for conveying meanings in logic; their states (e.g., ambiguity, generality) affect reasoning. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage.]
المخاطبة أو المحاورة (al-mukhataba aw al-muhawara): Address or discussion; the context of teaching and debating logic which requires words. [Note: These terms are common in logic and dialectic contexts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What hypothetical scenario does the author present to show that words are normally necessary?
A) If logic were only for Arabic speakers.
B) If the mind could directly apprehend logic without words and communicate without language.
C) If logic were a divine gift.
D) If words were not ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
Words are necessary tools in logic because the mind cannot arrange meanings without them, and communication requires them.

The Subject of Logic: Words or Meanings?
Author refutes the view that logic is about words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that logic's subject is words and justify why meanings are primary.

Core Idea
Logic's subject is not words but concepts; words are only accidental to the discipline.

Having established that words are necessary, the author now clarifies that logic's proper subject is meanings (secondary intelligibles), not words. He warns against a common misconception that logic studies words as indicators of meanings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the universality of words
Find the phrase 'لا تكون مختصة بلغة قوم دون قوم' – the author states that words are not restricted to any language.
This supports that logic deals with universal meanings.
2
Recall the subject of logic
Look at 'لما تعرفت على حقيقة الموضوع' – the author reminds the reader that the subject (secondary intelligibles) has already been defined.
This is key to the refutation.
3
Identify the erroneous view
Find 'إن موضوع المنطق هو النظر في الألفاظ من حيث إنها تدل على المعاني' – the mistaken claim that logic's subject is words as indicators.
This is what is being refuted.
4
Understand the dismissal
The phrase 'فلا تلتفت إلى قول من يقول' – the author says not to pay attention to that view because it conflicts with the true subject.
The dismissal is based on the established definition.

Muhmal and Musta'mal: The Two Types of Utterances
Author defines muhmal and musta'mal, with utterance as their genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muhmal and musta'mal utterances and explain the role of wad' in defining them.

Core Idea
Every utterance is either coined for a meaning (musta'mal) or left uncoined (muhmal).

In logic, not every combination of sounds carries meaning. The author introduces two fundamental categories: muhmal (unassigned) and musta'mal (assigned). Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing definitions and arguments. Let's break down the passage's definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus
The passage states that 'utterance insofar as it is utterance' functions as a genus for both muhmal and musta'mal. Recognize that they share the same genus.
2
Define muhmal
Muhmal is an utterance that the language coiner (wadi') did not address in the first coinage. It indicates nothing by coinage. Example: al-'ilm (as a raw sound?) – but the passage uses 'science' as an illustration of something not coined? Actually the example is confusing; focus on the definition: no meaning assigned.
3
Define musta'mal
Musta'mal is an utterance coined for a specific meaning. It indicates that meaning by coinage. Example: 'science' (al-'ilm) when coined to convey knowledge.
4
Check the example
The author uses 'science' (العلم) as an example for both? It seems he means the word 'science' considered as a mere sound is muhmal, but when coined it becomes musta'mal. This is subtle: the same phoneme can be considered unassigned or assigned depending on context.
This example may cause confusion; focus on the logical categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجملة الأولى : في المفردات | النوع الأول : في اللفظ وما يتعلق به واعلم أولا بأن اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ يجري مجرى الأجناس بالنسبة إلى المهمل والمستعمل؛ والمهمل هو الذي لا يتعرض له الواضع في الوضع الأول كالعلم مثلًا، وأنه لا يدل على شيء ما أصلًا بالوضع، والمستعمل هو الذي وضع بإزاء شيء ما كالعلم، وأنه يدل على ذلك الشيء بالوضع. فيكون كل واحد منهما مشتملا على اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ وعلى غيره كذلك. واللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ [أ] قد يكون مركبا من الحرفين، وذلك على ضربين من حيث إنه يمكن أن يقدّم هذا على ذلك وبالعكس؛ [ب] وقد يكون مركبًا من ثلاثة أحرف، وذلك على ستة أضرب بحسب تقدم البعض منها على البعض ؛ [ت] وقد يكون مركبًا من أربعة أحرف وذلك على أربعة وعشرين ضربا؛ [ث] وعلى هذا إذا كان مركبًا من خمسة أحرف أو ستة كذلك. والضابط فيه أن يضرب حروف الأعلى من هذه المراتب في تراكيب الأدنى منه كما إذا ضربت الثلاثة في الاثنين، والأربعة في الستة، والخمسة في أربعة وعشرين حتى يحصل منه مائة وعشرين. ثم البعض من هذه التراكيب قد يكون بمعزل عن الإفادة لما أن اللفظ في كل مرتبة من هذه المراتب قد يكون مهملا، وقد يكون مستعملا؛ والمهمل منها ما لا يدل بحسب الوضع على شيء أصلا، والمستعمل منها ما يدل على شيء وضع اللفظ بإزائه، فإنه إذا وضع بإزاء الشيء فقد صار مختصا به من حيث إنه
The first section: On Singulars | The first type: On Utterance and what pertains to it. Know first that the utterance, insofar as it is utterance, functions as a genus with respect to the unassigned (muhmal) and the assigned (musta'mal). The unassigned is that which the coiner does not address in the first coinage, like 'science' for example, and it does not indicate anything at all by coinage. The assigned is that which is coined in exchange for something, like 'science', and it indicates that thing by coinage. Thus each of them comprises the utterance insofar as it is utterance, and other things likewise. The utterance insofar as it is utterance: [a] may be composed of two letters, and that in two ways, since one can be placed before the other and vice versa; [b] may be composed of three letters, and that in six ways according to the precedence of some over others; [c] may be composed of four letters, and that in twenty-four ways; [d] and likewise if it is composed of five or six letters. The rule for that is to multiply the letters of the higher of these ranks by the permutations of the lower, as when you multiply three by two, four by six, five by twenty-four, until you get one hundred and twenty. Then some of these permutations may be devoid of benefit, because the utterance in each of these ranks may be unassigned or assigned. The unassigned among them is what does not indicate anything by coinage, and the assigned among them is what indicates something for which the utterance was coined; for when it is coined for something, it becomes specific to it, insofar as it is.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory text. It begins the first major section (الجملة الأولى) on singulars (مفردات) and specifically the first type (النوع الأول) on utterance (لفظ) and related matters. The author defines two key terms: المهمل (the unassigned) and المستعمل (the assigned). المهمل is an utterance that the language coiner (واضع) did not assign any meaning to in the original coinage, like the word 'science' used as an example (though the example is an odd choice because 'science' does have meaning; perhaps it is meant as a generic term). المستعمل is an utterance that was coined to indicate a specific meaning. The author then discusses the combinatorial possibilities of letters in utterances. He notes that words can be composed of two, three, four, etc., letters, and the number of permutations for n letters is n!. He gives the rule: multiply the number of letters of the higher rank by the permutations of the lower rank. For example, 2 letters have 2 permutations, 3 letters have 6 (3x2), 4 letters have 24 (4x6), 5 have 120 (5x24). He then notes that some of these permutations are meaningless (مهمل) because they are not assigned, while some are assigned (مستعمل). This sets the foundation for discussing meaningful vs. meaningless words in logic.

Key Terms
لفظ
مهمل
مستعمل
وضع

Key Takeaway
The classification of utterances into muhmal and musta'mal hinges on whether the language coiner assigned a meaning to them.

Counting Permutations of Letters
Author calculates the number of letter permutations for utterances of increasing length.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the number of possible permutations for an n-letter utterance and apply the rule given in the passage.

Core Idea
The number of permutations of n letters is n! and is computed by multiplying n by the permutations of n-1.

After defining muhmal and musta'mal, the author calculates how many possible utterances can be formed from a given number of letters. This combinatorial exercise shows how many words could theoretically exist, but only those assigned a meaning are musta'mal. Let's work through the calculation.

Guided Path
1
Start with two letters
Two letters can be arranged in 2 ways (AB, BA). The author calls these 'two ways' (ضربين). Note that the letters are distinct.
2
Move to three letters
Three letters yield 6 permutations. The passage says 'six ways' (ستة أضرب). This is 3! = 6.
3
Generalize the rule
The rule given: multiply the number of letters of the higher rank by the permutations of the lower. For 4 letters: 4 × 6 = 24; for 5: 5 × 24 = 120; for 6: 6 × 120 = 720; etc.
This is equivalent to n! = n × (n-1)!.
4
Apply the rule to your own example
Try computing permutations for 7 letters: 7 × 720 = 5040. This matches the factorial pattern.
The passage stops at 5 letters giving 120, but the rule extends.

الجملة الأولى : في المفردات | النوع الأول : في اللفظ وما يتعلق به واعلم أولا بأن اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ يجري مجرى الأجناس بالنسبة إلى المهمل والمستعمل؛ والمهمل هو الذي لا يتعرض له الواضع في الوضع الأول كالعلم مثلًا، وأنه لا يدل على شيء ما أصلًا بالوضع، والمستعمل هو الذي وضع بإزاء شيء ما كالعلم، وأنه يدل على ذلك الشيء بالوضع. فيكون كل واحد منهما مشتملا على اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ وعلى غيره كذلك. واللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ [أ] قد يكون مركبا من الحرفين، وذلك على ضربين من حيث إنه يمكن أن يقدّم هذا على ذلك وبالعكس؛ [ب] وقد يكون مركبًا من ثلاثة أحرف، وذلك على ستة أضرب بحسب تقدم البعض منها على البعض ؛ [ت] وقد يكون مركبًا من أربعة أحرف وذلك على أربعة وعشرين ضربا؛ [ث] وعلى هذا إذا كان مركبًا من خمسة أحرف أو ستة كذلك. والضابط فيه أن يضرب حروف الأعلى من هذه المراتب في تراكيب الأدنى منه كما إذا ضربت الثلاثة في الاثنين، والأربعة في الستة، والخمسة في أربعة وعشرين حتى يحصل منه مائة وعشرين. ثم البعض من هذه التراكيب قد يكون بمعزل عن الإفادة لما أن اللفظ في كل مرتبة من هذه المراتب قد يكون مهملا، وقد يكون مستعملا؛ والمهمل منها ما لا يدل بحسب الوضع على شيء أصلا، والمستعمل منها ما يدل على شيء وضع اللفظ بإزائه، فإنه إذا وضع بإزاء الشيء فقد صار مختصا به من حيث إنه
The first section: On Singulars | The first type: On Utterance and what pertains to it. Know first that the utterance, insofar as it is utterance, functions as a genus with respect to the unassigned (muhmal) and the assigned (musta'mal). The unassigned is that which the coiner does not address in the first coinage, like 'science' for example, and it does not indicate anything at all by coinage. The assigned is that which is coined in exchange for something, like 'science', and it indicates that thing by coinage. Thus each of them comprises the utterance insofar as it is utterance, and other things likewise. The utterance insofar as it is utterance: [a] may be composed of two letters, and that in two ways, since one can be placed before the other and vice versa; [b] may be composed of three letters, and that in six ways according to the precedence of some over others; [c] may be composed of four letters, and that in twenty-four ways; [d] and likewise if it is composed of five or six letters. The rule for that is to multiply the letters of the higher of these ranks by the permutations of the lower, as when you multiply three by two, four by six, five by twenty-four, until you get one hundred and twenty. Then some of these permutations may be devoid of benefit, because the utterance in each of these ranks may be unassigned or assigned. The unassigned among them is what does not indicate anything by coinage, and the assigned among them is what indicates something for which the utterance was coined; for when it is coined for something, it becomes specific to it, insofar as it is.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory text. It begins the first major section (الجملة الأولى) on singulars (مفردات) and specifically the first type (النوع الأول) on utterance (لفظ) and related matters. The author defines two key terms: المهمل (the unassigned) and المستعمل (the assigned). المهمل is an utterance that the language coiner (واضع) did not assign any meaning to in the original coinage, like the word 'science' used as an example (though the example is an odd choice because 'science' does have meaning; perhaps it is meant as a generic term). المستعمل is an utterance that was coined to indicate a specific meaning. The author then discusses the combinatorial possibilities of letters in utterances. He notes that words can be composed of two, three, four, etc., letters, and the number of permutations for n letters is n!. He gives the rule: multiply the number of letters of the higher rank by the permutations of the lower rank. For example, 2 letters have 2 permutations, 3 letters have 6 (3x2), 4 letters have 24 (4x6), 5 have 120 (5x24). He then notes that some of these permutations are meaningless (مهمل) because they are not assigned, while some are assigned (مستعمل). This sets the foundation for discussing meaningful vs. meaningless words in logic.

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
10 min
Lafz: Muhmal and Musta'mal
Author defines lafz, distinguishes muhmal and musta'mal, and enumerates possible letter combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muhmal and musta'mal utterances and calculate the number of possible letter arrangements for a given number of letters.

Core Idea
Every utterance is either coined (musta'mal) or neglected (muhmal); the logician studies both.

In logic, the first step is to understand the raw material: words. The author explains what an utterance (lafz) is, and how it can be either coined for a meaning or left uncoined. He also marvels at the mathematical possibilities of letter combinations, showing that most possible words are meaningless without convention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and species
The author says lafz runs as a genus for muhmal and musta'mal. Notice that lafz is common to both; muhmal and musta'mal are species.
2
Define muhmal and musta'mal
Muhmal is an utterance not coined for any meaning; musta'mal is coined for a specific meaning. The author gives 'science' as an example for both, but note that in different contexts the same word could be either.
3
Work out the combinatorial rule
The author gives permutations for 2, 3, 4 letters: 2, 6, 24. The rule is factorial: n! permutations. Verify: 2! = 2, 3! = 6, 4! = 24, 5! = 120.
4
Connect permutations to meaning
Among all permutations, some are muhmal and some are musta'mal. The logician considers all utterances regardless of language.
5
Recognize the logician's focus
The logician primarily studies meanings, but utterance states affect meanings, so he considers utterances indirectly. Some states are universal (noun/verb, literal/metaphorical), some language-specific (like Arabic grammatical distinctions).

واعلم أولا بأن اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ يجري مجرى الأجناس بالنسبة إلى المهمل والمستعمل؛ والمهمل هو الذي لا يتعرض له الواضع في الوضع الأول كالعلم مثلًا، وأنه لا يدل على شيء ما أصلًا بالوضع، والمستعمل هو الذي وضع بإزاء شيء ما كالعلم، وأنه يدل على ذلك الشيء بالوضع. فيكون كل واحد منهما مشتملا على اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ وعلى غيره كذلك. واللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ قد يكون مركبا من الحرفين، وذلك على ضربين من حيث إنه يمكن أن يقدّم هذا على ذلك وبالعكس؛ وقد يكون مركبًا من ثلاثة أحرف، وذلك على ستة أضرب بحسب تقدم البعض منها على البعض ; وقد يكون مركبًا من أربعة أحرف وذلك على أربعة وعشرين ضربا؛ وعلى هذا إذا كان مركبًا من خمسة أحرف أو ستة كذلك. والضابط فيه أن يضرب حروف الأعلى من هذه المراتب في تراكيب الأدنى منه كما إذا ضربت الثلاثة في الاثنين، والأربعة في الستة، والخمسة في أربعة وعشرين حتى يحصل منه مائة وعشرين. ثم البعض من هذه التراكيب قد يكون بمعزل عن الإفادة لما أن اللفظ في كل مرتبة من هذه المراتب قد يكون مهملا، وقد يكون مستعملا؛ والمهمل منها ما لا يدل بحسب الوضع على شيء أصلا، والمستعمل منها ما يدل على شيء وضع اللفظ بإزائه، فإنه إذا وضع بإزاء الشيء فقد صار مختصا به من حيث إنه اسم ذلك. وذلك مسماه على وجه لا يفارق هذا ذلك وبالعكس، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون دالا عليه؛ إذ الدال على شيء هو الذي يلزم من العلم به العلم بوجود غيره كذلك بالنسبة إلى مسماه وضعًا وبالنسبة إلى ما هو اللازم للمسمى عقلا. ولا يستراب في أنه إذا وضع بإزاء شيء فقد تعلَّق بذلك الشيء على حسب ذلك التعلَّق، فلا يبعد إذن أن تؤثر أحوال في اللفظ في أحوال في المعنى ولذلك يلزم للمنطقي أن يراعي جانب اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ أي لفظ كان من أي لغة كان كما مرَّ من قبل ، لكن لا يلزمه بالقصد الأول. فإن نظره في المعاني أولا دون الألفاظ لما أن الألفاظ جارية مجرى المعاني لتعريفها، ولها من الأحوال لا محالة؛ وإنها إما عامة لا اختصاص لها بلغة قوم دون قوم، مثل كونها منقسمة إلى الأسماء والأفعال وكونها مستعملة بطريق الحقيقة والمجاز كذلك، وإما خاصة بلغة قوم، كما يكون مختصا بلغة العرب من الفرق بين كل الإنسان وكل إنسان ولا إنسانَ بالفتح ولا إنسان وغير ذلك. ثم اللفظ المطلق - وهو المتعرض للذات دون الصفات - إذا كان دالا على الشيء كما مر , فدلالته وهي اتصافه بصفة أن يلزم من العلم به العلم بوجود غيره على ثلاثة أقسام لا غير. وذلك لأنه إما أن يدل على تمام مسماه أو على ما يكون داخلا فيه أو على ما يكون خارجا عنه. والأول بطريق المطابقة كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على الحيوان الناطق، ودلالة لفظ الأسد على الحيوان الزائر ; والثاني بطريق التضمن كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على الحيوان مجرَّدًا أو على الناطق كذلك، ودلالة لفظ الأسد على الحيوان مجرَّدًا أو على الزائر كذلك؛ والثالث بطريق الالتزام كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على قابل صنعة الكتابة، ودلالة لفظ الأسد على الشجاع مثلا. وأما التعرض لقابل صنعة الكتابة دون الكاتب، فذلك أن القابل يلزم الإنسان والكاتب لا يلزمه.
Know first that lafz (utterance) insofar as it is an utterance runs in the manner of genera with respect to al-muhmal (the neglected) and al-musta'mal (the used). Al-muhmal is that which the coiner does not address in the first coinage, like 'science' for example, and it does not indicate anything at all by coinage. Al-musta'mal is that which is coined for something, like 'science', and it indicates that thing by coinage. So each of them includes the utterance insofar as it is an utterance and something else as well. The utterance insofar as it is an utterance may be composed of two letters, and that is in two ways in terms of the possibility of putting this one before that one and vice versa. It may be composed of three letters, and that is in six ways according to the precedence of some over others. It may be composed of four letters, and that is in twenty-four ways. And likewise if it is composed of five or six letters. The rule for that is to multiply the number of letters of the higher rank by the permutations of the lower, as when you multiply three by two, four by six, five by twenty-four, until you get one hundred and twenty. Then some of these permutations may be devoid of signification, because the utterance at each of these ranks may be muhmal or musta'mal. Al-muhmal from them is that which does not indicate anything at all according to coinage. Al-musta'mal from them is that which indicates something for which the utterance was coined. For when it is coined for a thing, it becomes specific to it in that it is its name. That is its named thing in a way that this does not separate from that and vice versa. Then necessarily it is a signifier of it; for the signifier of something is that from the knowledge of which knowledge of the existence of something else follows, similarly with respect to its named thing by coinage and with respect to what is necessary for the named thing by reason. And there is no doubt that when it is coined for something, it is attached to that thing according to that attachment. So it is not unlikely that states in the utterance affect states in the meaning, and therefore the logician must consider the side of the utterance insofar as it is an utterance, whatever utterance it may be from any language, as was stated before, but he does not need to do so as a primary intention. For his consideration is primarily of meanings, not utterances, because utterances are in the position of meanings for their definition, and they inevitably have states. And these states are either general, not specific to the language of one people over another, such as their being divided into nouns and verbs, and their being used by way of reality and metaphor likewise; or they are specific to the language of a people, such as being specific to the language of the Arabs regarding the difference between 'kullu al-insan' and 'kullu insan' and 'la insana' with fatha and 'la insana' etc. Then the absolute utterance—which addresses the essence without attributes—when it indicates a thing as previously mentioned, its indication, which is its being characterized by the property that from knowledge of it follows knowledge of the existence of something else, is of three divisions only. That is because it either indicates the entirety of its named thing, or what is included in it, or what is external to it. The first is by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), like the indication of the utterance 'insan' (human) for 'rational animal', and the indication of the utterance 'asad' (lion) for 'the ferocious animal'. The second is by way of inclusion (tadammun), like the indication of the utterance 'insan' for 'animal' alone or for 'rational' alone, and the indication of the utterance 'asad' for 'animal' alone or for 'ferocious' alone. The third is by way of implication (iltizam), like the indication of the utterance 'insan' for 'receptive of the craft of writing', and the indication of the utterance 'asad' for 'brave' for example. As for addressing 'receptive of the craft of writing' rather than 'writer', that is because the receptive is necessary for the human, but the writer is not necessary for him.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is definitional and explanatory. It opens by establishing the fundamental concept of 'lafz' (utterance) and its two primary divisions: muhmal (neglected, not coined) and musta'mal (used, coined). The author clarifies that any utterance, as a sound or script, can be considered as a genus for these two species. He then explores the combinatorial possibilities of utterances based on the number of letters, illustrating how many distinct two-letter, three-letter, etc., combinations are possible, and provides a mathematical rule: the permutations of n letters are n! (though he only multiplies up to 5 letters giving 120). This demonstrates the vastness of possible utterances. Next, he returns to the distinction between muhmal and musta'mal, emphasizing that among these permutations, some are assigned meanings by convention (musta'mal) and some are not (muhmal). The musta'mal becomes specifically tied to its meaning such that whenever the utterance is known, the meaning is also known. This leads to the definition of dalaala (indication): a property of the utterance such that knowledge of the utterance necessitates knowledge of something else. He then explains that the logician must consider utterances because they affect meanings, but only indirectly—the primary focus of logic is on meanings, not words. However, some properties of utterances are universal (like being noun or verb, literal or metaphorical) and some are language-specific (e.g., Arabic distinctions). Finally, he introduces the three types of indication for a word that signifies its essence (al-lafz al-mutlaq). These are: (1) mutabaqa (correspondence): the word indicates the entire meaning, e.g., 'insan' indicates 'rational animal'; (2) tadammun (inclusion): the word indicates a part of the meaning, e.g., 'insan' indicates 'animal' or 'rational' alone; (3) iltizam (implication): the word indicates something external to the meaning but necessarily connected, e.g., 'insan' indicates 'capable of writing' because rationality implies that capability, whereas 'writer' is not necessary. The author carefully notes that the implication is of a necessary concomitant, not an accidental property.

Key Terms

مهمل
مستعمل

Key Takeaway
An utterance can be either coined (musta'mal) or neglected (muhmal); the logician analyzes all utterances as potential carriers of meaning.

Take the Arabic word 'kitab' (book). Determine whether it is muhmal or musta'mal, and explain why. Then, following the author's method, calculate how many permutations are possible for a three-letter root like k-t-b (assuming each letter used once).

What Is Mantiq?
beginner
10 min
The Three Types of Indication
Author defines the three types of indication for absolute utterances: mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the three types of indication (dilala) for a coined utterance: mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam.

Core Idea
A word can indicate its entire meaning, a part of it, or an external necessary concomitant.

After clarifying the nature of utterances, the author turns to how coined words signify meanings. He explains that indication is not monolithic: a word like 'insan' simultaneously points to its full definition, to components like 'animal', and to necessary attributes like 'capable of writing'. Understanding these three types is essential for analyzing definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of dilala
The author defines indication as the property of a word such that knowledge of the word leads to knowledge of something else. This is the genus for the three types.
2
Identify the three divisions
Indication is either of the whole meaning (mutabaqa), a part (tadammun), or an external necessary concomitant (iltizam). The author presents these as the only three possibilities: 'either indicates its entire named thing, or what is included in it, or what is external to it.'
3
Analyze the example of 'insan'
For 'insan' (human): mutabaqa gives 'rational animal', tadammun gives 'animal' alone or 'rational' alone, iltizam gives 'capable of writing'. Note that 'writer' is not implied because it is not necessary.
4
Test another example: 'asad'
For 'asad' (lion): mutabaqa gives 'ferocious animal', tadammun gives 'animal' alone or 'ferocious' alone, iltizam gives 'bravery'.
5
Recognize the logician's use
The logician uses these distinctions when defining terms or evaluating arguments that rely on word meaning. For instance, a definition must capture the mutabaqa indication, while an argument may exploit tadammun or iltizam.

واعلم أولا بأن اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ يجري مجرى الأجناس بالنسبة إلى المهمل والمستعمل؛ والمهمل هو الذي لا يتعرض له الواضع في الوضع الأول كالعلم مثلًا، وأنه لا يدل على شيء ما أصلًا بالوضع، والمستعمل هو الذي وضع بإزاء شيء ما كالعلم، وأنه يدل على ذلك الشيء بالوضع. فيكون كل واحد منهما مشتملا على اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ وعلى غيره كذلك. واللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ قد يكون مركبا من الحرفين، وذلك على ضربين من حيث إنه يمكن أن يقدّم هذا على ذلك وبالعكس؛ وقد يكون مركبًا من ثلاثة أحرف، وذلك على ستة أضرب بحسب تقدم البعض منها على البعض ; وقد يكون مركبًا من أربعة أحرف وذلك على أربعة وعشرين ضربا؛ وعلى هذا إذا كان مركبًا من خمسة أحرف أو ستة كذلك. والضابط فيه أن يضرب حروف الأعلى من هذه المراتب في تراكيب الأدنى منه كما إذا ضربت الثلاثة في الاثنين، والأربعة في الستة، والخمسة في أربعة وعشرين حتى يحصل منه مائة وعشرين. ثم البعض من هذه التراكيب قد يكون بمعزل عن الإفادة لما أن اللفظ في كل مرتبة من هذه المراتب قد يكون مهملا، وقد يكون مستعملا؛ والمهمل منها ما لا يدل بحسب الوضع على شيء أصلا، والمستعمل منها ما يدل على شيء وضع اللفظ بإزائه، فإنه إذا وضع بإزاء الشيء فقد صار مختصا به من حيث إنه اسم ذلك. وذلك مسماه على وجه لا يفارق هذا ذلك وبالعكس، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون دالا عليه؛ إذ الدال على شيء هو الذي يلزم من العلم به العلم بوجود غيره كذلك بالنسبة إلى مسماه وضعًا وبالنسبة إلى ما هو اللازم للمسمى عقلا. ولا يستراب في أنه إذا وضع بإزاء شيء فقد تعلَّق بذلك الشيء على حسب ذلك التعلَّق، فلا يبعد إذن أن تؤثر أحوال في اللفظ في أحوال في المعنى ولذلك يلزم للمنطقي أن يراعي جانب اللفظ من حيث هو اللفظ أي لفظ كان من أي لغة كان كما مرَّ من قبل ، لكن لا يلزمه بالقصد الأول. فإن نظره في المعاني أولا دون الألفاظ لما أن الألفاظ جارية مجرى المعاني لتعريفها، ولها من الأحوال لا محالة؛ وإنها إما عامة لا اختصاص لها بلغة قوم دون قوم، مثل كونها منقسمة إلى الأسماء والأفعال وكونها مستعملة بطريق الحقيقة والمجاز كذلك، وإما خاصة بلغة قوم، كما يكون مختصا بلغة العرب من الفرق بين كل الإنسان وكل إنسان ولا إنسانَ بالفتح ولا إنسان وغير ذلك. ثم اللفظ المطلق - وهو المتعرض للذات دون الصفات - إذا كان دالا على الشيء كما مر , فدلالته وهي اتصافه بصفة أن يلزم من العلم به العلم بوجود غيره على ثلاثة أقسام لا غير. وذلك لأنه إما أن يدل على تمام مسماه أو على ما يكون داخلا فيه أو على ما يكون خارجا عنه. والأول بطريق المطابقة كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على الحيوان الناطق، ودلالة لفظ الأسد على الحيوان الزائر ; والثاني بطريق التضمن كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على الحيوان مجرَّدًا أو على الناطق كذلك، ودلالة لفظ الأسد على الحيوان مجرَّدًا أو على الزائر كذلك؛ والثالث بطريق الالتزام كدلالة لفظ الإنسان على قابل صنعة الكتابة، ودلالة لفظ الأسد على الشجاع مثلا. وأما التعرض لقابل صنعة الكتابة دون الكاتب، فذلك أن القابل يلزم الإنسان والكاتب لا يلزمه.
Know first that lafz (utterance) insofar as it is an utterance runs in the manner of genera with respect to al-muhmal (the neglected) and al-musta'mal (the used). Al-muhmal is that which the coiner does not address in the first coinage, like 'science' for example, and it does not indicate anything at all by coinage. Al-musta'mal is that which is coined for something, like 'science', and it indicates that thing by coinage. So each of them includes the utterance insofar as it is an utterance and something else as well. The utterance insofar as it is an utterance may be composed of two letters, and that is in two ways in terms of the possibility of putting this one before that one and vice versa. It may be composed of three letters, and that is in six ways according to the precedence of some over others. It may be composed of four letters, and that is in twenty-four ways. And likewise if it is composed of five or six letters. The rule for that is to multiply the number of letters of the higher rank by the permutations of the lower, as when you multiply three by two, four by six, five by twenty-four, until you get one hundred and twenty. Then some of these permutations may be devoid of signification, because the utterance at each of these ranks may be muhmal or musta'mal. Al-muhmal from them is that which does not indicate anything at all according to coinage. Al-musta'mal from them is that which indicates something for which the utterance was coined. For when it is coined for a thing, it becomes specific to it in that it is its name. That is its named thing in a way that this does not separate from that and vice versa. Then necessarily it is a signifier of it; for the signifier of something is that from the knowledge of which knowledge of the existence of something else follows, similarly with respect to its named thing by coinage and with respect to what is necessary for the named thing by reason. And there is no doubt that when it is coined for something, it is attached to that thing according to that attachment. So it is not unlikely that states in the utterance affect states in the meaning, and therefore the logician must consider the side of the utterance insofar as it is an utterance, whatever utterance it may be from any language, as was stated before, but he does not need to do so as a primary intention. For his consideration is primarily of meanings, not utterances, because utterances are in the position of meanings for their definition, and they inevitably have states. And these states are either general, not specific to the language of one people over another, such as their being divided into nouns and verbs, and their being used by way of reality and metaphor likewise; or they are specific to the language of a people, such as being specific to the language of the Arabs regarding the difference between 'kullu al-insan' and 'kullu insan' and 'la insana' with fatha and 'la insana' etc. Then the absolute utterance—which addresses the essence without attributes—when it indicates a thing as previously mentioned, its indication, which is its being characterized by the property that from knowledge of it follows knowledge of the existence of something else, is of three divisions only. That is because it either indicates the entirety of its named thing, or what is included in it, or what is external to it. The first is by way of correspondence (mutabaqa), like the indication of the utterance 'insan' (human) for 'rational animal', and the indication of the utterance 'asad' (lion) for 'the ferocious animal'. The second is by way of inclusion (tadammun), like the indication of the utterance 'insan' for 'animal' alone or for 'rational' alone, and the indication of the utterance 'asad' for 'animal' alone or for 'ferocious' alone. The third is by way of implication (iltizam), like the indication of the utterance 'insan' for 'receptive of the craft of writing', and the indication of the utterance 'asad' for 'brave' for example. As for addressing 'receptive of the craft of writing' rather than 'writer', that is because the receptive is necessary for the human, but the writer is not necessary for him.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is definitional and explanatory. It opens by establishing the fundamental concept of 'lafz' (utterance) and its two primary divisions: muhmal (neglected, not coined) and musta'mal (used, coined). The author clarifies that any utterance, as a sound or script, can be considered as a genus for these two species. He then explores the combinatorial possibilities of utterances based on the number of letters, illustrating how many distinct two-letter, three-letter, etc., combinations are possible, and provides a mathematical rule: the permutations of n letters are n! (though he only multiplies up to 5 letters giving 120). This demonstrates the vastness of possible utterances. Next, he returns to the distinction between muhmal and musta'mal, emphasizing that among these permutations, some are assigned meanings by convention (musta'mal) and some are not (muhmal). The musta'mal becomes specifically tied to its meaning such that whenever the utterance is known, the meaning is also known. This leads to the definition of dalaala (indication): a property of the utterance such that knowledge of the utterance necessitates knowledge of something else. He then explains that the logician must consider utterances because they affect meanings, but only indirectly—the primary focus of logic is on meanings, not words. However, some properties of utterances are universal (like being noun or verb, literal or metaphorical) and some are language-specific (e.g., Arabic distinctions). Finally, he introduces the three types of indication for a word that signifies its essence (al-lafz al-mutlaq). These are: (1) mutabaqa (correspondence): the word indicates the entire meaning, e.g., 'insan' indicates 'rational animal'; (2) tadammun (inclusion): the word indicates a part of the meaning, e.g., 'insan' indicates 'animal' or 'rational' alone; (3) iltizam (implication): the word indicates something external to the meaning but necessarily connected, e.g., 'insan' indicates 'capable of writing' because rationality implies that capability, whereas 'writer' is not necessary. The author carefully notes that the implication is of a necessary concomitant, not an accidental property.

Key Terms
دلالة مطابقة
دلالة تضمن
دلالة التزام

Key Takeaway
The three types of indication—correspondence, inclusion, and implication—are essential for understanding how words convey meaning beyond their full definitions.

Words in Use and the Concept of Wad'
Author defines words in use and their multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-alfaz al-musta'mala and explain why their multiplicity follows from the multiplicity of meanings in discourse.

Core Idea
Words are defined by their assignment (wad') to meanings; their diversity mirrors the needs of communication.

This passage opens a new section on words that are actually used in discourse. The author establishes that each such word is set (wad') to indicate a specific meaning. The number of words corresponds to the number of meanings required for communication to be orderly. This foundational concept is essential for understanding how terms function in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
The passage defines 'words in use' (al-alfaz al-musta'mala) as words that are 'placed to define what they are set against.' Recognize that wad' (conventional assignment) is the key link between a word and its meaning.
This definition is broad, covering all words used with specific meanings in discourse.
2
Trace the reason for multiplicity
The author states that words are multiple because of the multiplicity of meanings needed in addresses and conversations. The purpose is to ensure correct communication and order (istiqamah al-ahwal).
This explains why languages have many words: to meet the diverse conceptual needs of speakers.

The author defines them as words that are specifically placed (wad') to indicate what they stand for (ma wudi'a bi-iza'ihi). Their multiplicity is due to the multiplicity of meanings required in speech and dialogue to ensure correct communication and order. The text cuts off, but the section likely continues to enumerate types or categories of such words, perhaps building on the earlier discussion of signification. This is a definitional passage that sets the stage for further classification.
