Three Types of Transferred Names in Islamic Terminology
Author classifies transferred names into shar'iyya, 'urfiyya, and istilahiyya with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given technical term as shar'iyya, 'urfiyya, or istilahiyya based on the source of its transferred meaning.

Core Idea
A word's true meaning depends on who transferred it: Sharia, common custom, or specialist convention.

This passage introduces the crucial distinction between original lexical meanings and transferred meanings in Islamic sciences. You will learn how to identify whether a term has been invested with a new sense by divine law, everyday usage, or scholarly consensus.

Guided Path
1
Extract the original linguistic meaning
Research the root meaning of the term in Arabic lexicons as used before the transfer. This is often found in classical dictionaries.
For example, 'salat' originally meant 'supplication'.
2
Identify the transferred technical meaning
Determine how the term is used in the specific field: Sharia, common speech, or a discipline like grammar.
In Sharia, 'salat' refers to the ritual prayer with specific actions.
3
Determine the transferrer (nāqil)
Check whether the new meaning was established by divine law, everyday custom, or specialist convention.
The transferrer for 'salat' is the Sharia, making it shar'iyya.
4
Classify the term
Assign it to shar'iyya, 'urfiyya, or istilahiyya based on the transferrer identified in step 3.
5
Verify against the fourfold division of haqiqa
If the term is transferred, it falls under one of the three categories; if not, it is wad'iyya (original lexical meaning).
The author notes that transferred names constitute three of the four kinds of haqiqa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأسماء المنقولة يسمى «شرعية» إن كان الناقل هو الشرع؛ كاسم الصلاة والزكاة والصوم وغير ذلك. فإن الصلاة في اللغة عبارة عن الدعاء بالنقل، وقول القائل: «وصل على ذاتها وارتسم، ثم صارت في الشرع عبارة عن الأركان المعهودة من القيام والقعود والركوع والسجود وغيرها على ما عرف والزكاة في اللغة عبارة عن الزيادة والنماء، يقال زكى الزرع إذا ازداد ونمى، ثم صارت في الشرع عبارة عن قطع طائفة من مال المزكي بجهة التمليك إلى الفقير والصوم في اللغة عبارة عن مجرد الإمساك، يقال خيل صيام وخيل غير صائمة، ثم صار في الشرع عبارة عن الإمساك من أول النهار إلى آخره مقرونا بوصف الطهارة عن الحيض والنفاس. وعليهذا يسمى عرفية إن كان الناقل هو العرف العام، كاسم القارورة والدابة وغير ذلك؛ فإن القارورة في اللغة عبارة عما يستقر فيه المائع، وصارت في عرف أهل اللغة عبارة عما يتحد من الزجاج من ذلك الجنس؛ والدابة في اللغة عبارة عما يدب في الأرض، ثم صارت في العرف عبارة عن الفرس، وذلك لكثرة الاستعمال. «اصطلاحية» أيضًا إن كان الناقل هو العرف الخاص، كما ويسمى اصطلاحات النحاة والنظار وغيرهم من أهل العلم، فالمبتدأ مثلا في اللغة عبارة عما يبتدأ به وفي اصطلاح أهل النحو عبارة عن الاسم المجرد عن العوامل اللفظية للإسناد؛ والإعراب أيضًا في اللغة عبارة عن الإبانة والإظهار، يقال أعرب كلامه إذا أظهره، وفي الحديث الشيب يعرب عنها لسانها أي يظهر، وفي اصطلاح أهل النحو عبارة عن اختلاف آخر الكلمة باختلاف العوامل. ولما كانت الحقيقة في الأسماء المنقولة على ثلاثة أقسام فالحقيقة في جميع الأسماء على أربعة أقسام: وضعية وشرعية وعرفية واصطلاحية كما مر ذكرها من قبل.
Then the transferred names are called 'shar'iyya' if the transferrer is the Sharia; like the name salat, zakat, siyam, and others. For salat in language is invocation by transfer, and the saying of the speaker: 'He prayed for its essence and was established', then in Sharia it came to be the well-known pillars of standing, sitting, bowing, prostrating, and others as known. Zakat in language is increase and growth; one says 'the crop increased' when it grows, then in Sharia it came to be a specified portion of the owner's wealth given by way of ownership to the poor. Siyam in language is mere abstention; one says 'horses fasting' and 'horses not fasting', then in Sharia it came to be abstention from the beginning of the day to its end accompanied by the condition of purity from menstruation and postnatal bleeding. Similarly, it is called 'urfiyya if the transferrer is common custom, like the names qarura and dabba and others; for qarura in language is that in which liquid settles, and in the custom of the people of language it came to be a specific type of glass; dabba in language is that which crawls on the earth, then in custom it came to mean horse, due to frequent usage. 'Istilahiyya' also if the transferrer is specific custom, as it is called the technical terms of grammarians and dialecticians and other scholars. For instance, mubtada' in language is that with which one begins; in the terminology of grammarians it is the noun stripped of verbal operators for predication. I'rab in language is clarification and manifestation; one says 'he clarified his speech' when he manifested it; in the noble hadith 'the grey hair reveals her tongue' meaning manifests; in the terminology of grammarians it is the change of the end of the word due to differing operators. Since the reality of transferred names is three kinds, the reality of all names is four kinds: wad'iyya, shar'iyya, 'urfiyya, and istilahiyya, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a classification of transferred names (al-asma' al-manqula). The author first defines the three categories based on the source of transference. The first is shar'iyya (religious terms) where the Sharia transfers a linguistic meaning to a new technical meaning. Examples: salat (prayer), zakat (alms), siyam (fasting). For each, the author shows the linguistic origin and the Sharia meaning. Second, 'urfiyya (customary terms) where common custom transfers meaning, like qarura (bottle) and dabba (animal). Third, istilahiyya (technical terms) from specific custom, e.g., grammatical terms mubtada' (subject) and i'rab (inflection). The author then summarizes: transferred names make up three of the four categories of reality (haqiqa), the fourth being wad'iyya (original lexical meaning). This classification is foundational for understanding how words carry meaning in religious and scholarly discourse.

Key Terms
الاسم المنقول
شرعية
عرفية
اصطلاحية
حقيقة
وضعية

Determining Simple vs. Compound Names
Author responds to objection that negative compounds are not simple names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given expression is a simple name (ism mufrad) or a compound (murakkab), using the criteria from the passage.

Core Idea
A true name cannot include a particle; 'lā insān' is a compound, not a simple name.

In logic, defining 'ism' (name) precisely is crucial because definitions (ḥadd) and descriptions (rasm) are built from names. This passage addresses an objection: aren't expressions like 'lā insān' (not-man) simple names? The author clarifies that such expressions are compounds, not true simple names, and explains why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression in question.
Look at the example 'lā insān' and 'lā baṣīr'. Note that they consist of a negation particle (lā) plus a noun.
2
Check for particles.
If the expression includes a particle like negation, it is not a simple name (mufrad) but a compound (murakkab).
3
Evaluate if it is a true name.
The author states that such expressions are not names in reality (ism bi-l-ḥaqīqa) because they are compounds.
Compounds like definitions (ḥadd) are also not simple names.
4
Consider the counterargument.
An objector says that 'lā baṣīr' is placed to mean a blind person, so it seems like a name. The response: if it were a name, it would be a proper name (alam), not a general simple name.
Proper names have their own logical status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول في المفردة : إن من الأسماء ما لا يكون كذلك كقولنا: «لا إنسان» و «لا بصير» مثلا، فإنه مركب من حرف السلب وغيره؛ فيقال في الأول مثل كقولنا : «لا إنسان» و «لا بصير» لا يكون اسما بالحقيقة بل هو مركب من اسم و حرف معناه معنى الاسم المفرد، ومن المركبات ما يكون كذلك كالحد والرسم مثلا. وإن قيل: كيف لا يكون اسما بالحقيقة، وإنه موضوع بإزاء شيء كاللا بصير بإزاء الأعمى مثلا؟ فنقول : لو كان كما ذكرتم لكان ذلك من الأعلام، وفيه من الكلام أيضًا.
Then an objector may say about the simple [word]: Among names there are some that are not like that, such as our saying 'lā insān' and 'lā baṣīr' for example, for it is composed of a particle of negation and another. So it is said in the first, like our saying 'lā insān' and 'lā baṣīr,' that it is not a name in reality, but rather it is composed of a name and a particle whose meaning is the meaning of a simple name. Among compounds there are some that are like that, such as the definition (ḥadd) and the description (rasm) for example. And if it is said: How is it not a name in reality, while it is placed in relation to something, like 'lā baṣīr' in relation to the blind man for example? We say: If it were as you mentioned, it would be among proper names, and there is also discussion in that.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the definition of a simple name (mufrad). The objector argues that some names, like 'lā insān' (not-man) or 'lā baṣīr' (not-seeing), are not simple but compound because they include a negation particle. The response affirms that such expressions are not true names (ism bi-l-ḥaqīqa) but rather compounds of a name and a particle. The particle itself carries the meaning of a simple name. Examples from logic: a definition (ḥadd) or description (rasm) are also compounds but serve a different purpose. A further objection questions why 'lā baṣīr' would not be a true name, given that it signifies something (like a blind person). The answer: if that were the case, it would fall under proper names (a'lām), which have their own issues discussed elsewhere.

Key Terms
اسم
مركب
أعلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is 'lā insān' not considered a true name (ism bi-l-ḥaqīqa)?
A) Because it is a proper name.
B) Because it is composed of a name and a particle of negation.
C) Because it refers to a nonexistent entity.
D) Because it is a definition (ḥadd).

Key Takeaway
A simple name (ism mufrad) cannot include a particle; negative compounds like 'lā insān' are compounds, not simple names.

The True Name: Condition of Convention
Author refutes claim that equivocal words can occur without denoting by convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish proper names and equivocal words from true names (asma' haqiqiyya) based on the condition of conventional placement (wad').

Core Idea
A true name is defined by being placed for a meaning; without placement, it is not a name.

This passage clarifies the essential condition for a word to be considered a true name: it must be placed (wad') for a specific meaning. The author addresses two apparent counterexamples: proper names and equivocal words. He argues that neither qualifies as a true name because they fail the placement condition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for any student of logic, as it prevents confusion about the nature of terms used in definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's core claim about names
Read the first sentence: 'الاسم هو الذي وضع بإزاء شيء' (The name is that which is placed in relation to something). This is the author's definition of a true name. Note that the key term is 'وضع' (placement), indicating that a name must be deliberately assigned to a meaning by a linguistic convention.
2
Examine the argument against proper names
The author states that proper names (أعلام) are not placed for a sign (علامة) in reality but are like pointing (كالإشارة). This means they do not convey a conceptual meaning; they simply refer to a specific individual. Therefore, they do not satisfy the definition of a true name.
The author's reasoning relies on the distinction between denoting a meaning and merely pointing to an entity.
3
Analyze the argument against equivocal words
The passage discusses words that denote by equivocation (تواطؤ) – words shared by multiple meanings. The author argues that such words are not names by nature; they only indicate meaning if they are placed for each meaning individually. Without a single conventional placement, they do not qualify as true names.
The text uses the example of sounds that indicate naturally (such as groans), which are not names because they lack conventional placement.
4
Summarize the author's criteria for a true name
A true name must (1) be a word (لفظ), (2) be placed (وضع) for a specific meaning (معنى), and (3) indicate that meaning by convention, not by nature. Proper names and equivocal words fail on the second or third criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال في الثاني : إنه في حيز المنع والاسم هو الذي وضع بإزاء شيء فإن ما يكون من الأعلام فذلك لا يكون اسما وضع لأجل العلامة بالحقيقة بل هو بمنزلة الإشارة كما مرّ ، وإلا لا يكون المهمل منها نحو المستعمل. وإن منع فذلك بحث لفظي وهو البحث في الاسم المحض بأنه ما هو، وأما ما يدل على الوجع فذلك وإن كان من الألفاظ ما لا يدلّ على الشيء إلا بالتواطؤ لا يكون اسما؛ إذ اللفظ الدال على الشيء لا يكون اسما بطبعه ولا يكون دالا على معناه بطبعه كذلك، كما في البعض من الأصوات الدالة التي تنبعث الطباع إلى استعمالها في الأحوال التي كيت وكيت بل يكون اسما بوضع الواضع ودالا فيكون دالا بطبعه (م) على الشيء بحس وذلك كذلك، وذلك اللفظ بل ما هو الأعم منه لا يكون كذلك، فلا يكون من الأسماء إذن.
And it is said regarding the second [objection]: It is in the realm of denial, and the name is that which is placed in relation to something. For what is among proper names, that is not a name placed for the sake of a sign in reality, but rather it is like pointing, as has passed; otherwise the unloaded [word] from them would not be like the used. And if [the objection] is denied, then that is a verbal inquiry, and it is the inquiry into the pure name, what it is. As for what denotes pain [or: as for what indicates by equivocation?], that—even if among words there are some that do not indicate a thing except by equivocation—it is not a name. For the word that indicates a thing is not a name by its nature, nor does it indicate its meaning by its nature likewise, as with some indicative sounds that natures are impelled to use in situations such and such; rather, it is a name by the placement of the placet and indicates, so it indicates by its nature the thing by sense, and that is such. That word, or what is more general than it, is not like that, so it is not among names then.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection concerning equivocal words (tawāṭu') and proper names. The author denies the claim that a word can denote by equivocation without being placed for a thing. The name is defined as something placed (wāḍiʿ) for a meaning. Proper names (aʿlām) are not true names placed for a sign; they are like pointing fingers, as previously explained. If one objects, it becomes a verbal dispute about pure names. The text then discusses words that indicate by equivocation (probably 'al-wajʿ' is a corruption; likely 'al-waḍʿ' or 'al-tawāṭu''). Such words are not names by nature; rather, they are names by convention. The author distinguishes between natural indications (like sounds) and conventional names. A word indicates its meaning by placement (waḍʿ), not by nature. Therefore, words denoting by equivocation are not names in the strict sense because they lack a single clear placement.

Key Terms
تواطؤ
أعلام
وضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are not placed for anything.
B) They are like pointing, not words placed for a meaning.
C) They are equivocal.
D) They are natural sounds.

Key Takeaway
The condition of conventional placement (wad') is what makes a word a true name; without it, a word is not a name in the strict sense.

Three Ways Words Indicate Time
Author classifies three ways words indicate time, refuting an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the three ways a given Arabic word indicates time (as named entity, as part of meaning, or via incidental form) and use this classification to evaluate objections about non-conjugated words.

Core Idea
Words indicate time in three distinct ways: as the thing named, as part of the meaning, or through conjugation.

This lesson unpacks the author's response to a claim that only conjugated verbs (like 'ḍaraba') can indicate time, while names like 'yawm' or 'ṣabūḥ' cannot. By classifying how words denote time, you will see that non-conjugated words can indeed carry temporal meaning — sometimes even more directly than verbs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage and note the three ways a word can indicate time: time as the named meaning (e.g., 'yawm' = day), time included in the definition (e.g., 'ṣabūḥ' = morning drink, defined with time), and time attached via incidental form (e.g., 'ḍaraba' / 'yaḍribu' through conjugation).
The categories are exhaustive: intrinsic, partially intrinsic, or extrinsic.
2
Match Arabic examples to each category
For each category, find the explicit examples given: 'al-yawm', 'al-ghad', 'al-zamān' for category 1; 'al-ṣabūḥ', 'al-ghasūq', 'al-taqaddum', 'al-ta'akhkhur' for category 2; 'ḍaraba, yaḍribu' and 'dhahaba, yadhhabu' for category 3.
Note that category 2 words have time embedded in their definition, even though they are not conjugated.
3
Distinguish intrinsic vs. incidental time indication
Categories 1 and 2 involve time as part of the word's core meaning (intrinsic). Category 3 has time indicated only by the morphological pattern (incidental). The objection fails because it assumed only category 3 (verbs) can indicate time, ignoring the first two.
This distinction is key to refuting the objection.
4
Apply the classification to the original objection
The objection claimed that non-conjugated words (mujarrada) cannot indicate time. Using the three categories, show that many non-conjugated words like 'yawm' or 'ṣabūḥ' do indicate time intrinsically. Thus the objection is invalid.
The author's conclusion: 'What we are dealing with, from time, is this time only' — i.e., time as commonly understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال في الثالث: إن ذلك لا يكون واردا؛ إذ اللفظ يدل على الزمان بوجوه ثلاثة؛ أحدها أن يكون الزمان مسمّى اللفظ كما في اليوم والغد والزمان ونحو ذلك، وثانيها أن يكون الزمان داخلا في المسمى كما في الصبوح والغسوق والتقدم والتأخر مثلا - فإن مسمى الكل يؤخذ في حده الزمان، وثالثها أن يكون الزمان خارجًا عن مسمى جوهر اللفظ لكن يلحقه فيقترن به اقترانا يدل عليه اللفظ بحسب الهيئة العارضة له؛ كما في قولنا «ضرب، يضرب» و «ذهب، يذهب» مثلا. فإن كلا منها لا يدل على الزمان إلا بحسب الهيئة العارضة له بالتصريف، وما نحن بصدده من الزمان فذلك هذا الزمان لا غير ولفظ التجرد عن الاقتران يشعر بأن المراد من الزمان هذا الزمان أيضًا.
And it is said regarding the third [objection]: That is not valid; for the word indicates time in three ways: one of them is that time is the named entity of the word, as in 'al-yawm' (the day), 'al-ghad' (tomorrow), 'al-zamān' (time) and the like. The second is that time is included in the named entity, as in 'al-ṣabūḥ' (morning drink), 'al-ghasūq' (dusk), 'al-taqaddum' (priority), 'al-ta'akhkhur' (posteriority) for example—for the named entity of each includes time in its definition. The third is that time is outside the named essence of the word but attaches to it, being associated with it in a way that the word indicates by virtue of the incidental form; as in our saying 'ḍaraba, yaḍribu' (he struck, he strikes) and 'dhahaba, yadhhabu' (he went, he goes) for example. For each of them does not indicate time except by virtue of the incidental form through conjugation. And what we are dealing with, from time, is this time only, and the word 'mujarrada' (unconjugated) suggests that what is meant by time is also this time.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage refutes the third objection, which claims that words like 'zamān', 'yawm', 'ghad', 'ṣabūḥ', 'ghasūq' are names even though they are not conjugated (mujarrada). The author explains that time is indicated in three ways: 1) Time is the named thing itself, as in 'yawm' (day) or 'ghad' (tomorrow). 2) Time is part of the meaning, as in 'ṣabūḥ' (morning drink) which inherently involves time in its definition. 3) Time is external but attached via conjugation, as in verbs like 'ḍaraba' (past tense) and 'yaḍribu' (present/future). The author clarifies that when the original objection spoke of 'mujarrada' (non-conjugated), it referred to the first two categories where time is intrinsic. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
زمان
مجردة
هيئة عارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Time is the named essence of the word.
B) Time is included in the definition of the word.
C) Time is indicated by the incidental morphological form through conjugation.
D) Time is not indicated at all.

Key Takeaway
Words indicate time in three ways: as the named meaning, as part of the meaning, or via incidental morphological form; non-conjugated words fall into the first two categories, so the objection fails.

5 min
Three Ways Words Indicate Time
Author refutes a division of words and explains three modes of time indication for 'ism'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three ways an utterance can indicate time and provide an Islamic Arabic example for each.

Core Idea
Time in language is not one; it can be the very meaning, part of the meaning, or externally attached via form.

In this passage, the author responds to a criticism by showing that utterances indicate time in three fundamentally different ways. Understanding these modes is essential for correctly applying the logical condition for being an 'ism' (name) versus a verb or particle. We'll walk through each mode with examples from Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context of the three modes
The author introduces the three modes after rejecting a claim that the third category of a previous division is invalid. Notice that he is defending the richness of time indication in Arabic words.
The previous division likely classified words into names, verbs, and particles.
2
Examine the first mode: time as the actual meaning
Here the word itself simply means a time. Examples given: 'today' (اليوم), 'tomorrow' (الغد), 'time' (الزمان). In these, the meaning of the word is a time period.
These words are still considered names because they do not indicate a specific tense (past/present/future).
3
Examine the second mode: time enters into the meaning
The word’s definition includes time as a component. Examples: 'morning draught' (الصبوح) – its meaning inherently involves morning time; similarly 'evening draught' (الغسوق), 'precedence' (التقدم), 'postponement' (التأخر). Their definitions require reference to time.
Again, these are names because the time included is not specifically past, present, or future.
4
Examine the third mode: time attached via morphological form
Here the core meaning does not involve time, but the morphological pattern (like verb conjugations) indicates time. Examples: 'he struck' (ضرب) indicates past, 'he strikes' (يضرب) indicates present/future. The time indication comes from the 'accidental form' (الهيئة العارضة) of the word.
These are verbs, not names, because they specify a tense. The author emphasizes that the time relevant to the 'ism' condition is specific tense (المعين).
5
Relate to the overall argument
The author uses these three modes to show that a previous objection fails: utterances can indicate time in ways that do not violate the definition of 'ism'. The next lesson will apply the precise condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك كذلك، وذلك اللفظ بل ما هو الأعم منه لا يكون كذلك، فلا يكون من الأسماء إذن فإن قلت: إذا لم يكن هو ولا مثله من الأسماء فلا تكون القسمة منحصرة فيما ذكرتم من الأقسام؛ وذلك لأنه لا يكون من الكلمات ولا من الأدوات أصلًا. فنقول : تلك القسمة في الألفاظ الدالة بالتواطؤ لا في الألفاظ الدالة على الإطلاق. ومن زعم بأن إيراد التواطؤ للتفرقة بين الألفاظ وأصوات البهائم فقد أخطأ؛ إذ اللفظ لا يتناول الصوت. وكيف يتناوله وإنه هو المادة للألفاظ ؟ ! نص عليه الشيخ في الشفاء : إن الصوت مادة لا جنس والمادة لا يحمل على الشيء المتحد من المادة والصورة إلا بطريق المجاز. والفرق بين المادة والجنس يعرف من بعد؛ وبهذا يظهر قول من يقول: «إنه يجب أن يقال بدل اللفظ الصوت». ويقال في الثالث: إن ذلك لا يكون واردا؛ إذ اللفظ يدل على الزمان بوجوه ثلاثة؛ أحدها أن يكون الزمان مسمّى اللفظ كما في اليوم والغد والزمان ونحو ذلك، وثانيها أن يكون الزمان داخلا في المسمى كما في الصبوح والغسوق والتقدم والتأخر مثلا - فإن مسمى الكل يؤخذ في حده الزمان، وثالثها أن يكون الزمان خارجًا عن مسمى جوهر اللفظ لكن يلحقه فيقترن به اقترانا يدل عليه اللفظ بحسب الهيئة العارضة له؛ كما في قولنا «ضرب، يضرب» و «ذهب، يذهب» مثلا. فإن كلا منها لا يدل على الزمان إلا بحسب الهيئة العارضة له بالتصريف، وما نحن بصدده من الزمان فذلك هذا الزمان لا غير ولفظ التجرد عن الاقتران يشعر بأن المراد من الزمان هذا الزمان أيضًا، فإن الشيء لا يقترن بالزمان إلا وأن يكون الزمان خارجًا عن نفسه. وقد يقال في التجرد إن المراد منه أنه لا يدل على الزمان دلالة المطابقة، أو لا يدل دلالة التضمن، أو لا يدل دلالة الالتزام؛ فإن كان الأول فلا يمكن أن يكون الزمان والوقت واليوم والغد وما هو بمثلها اسما، وإن كان الثاني فلا يمكن أن يكون الغسوق والصبوح وقبل وبعد اسمًا، وإن كان الثالث فلا يمكن أن يكون اسم الفاعل والمفعول، وقد كان كل واحد منها يدل بالالتزام على الزمان. لكنا نقول: الشرط في كون اللفظ اسما أن لا يدل على الزمان المعين من الأزمنة الثلاثة نحو الماضي والحال والاستقبال، وما ذكرتم من الألفاظ وإن كان يدل على الزمان فلا يكون دالة على الزمان المعين من الأزمنة الثلاثة.
And that is such, and that utterance, rather what is more general than it, is not such, so it is not among the names. If you say: When neither it nor its like is among the names, the division is not confined to the divisions you mentioned; because it is not at all among words or particles. We say: That division concerns utterances that indicate by mutual agreement, not utterances indicating absolutely. Whoever claims that introducing mutual agreement is to distinguish between utterances and animal sounds has erred; for utterance does not encompass sound. How could it encompass it when it is the matter for utterances? The Master stated in al-Shifa': 'Sound is matter, not genus, and matter is not predicated of a thing composed of matter and form except metaphorically.' The difference between matter and genus will be known later; and by this the statement of whoever says: 'One should say 'sound' instead of 'utterance'' becomes clear. And it is said concerning the third: That is not applicable, for utterance indicates time in three ways: first, time is the meaning of the utterance, as in 'today', 'tomorrow', 'time', and the like; second, time enters into the meaning, as in 'morning draught', 'evening draught', 'precedence', 'postponement', for example – for the meaning of each includes time in its definition; third, time is external to the essence of the utterance but attaches to it in a way that the utterance indicates it by the accidental form, as in our saying 'he struck', 'he strikes', and 'he went', 'he goes' for example. Each of them indicates time only by the accidental form due to conjugation. The time we are dealing with is this time only, and the expression 'abstraction from attachment' indicates that the intended time is also this time, for a thing does not attach to time unless time is external to itself. And it is said concerning 'abstraction' that its meaning is that it does not indicate time by correspondence, or does not indicate by inclusion, or does not indicate by implication. If the first, then 'time', 'moment', 'today', 'tomorrow', and the like cannot be names; if the second, then 'evening draught', 'morning draught', 'before', 'after' cannot be names; if the third, then the active and passive participles cannot be names, even though each of them indicates time by implication. But we say: The condition for an utterance being a name is that it does not indicate a specific time from among the three times – past, present, future – and the utterances you mentioned, although they indicate time, do not indicate a specific time from among the three times.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defends and refines the definition of 'ism' (name/noun) within a logical-grammatical context. It begins by addressing an objection: if the word under discussion is not a name nor similar to one, then a previous division of words is incomplete. The author responds that the division applies only to words that indicate by mutual agreement (tawātu'), not absolutely. He then criticizes a claim that introducing the concept of tawātu' is to distinguish human speech from animal sounds, arguing that 'lafẓ' (utterance) does not encompass sound (ṣawt), because sound is the matter (mādda) of utterances, not a genus (jins), as Ibn Sina said. This leads to a main point: the third category in a previous classification is invalid because utterance indicates time in three ways. The author enumerates these: (1) time is the very meaning (e.g., 'today'), (2) time is part of the meaning (e.g., 'morning draught'), (3) time is external but attached via morphological form (e.g., tenses of verbs). He clarifies that the relevant time is not any absolute time but specific time (past, present, future). He then examines a possible interpretation of 'abstraction from time' – whether it means no indication by correspondence, inclusion, or implication – and shows that under each interpretation, some obvious names would be excluded. The resolution is that the condition for being a name is not total absence of time indication, but rather not indicating a specific time among the three. Thus words like 'today' or 'active participle' are still names because they do not specify past/present/future.

Key Terms
اللفظ
التواطؤ
الزمان المعين
المادة
الهيئة العارضة
التصريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of the second mode where time enters into the meaning of the word?
A) 'الزمان' (time) – time is the meaning itself
B) 'ضرب' (he struck) – time via conjugation
C) 'الصبوح' (morning draught) – time is part of the definition
D) 'اليوم' (today) – time is the meaning itself

Key Takeaway
Utterances indicate time in three distinct ways, and only the third mode (time attached via conjugation) excludes a word from being an 'ism' because it specifies a tense.

5 min
The Condition for Being an 'Ism' (Name)
Author refutes a division of words and explains three modes of time indication for 'ism'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition for 'ism' (not indicating specific time) to determine whether a given Arabic word qualifies as a name or not.

Core Idea
A word is a name if it does not indicate a specific time (past, present, future), even if it indicates time in a general sense.

In the latter part of the passage, the author resolves an apparent contradiction: many words that seem to involve time are still considered names. The key is the precise condition that a name must not refer to a specific tense. We will dissect this definition and see how it applies to common Arabic words.

Guided Path
1
State the condition for 'ism'
The author says: 'The condition for an utterance being a name is that it does not indicate a specific time from among the three times – past, present, future.' Write this in your own words.
This is the differentia that separates names from verbs.
2
Test the condition on the three problematic interpretations
The author considers three interpretations of 'abstraction from time': (a) no indication by correspondence, (b) no indication by inclusion, (c) no indication by implication. Under each, some obvious names would be excluded. Work through each: under (a), 'time' itself would not be a name; under (b), 'morning draught' would not be a name; under (c), participles would not be a name.
This shows that the abstract condition cannot be that the word indicates no time at all.
3
Identify the correct differentia
The correct condition is: not indicating a specific time (المعين) from the three. 'Today' indicates time (present in a general sense) but not a specific tense; 'morning draught' includes time but not a specific one; a verb like 'he struck' indicates past specifically. Distinguish these.
The differentia is 'not indicating specific time'.
4
Apply the condition to various examples
Classify these as 'ism' or not: 'الزمان' (time), 'الغد' (tomorrow), 'الصبوح' (morning draught), 'ضرب' (he struck), 'اسم الفاعل' (active participle). Justify each based on the condition.
Answers: all except 'ضرب' are names because they do not specify past/present/future.
5
Understand the significance in logic and grammar
This definition of 'ism' from logic feeds into Arabic grammar (ism as noun) and is used in usul al-fiqh for understanding divine commands and classifications of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك كذلك، وذلك اللفظ بل ما هو الأعم منه لا يكون كذلك، فلا يكون من الأسماء إذن فإن قلت: إذا لم يكن هو ولا مثله من الأسماء فلا تكون القسمة منحصرة فيما ذكرتم من الأقسام؛ وذلك لأنه لا يكون من الكلمات ولا من الأدوات أصلًا. فنقول : تلك القسمة في الألفاظ الدالة بالتواطؤ لا في الألفاظ الدالة على الإطلاق. ومن زعم بأن إيراد التواطؤ للتفرقة بين الألفاظ وأصوات البهائم فقد أخطأ؛ إذ اللفظ لا يتناول الصوت. وكيف يتناوله وإنه هو المادة للألفاظ ؟ ! نص عليه الشيخ في الشفاء : إن الصوت مادة لا جنس والمادة لا يحمل على الشيء المتحد من المادة والصورة إلا بطريق المجاز. والفرق بين المادة والجنس يعرف من بعد؛ وبهذا يظهر قول من يقول: «إنه يجب أن يقال بدل اللفظ الصوت». ويقال في الثالث: إن ذلك لا يكون واردا؛ إذ اللفظ يدل على الزمان بوجوه ثلاثة؛ أحدها أن يكون الزمان مسمّى اللفظ كما في اليوم والغد والزمان ونحو ذلك، وثانيها أن يكون الزمان داخلا في المسمى كما في الصبوح والغسوق والتقدم والتأخر مثلا - فإن مسمى الكل يؤخذ في حده الزمان، وثالثها أن يكون الزمان خارجًا عن مسمى جوهر اللفظ لكن يلحقه فيقترن به اقترانا يدل عليه اللفظ بحسب الهيئة العارضة له؛ كما في قولنا «ضرب، يضرب» و «ذهب، يذهب» مثلا. فإن كلا منها لا يدل على الزمان إلا بحسب الهيئة العارضة له بالتصريف، وما نحن بصدده من الزمان فذلك هذا الزمان لا غير ولفظ التجرد عن الاقتران يشعر بأن المراد من الزمان هذا الزمان أيضًا، فإن الشيء لا يقترن بالزمان إلا وأن يكون الزمان خارجًا عن نفسه. وقد يقال في التجرد إن المراد منه أنه لا يدل على الزمان دلالة المطابقة، أو لا يدل دلالة التضمن، أو لا يدل دلالة الالتزام؛ فإن كان الأول فلا يمكن أن يكون الزمان والوقت واليوم والغد وما هو بمثلها اسما، وإن كان الثاني فلا يمكن أن يكون الغسوق والصبوح وقبل وبعد اسمًا، وإن كان الثالث فلا يمكن أن يكون اسم الفاعل والمفعول، وقد كان كل واحد منها يدل بالالتزام على الزمان. لكنا نقول: الشرط في كون اللفظ اسما أن لا يدل على الزمان المعين من الأزمنة الثلاثة نحو الماضي والحال والاستقبال، وما ذكرتم من الألفاظ وإن كان يدل على الزمان فلا يكون دالة على الزمان المعين من الأزمنة الثلاثة.
And that is such, and that utterance, rather what is more general than it, is not such, so it is not among the names. If you say: When neither it nor its like is among the names, the division is not confined to the divisions you mentioned; because it is not at all among words or particles. We say: That division concerns utterances that indicate by mutual agreement, not utterances indicating absolutely. Whoever claims that introducing mutual agreement is to distinguish between utterances and animal sounds has erred; for utterance does not encompass sound. How could it encompass it when it is the matter for utterances? The Master stated in al-Shifa': 'Sound is matter, not genus, and matter is not predicated of a thing composed of matter and form except metaphorically.' The difference between matter and genus will be known later; and by this the statement of whoever says: 'One should say 'sound' instead of 'utterance'' becomes clear. And it is said concerning the third: That is not applicable, for utterance indicates time in three ways: first, time is the meaning of the utterance, as in 'today', 'tomorrow', 'time', and the like; second, time enters into the meaning, as in 'morning draught', 'evening draught', 'precedence', 'postponement', for example – for the meaning of each includes time in its definition; third, time is external to the essence of the utterance but attaches to it in a way that the utterance indicates it by the accidental form, as in our saying 'he struck', 'he strikes', and 'he went', 'he goes' for example. Each of them indicates time only by the accidental form due to conjugation. The time we are dealing with is this time only, and the expression 'abstraction from attachment' indicates that the intended time is also this time, for a thing does not attach to time unless time is external to itself. And it is said concerning 'abstraction' that its meaning is that it does not indicate time by correspondence, or does not indicate by inclusion, or does not indicate by implication. If the first, then 'time', 'moment', 'today', 'tomorrow', and the like cannot be names; if the second, then 'evening draught', 'morning draught', 'before', 'after' cannot be names; if the third, then the active and passive participles cannot be names, even though each of them indicates time by implication. But we say: The condition for an utterance being a name is that it does not indicate a specific time from among the three times – past, present, future – and the utterances you mentioned, although they indicate time, do not indicate a specific time from among the three times.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defends and refines the definition of 'ism' (name/noun) within a logical-grammatical context. It begins by addressing an objection: if the word under discussion is not a name nor similar to one, then a previous division of words is incomplete. The author responds that the division applies only to words that indicate by mutual agreement (tawātu'), not absolutely. He then criticizes a claim that introducing the concept of tawātu' is to distinguish human speech from animal sounds, arguing that 'lafẓ' (utterance) does not encompass sound (ṣawt), because sound is the matter (mādda) of utterances, not a genus (jins), as Ibn Sina said. This leads to a main point: the third category in a previous classification is invalid because utterance indicates time in three ways. The author enumerates these: (1) time is the very meaning (e.g., 'today'), (2) time is part of the meaning (e.g., 'morning draught'), (3) time is external but attached via morphological form (e.g., tenses of verbs). He clarifies that the relevant time is not any absolute time but specific time (past, present, future). He then examines a possible interpretation of 'abstraction from time' – whether it means no indication by correspondence, inclusion, or implication – and shows that under each interpretation, some obvious names would be excluded. The resolution is that the condition for being a name is not total absence of time indication, but rather not indicating a specific time among the three. Thus words like 'today' or 'active participle' are still names because they do not specify past/present/future.

Key Takeaway
A word is a name if it does not specify a particular tense, even if it refers to time in a general or inherent way.

Defining 'Ism': Why Conceptual Independence Fails
Author critiques a definition based on conceptual independence and offers an alternative based on inflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate definitions of 'ism' using the criterion of conceptual independence and contrast them with the grammatical criterion of inflection.

Core Idea
A good definition must exclude what it should; 'independent in conceptuality' fails because particles also have independent concepts.

In this passage, the author examines a common definition of 'ism' (name/noun) that relies on 'independence in conceptuality'. He shows that this definition inadvertently includes particles, which are traditionally considered a separate category. He then proposes a more precise definition based on inflection (i'rāb). Understanding why this definition fails sharpens our skill of evaluating definitions in Islamic logic and grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique.
Locate the definition presented: 'The name is a word indicating what is independent in conceptuality, without indicating the time of that meaning.' This is the target.
2
Understand the main objection: particles are independent too.
The author argues that particles (ḥurūf) like 'fī' (in) have independent concepts; otherwise we could not form compounds with them. Therefore the definition wrongly includes particles.
This shows the definition is too broad.
3
Follow the reductio argument.
Even if we assume particles are non-independent, accepting that judgment itself is an act of independence, leading to contradiction. This logical move exposes the problem with the notion of 'independence'.
This is a technical argument against the definition.
4
Examine the counterexample of relative pronouns and demonstratives.
Words like 'alladhī' (which) have non-independent concepts but are considered names by most grammarians. This shows the definition is also too narrow.
The definition fails both ways.
5
Recognize the alternative definition based on inflection.
The author presents a practical definition: 'a word that deserves inflection in its first position.' This uses a formal grammatical property (i'rāb) that uniquely applies to nouns.
This definition reliably distinguishes nouns from particles and verbs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الاسم من جملة ما يعرف بالتعريفات المختلفة، كما قيل: «الاسم كلمة دالة على ما هو المستقل في المفهومية من غير أن يدل على زمان ذلك المعنى؛ وفيه من الكلام فإن الحروف كلها كذلك لما أن المفهوم من الحرف أي حرف كان مستقلاً في المفهومية، وإلا لا يمكن أن تكون مفهومات الحروف متصورة يمكن تركيب منها ومن غيرها. ولأنه إذا لم يكن مستقلا في المفهومية كان مستقلًا لقبول هذا الحكم وهو كونه غير مستقل، والاستقلال لقبول الاستقلالية استقلال فيكون مستقلا في التصور؛ ولو كان مستقلا فلا يصح أن يقال إنه غير مستقل، وإن سلمنا بأنه غير مستقل لكن اللفظ «الذي» وما يجرى مجراه من الموصولات والمضمرات والمنبهات مفهوماتها غير مستقل، وإن كانت الجملة بجميع أفرادها من الأسماء. وإن قيل هذا في حيز المنع لما أنها لا تكون من الأسماء عند البعض من أهل الكوفة، فنقول: كلامنا في هذا الباب مع الغير، وقد قيل في تعريف الاسم أيضًا: إنه كلمة تستحق الإعراب في أول موضوعها من الرسوم العائدة كذلك.
Then the name is among things known by various definitions, as it was said: 'The name is a word indicating what is independent in conceptuality, without indicating the time of that meaning.' But there is discussion in this: for particles are all like that, because the concept of a particle, whichever particle it is, is independent in conceptuality; otherwise the concepts of particles could not be conceived such that composition from them and from others is possible. And because if it were not independent in conceptuality, it would be independent in accepting this judgment, i.e., its being non-independent; and independence for accepting independence is independence, so it would be independent in conception. And if it were independent, it would not be correct to say that it is non-independent. And if we concede that it is non-independent, the word 'which' and what runs its course from relative pronouns, pronouns, and demonstratives have non-independent concepts, even though the whole set of their individuals are among names. And if it is said that this is in the realm of prevention because they are not among names according to some of the Kufans, we say: Our discussion in this chapter is with others. And it was also said in the definition of the name: 'It is a word that deserves inflection in its first position,' from such definitions that recur likewise.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage critiques a specific definition of 'ism' (name/noun) and offers an alternative. The author begins by noting that the name is known by various definitions. He then presents one definition: 'The name is a word indicating what is independent in conceptuality, without indicating the time of that meaning.' He immediately criticizes it, arguing that particles (ḥurūf) also have independent concepts; otherwise we could not form compounds using them. He then presents a reductio: if a particle's concept were not independent, it would still be independent in accepting the judgment of non-independence, leading to a contradiction. Even if we accept non-independence, relative pronouns, pronouns, and demonstratives have non-independent concepts yet are considered names. The objection that Kufans exclude these from being names is dismissed because the discussion is with others (presumably Basrans and the mainstream). Finally, the author mentions another definition: a name is a word that deserves inflection (i‘rāb) in its first position, which is a formal grammatical criterion. This practical definition avoids the problematic notion of 'independence in conceptuality'.

Key Terms
المستقل في المفهومية
الحروف
الإعراب

D) Because it applies only to verbs

Key Takeaway
The definition of 'ism' based on conceptual independence fails because it includes particles; a more effective criterion is the grammatical property of inflection (i'rāb).

Derived Nouns: Material, Form, and Time
Author explains how derived nouns indicate time via material and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a derived noun into its material (madda) and form (sura) and identify the temporal meaning contributed by the form.

Core Idea
A derived noun carries both a root meaning and a temporal stamp — material gives meaning, form gives time.

This passage begins by examining how derived nouns (al-asma' al-mushtaqqa) indicate time. The author explains that these nouns are composed of a material (the root consonants) and a form (the morphological pattern). The material conveys the core meaning, while the form adds additional senses such as the agent or the time (present, past, future). This analysis is crucial for understanding how words in Arabic (and other languages) encode temporal information, a skill needed in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh where verb tenses carry legal implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify a derived noun
Select a derived noun from Arabic, e.g., 'كاتب' (writer) or 'يضرِب' (he hits). Note that it is built from a root.
Derived nouns include verbs and participles.
2
Extract the material (madda)
Find the root consonants that carry the core meaning, independent of time. For 'كاتب', the material is 'كتب' (to write).
The material is the unchanging semantic core.
3
Identify the form (sura)
Determine the morphological pattern that adds extra meaning. For 'كاتب', the pattern is 'فاعل' indicating the doer of the action.
The form may also indicate tense, mood, or voice.
4
Determine the temporal indication
For verbs, the form indicates a specific time (past, present, future). For 'يكتب' (yaktubu), the form 'يفعل' indicates present/future tense. For 'كتب' (kataba), the form 'فعل' indicates past tense.
Participles (like 'كاتب') indicate a state without explicit time; the passage focuses on verbs.
5
Confirm the definition
The passage states that derived nouns indicate 'one meaning according to the material, and another meaning according to the form... and upon time as previously.' Verify that your analysis matches this claim.
The temporal meaning is a key differentiator for derived nouns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الدلالة بين أن يدل هذا على الحاضر وذلك على الغائب. وعلى هذا في الأسماء المشتقة؛ فإنها بحسب المادة تدل على معنى، وبحسب الصورة على معنى آخر نحو الموضوع الغير المعين وعلى الزمان كما مر . لكنا نقول أولا هب أنها مركبة في اللغة العربية وإن كانت بسيطة في الحقيقة لكن لا اعتبار في هذه الصناعة أن تنظر في لغة دون لغة، ربما يتفق أن يوجد للمعنى المركب لفظ مفرد في لغة ولا يوجد في لغة أخرى كما في الأذان مثلا؛ فإنه لا يوجد في اللغة الفارسية لفظ مفرد بإزائه، بل الاعتبار فيها أن ينظر ويعلم أن من الممكن أن يوجد لفظ دالّ على معنى وعلى زمان ذلك المعنى من الأزمنة الثلاثة، فلو وجدت هي لكانت مفردة موجودة وإلا فلا.
Regarding indication: either this indicates the present and that indicates the absent. And accordingly for derived nouns: they indicate one meaning according to the material, and another meaning according to the form, such as the unspecified subject and upon time as previously. But we first say: suppose they are compound in the Arabic language even though they are simple in reality, but in this discipline there is no consideration to look in one language rather than another. It may happen that a compound meaning has a single word in one language and does not exist in another language, as in the case of 'adhan' for example; for there is no single word in Persian corresponding to it. Rather, the consideration is to look and know that it is possible that there is a word that indicates a meaning and the time of that meaning from the three times. If such words exist, they would be simple and existent; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage discusses the semantics of derived nouns (al-asma' al-mushtaqqa) in relation to time. The author begins by stating that derived nouns indicate both a meaning and a time (present, past, future) through their material (madda) and form (sura). He then makes a methodological point: even if such words are considered compound in Arabic (because they contain a subject and time), in logic we do not restrict ourselves to a particular language. A compound meaning might be expressed by a single word in one language (e.g., 'adhan' in Arabic) and not in another (e.g., Persian). Therefore, the logician's task is to consider the possibility of a word that can indicate both meaning and time; if it exists, it is considered a simple (mufrad) word in that language, otherwise not. This is a 'definitional' passage clarifying the notion of derived nouns and the scope of logical analysis.

Key Terms
الأسماء المشتقة
المادة
الصورة

Key Takeaway
Derived nouns are built from a material giving the core meaning and a form adding secondary meanings like time and agent; this analysis is foundational for understanding how language encodes temporal and relational information in Islamic texts.

Logic Transcends Language: The Adhan Example
Author argues logic is not bound to any specific language, using adhan example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue why the discipline of logic must consider possibilities across languages rather than being tied to a specific language, using the example of 'adhan'.

Core Idea
Logic studies what is possible in any language, not what happens to exist in one.

After explaining how derived nouns indicate time, the author makes an important methodological point: logic (this discipline) is not concerned with any particular language. A compound meaning may be expressed as a single word in one language (e.g., 'adhan' in Arabic) but not in another (e.g., Persian). Therefore, the logician's job is to consider the possibility of such a word existing, not whether it actually exists in a given language. This principle protects logic from becoming language-specific and ensures its universality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The author claims: 'there is no consideration in this discipline to look in one language rather than another.' This means logic is language-independent.
The claim is methodological, not empirical.
2
Find the supporting evidence
The author uses the example of 'adhan': in Arabic it is a single word for the call to prayer; in Persian, there is no single word equivalent.
This shows that a compound concept can be lexicalized differently across languages.
3
Understand the reasoning
If logic were tied to the vocabulary of a specific language, then a compound concept encoded as a simple word in that language would be treated as simple, but in another language it would be compound. Logic would then give different analyses for the same concept, which is inconsistent.
The core of logic is the structure of reasoning, not the accidents of lexicalization.
4
Extract the positive principle
The author states: 'the consideration is to look and know that it is possible that there is a word that indicates a meaning and the time of that meaning.' So logic examines possibilities, not actualities.
This aligns with the nature of logic as a formal science.
5
Apply to the derived noun discussion
In the earlier part, the author said derived nouns are compound in Arabic but simple in reality. The language-independence principle justifies focusing on the logical (real) simplicity rather than the linguistic (Arabic) compoundness.
This resolves the apparent tension between Arabic grammar and logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الدلالة بين أن يدل هذا على الحاضر وذلك على الغائب. وعلى هذا في الأسماء المشتقة؛ فإنها بحسب المادة تدل على معنى، وبحسب الصورة على معنى آخر نحو الموضوع الغير المعين وعلى الزمان كما مر . لكنا نقول أولا هب أنها مركبة في اللغة العربية وإن كانت بسيطة في الحقيقة لكن لا اعتبار في هذه الصناعة أن تنظر في لغة دون لغة، ربما يتفق أن يوجد للمعنى المركب لفظ مفرد في لغة ولا يوجد في لغة أخرى كما في الأذان مثلا؛ فإنه لا يوجد في اللغة الفارسية لفظ مفرد بإزائه، بل الاعتبار فيها أن ينظر ويعلم أن من الممكن أن يوجد لفظ دالّ على معنى وعلى زمان ذلك المعنى من الأزمنة الثلاثة، فلو وجدت هي لكانت مفردة موجودة وإلا فلا.
Regarding indication: either this indicates the present and that indicates the absent. And accordingly for derived nouns: they indicate one meaning according to the material, and another meaning according to the form, such as the unspecified subject and upon time as previously. But we first say: suppose they are compound in the Arabic language even though they are simple in reality, but in this discipline there is no consideration to look in one language rather than another. It may happen that a compound meaning has a single word in one language and does not exist in another language, as in the case of 'adhan' for example; for there is no single word in Persian corresponding to it. Rather, the consideration is to look and know that it is possible that there is a word that indicates a meaning and the time of that meaning from the three times. If such words exist, they would be simple and existent; otherwise not.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage discusses the semantics of derived nouns (al-asma' al-mushtaqqa) in relation to time. The author begins by stating that derived nouns indicate both a meaning and a time (present, past, future) through their material (madda) and form (sura). He then makes a methodological point: even if such words are considered compound in Arabic (because they contain a subject and time), in logic we do not restrict ourselves to a particular language. A compound meaning might be expressed by a single word in one language (e.g., 'adhan' in Arabic) and not in another (e.g., Persian). Therefore, the logician's task is to consider the possibility of a word that can indicate both meaning and time; if it exists, it is considered a simple (mufrad) word in that language, otherwise not. This is a 'definitional' passage clarifying the notion of derived nouns and the scope of logical analysis.

Key Terms
الأسماء المشتقة
المادة
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) To claim that 'adhan' is not a derived noun

Key Takeaway
Logic transcends language: it analyzes the structure of meanings and possibilities, not the accidents of any particular vocabulary.

Essential and Accidental Terms in Islamic Logic
Author defines essential and accidental based on existential priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and accidental universal terms based on the criterion of existential priority as defined in the passage.

Core Idea
Essential terms indicate what must exist first; accidental terms indicate what is external.

This passage introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic logic: how to classify universal terms based on their relation to a thing's essence. The author uses existential priority—what must be present before the thing's essence can be constituted—as the decisive criterion. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing definitions, since only essential terms reveal the quiddity (māhiyya) of a thing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of universal terms
According to the passage, universal terms either indicate the true essence (haqīqa) of a thing or not. The first category is essential (dhātī), the second accidental (ʿaraḍī).
Focus on the phrase 'indicates the true essence' as the initial split.
2
Extract the criterion for essential terms
Essential terms are those that indicate what must be prior in existence (mutaqaddim fī al-wujūd) to the thing's essence, such that by their composition the essence comes to be. This existential priority is the key test.
The author stresses that essential terms are necessary for constituting the essence, not just accompanying it.
3
Determine the definition of accidental terms
Any term that does not indicate such a prior constituent—whether the property is necessary (lāzim) or not—is accidental. Accidental terms point to what is external to the essence.
Note that even necessary accidents like 'laughing' for humans are still accidental in this framework.
4
Apply the criterion to a concrete example
Test the term 'animal' (ḥayawān) for 'human' (insān). Can 'human' exist without 'animal'? No—animality must precede humanity. So 'animal' is essential. Test 'laughing' (ḍaḥik) for 'human'—it does not precede the essence, so it is accidental.
This example illustrates how the criterion works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوده فيما كان من الألفاظ الكلية يدل على حقيقة ذات الشيء. فذلك هو الدال على الماهية، وما لم يكن كذلك فلا يكون دالا على الماهية، فما كان يدل على ما لا بد من أن يكون متقدما في الوجود على ذات الشيء حتى يكون بالتئامه مع غيره ذات الشيء. فذلك ينبغي أن يقال له اللفظ الذاتي وما لا يدل عليه بل يدل على ما لا يكون كذلك، وذلك هو الخارج عن الذات لازما كان أو غير لازم؛ فإنما يقال له لفظ عرضي ولمعناه معنى عرضي.
Its existence, regarding what is among the universal terms, indicates the true essence of the thing. That is the indicator of the quiddity, and what is not like that is not an indicator of the quiddity. So what indicates what must be prior in existence to the essence of the thing so that by its composition with others the essence of the thing comes to be, that should be called the essential term. And what does not indicate that but indicates what is not like that, and that is what is external to the essence, whether necessary or not necessary, then it is called an accidental term and its meaning is an accidental meaning.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage gives the initial definition of essential (ذاتي) and accidental (عرضي) terms. The author establishes that universal terms indicate either the true reality of a thing (ما هو) or not. A term is essential if it signifies what must exist prior to the thing's essence, such that by its combination with other essentials the thing's essence is constituted. For example, 'animal' is essential for 'human' because animality must precede humanity. Any term that does not indicate such a prerequisite, but rather something external to the essence—whether necessary in existence (like 'laughing' for humans) or not—is called accidental. The definition hinges on existential priority, not mere concomitance.

عرضي

Key Takeaway
Essential terms are those without which the essence cannot be constituted; they must be prior in existence to the thing's quiddity.

Does the Essential Term Include the Essence Indicator?
Author investigates whether an essential term must encompass the term indicating the quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the essential term in Islamic logic is defined as what answers 'what is it?' rather than any term indicating the quiddity itself.

Core Idea
The essential term indicates a part of the quiddity, not the quiddity itself.

This passage probes a subtle nuance: if an essential term expresses a relation to the essence, how can the term for the essence itself be essential? The author works through this paradox to refine the definition of 'essential term' as what is said in answer to 'what is it?'—i.e., the genus or differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The author asks whether an essential term must include the term that indicates the quiddity. What does 'essential term' mean here? Note that it is a term with a relation to the essence.
2
Understand the puzzle
If 'human' is essential for a human, then 'human' relates to the essence, but the essence cannot relate to itself. So maybe 'human' is not essential, but only its parts (animal, rational) are.
The essence cannot be related to itself, so the term indicating the quiddity may not be essential.
3
Consider the alternative
If 'human' is essential for a specific person, it could be essential either to the quiddity (which is again 'human') or to the individuated whole. If the latter, even accidents like 'where is so-and-so?' could be essential.
This shows the implication of defining essentiality relative to the individuated whole.
4
See the resolution
These reflections lead to the conclusion that the essential term is what is said in answer to 'what is it?'—i.e., the differentia or genus given in a definition.
The essential term answers ma huwa, not the quiddity term itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا موضع نظر أنه هل يجب أن يكون معنى اللفظ الذاتي مشتملا على معنى اللفظ الدال على الماهية اشتمال العام على الخاص أو لا يكون. فإن قولنا لفظ ذاتي يدل على لفظ لمعناه نسبة إلى ذات الشيء، وذات الشيء لا يكون منسوبا إلى ذات الشيء؛ بل ينسب إلى الشيء غيره لا محالة. فبالحري أيضا أن يظن أن لفظ الذاتي يشتمل على المعاني التي تقوم الماهية، فلا يكون اللفظ الدال على الماهية ذاتيا؛ فلا يكون الإنسان ذاتيا للإنسان، لكن الذاتي هو الحيوان أو الناطق وإذا لم يكن الإنسان ذاتيا للإنسان بما هو إنسان بل لشخص شخص لم يخل إما أن يكون نسبته بالذاتية إلى حقيقة ماهية الشخص - وذلك هي الإنسان أيضا - أو إلى الجملة التي بها يتشخص فيكون ليس هو بكماله بل جزء ما هو منه من حيث هو جملة. فحينئذ يعرض أن لا يكون الحيوان والناطق والإنسان وما يجري مجراها قد يكون ذاتيا لشخص شخص فقط، بل الأمور العرضية أيضا مثل أين فلان؟ وما يجري مجراه قد يكون ذاتية؛ لأنها أجزاء مقومة للجملة، فهذه الأفكار تدعو إلى أن يكون الذاتي مشتملا على المقول في جواب ما هو؟
Then here is a point of investigation: whether the meaning of the essential term must include the meaning of the term indicating the quiddity, as the general includes the specific, or not. For our saying 'essential term' indicates a term whose meaning has a relation to the essence of the thing, and the essence of the thing is not related to itself; rather it is necessarily related to something else. So it is also plausible to think that the term 'essential' includes the meanings that constitute the quiddity, so the term indicating the quiddity would not be essential; thus 'human' would not be essential for human, but the essential would be 'animal' or 'rational'. And if 'human' is not essential for human qua human but for a specific person, it is either that its relation by essentiality is to the reality of the person's quiddity—and that is also 'human'—or to the whole by which it is individuated, so it is not in its entirety but a part of what it is from the perspective of it being a whole. Then it may happen that animal, rational, human and the like may be essential only for a specific person, indeed accidental things like 'where is so-and-so?' and the like may be essential because they are constitutive parts of the whole. These thoughts lead to the conclusion that the essential term includes what is said in answer to 'what is it?'.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is an investigation (موضع نظر) into whether the essential term must encompass the term that indicates the quiddity (e.g., 'human' itself). The author begins by noting that if 'human' is essential for human, the term 'essential' implies a relation to the essence, but the essence cannot relate to itself. Thus perhaps only parts like 'animal' or 'rational' are truly essential. If 'human' is essential for a specific person, it might be essential either to the quiddity of that person (which is again 'human') or to the individuated whole. In the latter case, even accidents like 'where is so-and-so' could be considered essential because they constitute the person's identity. These reflections drive the conclusion that the essential term is what answers 'what is it?', i.e., the genus or differentia used in a definition.

دال على الماهية
ما هو

Key Takeaway
The essential term is not the term for the quiddity itself, but the term that answers 'what is it?'—i.e., the genus or differentia in a definition.

The Imagination Criterion for Essential vs Accidental
Author provides the logical criterion: essential if removal in imagination removes the thing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a predicate is essential or accidental using the imagination criterion, and distinguish between essential that indicates quiddity and essential that does not.

Core Idea
An essential predicate is one you cannot imagine removing without destroying the subject.

The terms 'essential' (dhati) and 'accidental' (aradi) in logic have precise technical meanings beyond their ordinary language. The author provides a clear criterion based on what happens when you mentally remove a universal term from its particulars. This lesson guides you through applying that test to examples, helping you distinguish between the essential components of a thing and its mere accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate and subject
For any universal term (like 'animal', 'rational', 'laughter', 'blackness'), recognize the subject to which it is attributed. For example, 'human' is the subject of these predicates.
2
Apply the imagination test
Try to imagine the predicate not existing while the subject still exists (in the mind). If you cannot—meaning the subject's essence would be negated—then the predicate is essential. If you can imagine the subject without the predicate, it is accidental.
The test uses imagination (al-wahm), not actual existence. Some accidents are inseparable in reality but still imaginably removed.
3
Distinguish subtypes of essential
Some essential terms indicate the quiddity itself (like 'animal' for human), while others are necessary concomitants that are not part of the quiddity (like 'laughter' for humans). Both pass the imagination test: removing them destroys the subject.
This distinction is important for understanding definition (hadd) vs description (rasm).
4
Classify as accidental otherwise
If the predicate can be imagined removed without destroying the subject, it is accidental. This includes both predicates that are removable in existence (like sitting) and those that are not (like the blackness of a black person, which cannot be removed in existence but can be imagined removed).
The text emphasizes that 'blackness' for a black person is accidental, because if you imagine a black person without blackness, the person still exists mentally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن قولنا «ذاتي» وإن كان بحسب قانون اللغة يدل على هذا المعنى النسبي فإنه بحسب اصطلاح أهل المنطق يدل على معنى آخر. وذلك أن اللفظ الكلي إذا دل على معنى بنسبة إلى جزئياته نسبة إذا توهمتها غير موجودة بحسب أن لا يكون ذات ذلك الشيء من الجزئيات موجودة فيكون رفعها موجبا رفع ذات ذلك الشيء، سواء كان هذا المرفوع حقيقة ذاته أو كان مما يفتقر إلى حقيقة ذاته في التقوم فإنه يقال له ذاتي. وما لا يكون كذلك كان يصح في الوجود أو في التوهم أن يكون ذلك الشيء حاصلا مع رفعه، أو كان لا يصح في الوجود، لكن ليس رفعه موجبا رفع ذلك الشيء فهو عرضي، فالمرتفع في الوجود هو كالقيام والقعود مثلا، والمرتفع في الوهم دون الوجود؛ فذلك مثل سواد الزنجي. والذي لا يكون رفعه موجبا فذلك مثل كون الإنسان معرضا للتعجب وهو كونه ضحكا بالطبع؛ فإنه لا يجوز أن يرفع عن الإنسان في الوجود، فإن أوهم مرفوعا كانت الإنسانية مرفوعة لأن رفعه سبب رفع الإنسانية بل لأنه لا يرفع إلا وأن تكون الإنسانية أو لا مرفوعة، وكانت الإنسانية سببا لثبوته في الحقيقة. وقد ظهرت التفرقة بين نسبة الحيوان والناطق والإنسان أيضا إلى الأشخاص وبين الأعراض إليها، فإن السببية الأولى إذا رفعتها بحيث رفع ذلك الشخص على خلاف الثانية. وإذا كان الأمر على هذه الجملة فالذاتي مشتمل على ما يدل على الماهية، وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون اللفظ المفرد الكلي منه ذاتي يدل على الماهية، ومنه ذاتي لا يدل على الماهية، ومنه عرضي. وقد قيل في التمييز بين الذاتي والعرضي إن الذاتي لا يصح توهمه مرفوعا مع بقاء الشيء، والعرضي يصح توهمه مرفوعا مع بقاء الشيء، وإنه من جملة ما يجب أن يذكر الذي مر ذكره، وهو أن المعنى الكلي قد يكون له أوصاف يفتقر إليها أولا حتى يحصل ذلك ويكون له أوصاف أخرى يلزمه ويتبعه. أما الأول فلا يمكن أن يحصل معقولا مع سلب تلك الأوصاف منه، وذلك أن للأشياء ماهيات تلك الماهيات قد تكون موجودة في الأعيان وقد تكون موجودة في الأذهان.
But our saying 'essential', although according to the law of language indicates this relative meaning, yet according to the convention of logicians it indicates another meaning. That is, the universal term, when it indicates a meaning in relation to its particulars—a relation such that if you imagine them nonexistent in the sense that the essence of that thing among the particulars is not existent, then its removal necessitates the removal of the essence of that thing, whether that which is removed is the true essence of it or is something that depends on its true essence in subsistence—then it is called essential. And what is not like that, either it is possible in existence or in imagination that the thing obtains while it is removed, or it is not possible in existence but its removal does not necessitate the removal of that thing, then it is accidental. So that which is removed in existence is like standing and sitting, for example; and that which is removed in imagination but not in existence is like the blackness of a black person. And that whose removal does not necessitate (removal) is like the human being being disposed to wonder, which is being naturally prone to laughter; for it is not possible to remove it from human in existence, for if it is imagined removed, humanity would be removed—not because its removal is a cause of humanity's removal, but because it is not removed unless humanity is already removed, and humanity is actually the cause of its subsistence. And the distinction has become clear between the relation of animal, rational, and human also to individuals and the relation of accidents to them, for the first causation when you remove it in such a way that it removes that individual, contrary to the second. And if the matter is on this summary, then the essential includes what indicates the quiddity, and then it follows that the singular universal term is sometimes essential indicating the quiddity, sometimes essential not indicating the quiddity, and sometimes accidental. And it has been said in distinguishing between essential and accidental that the essential cannot be imagined removed while the thing remains, and the accidental can be imagined removed while the thing remains; and it is among what must be mentioned that which has passed, namely that the universal meaning may have attributes which it depends on first until it obtains, and it may have other attributes that follow it and are consequent. As for the first, it cannot be conceived while those attributes are negated from it, and that is because things have quiddities; those quiddities may exist in external objects and may exist in minds.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage provides the technical logical definition of essential and accidental. The author contrasts the linguistic meaning with the technical meaning used by logicians. The key criterion: imagine whether removing a universal predicate would necessitate the removal of the subject. If removing it would destroy the subject (even mentally), then the predicate is essential; otherwise it is accidental. Examples are given: standing and sitting can be removed without destroying the subject (accidental). Blackness of a black person cannot be removed in existence but can be imagined removed, so it is accidental. The disposition to laughter (ضحك) is essential to humans because if you imagine a human without it, humanity itself is removed—not because laughter causes humanity, but because it is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of humanity. The author then summarizes that essential terms include both those that indicate the quiddity (like 'animal') and those that do not (like 'laughter'), and accidental terms are those whose removal does not necessitate removal. Finally, an additional note distinguishes attributes that are prerequisites for the concept (cannot be negated) from attributes that follow it.

Key Terms
ذاتي
عرضي
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) الناطق (the rational)
B) الضحك (laughter)
C) السواد (blackness)
D) الحيوان (the animal)

Key Takeaway
The imagination test is the definitive criterion: if removing a predicate in thought eliminates the subject, it is essential; otherwise it is accidental.
