Essential vs Accidental Knowledge of Essence
Author distinguishes knowing essence by itself from knowing it by its accompaniments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential knowledge (bi dhatiha) from accidental knowledge (bi tawabi'iha) and classify given Islamic science examples into the appropriate type.

Core Idea
Two paths to knowing a thing: its essence or its accompaniments.

The author opens by noting that we can know something either by its very essence or by the things that accompany it. This distinction is foundational for definitions: knowing the essence yields an essential definition (hadd), while knowing by accompaniments yields a descriptive definition (rasm). As a student of Islamic logic, you must recognise which type of knowledge you have.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
Find the Arabic phrases 'بحسب ذاتها' (by its own self) and 'بحسب ما يتعلق بها' (by what is attached to it). These contrast essential and accidental knowledge.
2
Analyse the examples
Heat known by sense is essential knowledge because we grasp its reality. The world's temporal origination known by proofs is accidental—we know a property (being originated) but not the essence of 'the originated' itself.
The author says essential knowledge is 'كمال درجات العلم' (perfection of knowledge) according to most.
3
Correlate with definition types
Essential knowledge leads to hadd (definition by genus and differentia). Accidental knowledge leads to rasm (description by properties). The passage promises full treatment later.
4
Apply the distinction to Islamic sciences
Given an Islamic statement, decide whether it gives essential knowledge (e.g., 'Wine is an intoxicant that clouds the mind' is accidental, not essential, because it gives a property).
Essential knowledge tells you what something is; accidental tells you something about it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العلم بالماهية بسيطة كانت الماهية أو مركبة قد يكون بحسب ذاتها وقد لا يكون بل يكون بحسب ما يتعلق بها. فتكون معلومة إذن: إما بذاتها وإما بتوابعها وصفاتها. فإنا إذا علمنا الحرارة بالحس مثلا، فذلك العلم بالشيء من حيث حقيقته المخصوصة وماهيته المعينة وهو كمال درجات العلم عند الأكثر . فأما إذا علمنا أن العالم حادث بالدلائل الدالة عليه فلا نعلم أن المحدث ما هو وإن كان معلوماً بأن العالم حادث بإحداثه. ولو كان كذلك لكان المطلوب في السؤال عن الماهية البسيطة: إما هو الأول منهما كما إذا سئل عن الحرارة فيقال ما يدركه الإنسان بحس اللمس عند مماسة جرم النار وإما السؤال الثاني كما إذا سئل عن المحدث فيقال المحدث هو الذي لأجله انتقل الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود. وعلى هذا في المركب؛ إذ المطلوب إما هو الأول منهما أو الثاني، وذلك يعرف بتمامه في التعريفات الحدية والرسمية من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى.
Then knowledge of the essence (mahiya), whether the essence is simple or composite, may be in accordance with its own self or may not be, but rather in accordance with what is attached to it. So it is known then: either by itself or by its appendages and attributes. For if we know heat by sense, for example, that is knowledge of the thing in terms of its specific reality and determinate essence, and it is the perfection of degrees of knowledge according to most. As for when we know that the world is temporally originated (hadith) by proofs indicating it, we do not know what the originated thing is, even though it is known that the world is originated by its origination. And if it were otherwise, then what is sought in the question about the simple essence would be either the first of the two, as when asked about heat, one says: that which a human perceives by the sense of touch when contacting the body of fire; or the second question, as when asked about the originated thing, one says: the originated is that on account of which a thing moves from non-existence to existence. And likewise for the composite: what is sought is either the first of them or the second, and that is known in full in the definitional (haddiyya) and descriptive (rasmiyya) definitions later, God willing.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines two ways of knowing the essence (mahiya): either by itself (essential knowledge) or by its accompaniments (accidental knowledge). The author illustrates this with two examples: knowing heat by sense of touch gives direct essential knowledge of heat, while knowing that the world is temporally originated (hadith) through proofs gives only accidental knowledge—we know the world's origin but not the essence of the originated thing. These two types correspond to two kinds of questions about simple essences: the first asks for the thing itself (e.g., 'What is heat?'), the second asks for its defining cause (e.g., 'What is the originated?'). The passage then extends this to composite essences and states that the full treatment will come in the discussion of definition (hadd) and description (rasm). This is a foundational distinction for the science of logic, separating what a thing is from its attributes.

Key Terms
الماهية
بحسب ذاتها
توابعها
التعريفات الحدية
التعريفات الرسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of knowing a thing by its essence (bi dhatiha)?
A) Knowing that the world is temporally originated.
B) Knowing heat by the sense of touch.
C) Knowing that an action is obligatory.
D) Knowing that a person is mortal.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of an essence can be either direct (essential) or through its accompaniments (accidental); this distinction underlies the difference between hadd and rasm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify: 'Knowing that prayer is obligatory' vs 'Knowing the essence of prayer' — which is essential knowledge and which is accidental?
?
Identify: The statement 'Wine is an intoxicating beverage' — does this give essential or accidental knowledge? Would it be classified as hadd or rasm?

What Is Mantiq?

The Correct Definition of the Accidental Universal
Defines the accidental universal, critiques false definitions, and enumerates its types and properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid definitions of al-'arad al-'amm and explain why they exclude other universals.

Core Idea
The accidental universal is non-essential predication over multiple species.

The accidental universal is one of the five universals. In this passage, the author presents several valid definitions that all center on the idea of non-essential predication over many species. Understanding these definitions is crucial for distinguishing the accidental universal from the other four universals.

Guided Path
1
Locate the valid definitions
Find where the author says 'such as what is said' three times. These are the three valid definitions of the accidental universal.
The definitions are: (1) predicated of many differing in species by non-essential predication; (2) predicate of two species by non-essential predication; (3) accidental universal predicated of individuals of species.
2
Identify what each part excludes
The author states that 'accidental universal' excludes genus, species, and differentia. 'Predicated of individuals of species' excludes differentia and property. Understand why each exclusion is correct.
Genus, species, differentia are essential; property is predicated of only one species.
3
Address the objection about 'two species'
The author anticipates an objection: how can 'species' be plural if it is predicated of two species? He explains that 'species' here means two or more.
This clarifies that the accidental universal can apply to exactly two species as well.
4
Distinguish the example
The author says the accidental universal is like 'white' not 'whiteness'. Reflect on why 'white' is a non-essential predicate (it can be predicated of many substances) while 'whiteness' is a category of accident.
This establishes the logical vs. metaphysical distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في العرض العام ] وأما العرض العام فإنه يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة أيضًا : مثل ما يقال إنه المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع قولا غير ذاتي، ومثل ما يقال إنه المحمول على النوعين حملا غير ذاتي ، ومثل ما يقال إنه كلي عرضي مقول على الأشخاص من الأنواع، فقولنا كلي عرضي يخرج الجنس والنوع والفصل، وقولنا مقول على الأشخاص من الأنواع يخرج الفصل والخاصة. ولا يقال كيف يصح هذا وقد كان المقول على الأشخاص من النوعين هو العرض العام، فإن لفظ الجمع وهو الأنواع فيما نحن فيه يطلق على النوعين فصاعدا، وقد مرَّ من قبل أنه المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع في هذا وفي غيره كذلك، والكلام فيه وفي ذلك على السواء. ثم إنه وهو العرض العام في المثال كالأبيض لا كالبياض، وليس هذا العرض هو العرض الذي يناظر الجوهر كما يظنه أكثر الناس، فإن ذلك لا يحمل على موضوعه بأنه هو، وهذا من جملة ما يحمل عليه كالأبيض. فإنك تقول هذا أبيض أي جوهر شيء ذي بياض ليس بعرض يناظر الجوهر؛ بل الذي يناظره هو البياض، وذلك غير هذا العرض. فإن معنى العرض هنا العرضي، وإنه بإزاء الذاتي والجوهري بخلاف ذلك وهو العرض، فإنه بإزاء الذات والجوهر؛ والذاتي قد يكون جوهرًا وقد يكون عرضًا، فكذلك العرضي قد يكون جوهرًا وقد يكون عرضًا. وإنه يعرف بتعريفات فاسدة مثل ما يقال: إنه هو الذي يكون ويفسد من غير فساد الموضوع، وفيه من الفساد؛ فإنه إن عُني بالكون والفساد حال ما يكون ويفسد في الوجود فالعرض العام اللازم ليس كذلك، وإن عُني به ما يكون في الوجود والوهم جميعًا فكثيرا ما تكون الأمور العرضية غير الذاتية ما إذا رفع بالتوهم استحال أن يبقى ذلك الشيء كما كان. ومثل ما يقال هو الذي يمكن أن يوجد لشيء واحد بعينه، وأن لا يوجد، وفيه من الفساد أيضًا؛ فإن كثيرا من الأعراض ما يكون دائما، والدائم لا يمكن أن لا يوجد إلا في الوهم. ومثل ما يقال إنه الذي ليس بجنس ولا فصل ولا نوع ولا خاصة وهو أبدا قائم في موضوع، وفيه من الفساد ما فيه أيضًا؛ فإن كل شخص من الأعراض يشارك فيه؛ إذ الطبائع من حيث هي طبائع لا من حيث هي كلية. فإن الحق به أنه كلي بهذه الصفة يكون مطابقا، لكن قوله «إنه قائم في موضوع على ظن أن ما يكون من الخمسة هو العرض الذي يناظر الجوهر»، فذلك باطل لما مر من قبل أن المعنى من العرض هنا هو العرضي. وإنه قد يكون عاما للجنس كالوجود وقد يكون عاما للنوع دون الجنس كالمتنفس فإنه عرض عام للإنسان وخاصة للحيوان. وكذلك قد يكون لازمًا كالموجود بالقوة وقد لا يكون بل يكون مفارقًا. وذلك قد يكون عاماً يعم جميع الأفراد كالمتنفس وقد لا يكون فيكون على ثلاثة أقسام على مثال الخاصة؛ إذ لا يكون لازما من الخاصة والعرض العام، فذلك قد يكون مفارقًا سريع الزوال كحمرة الخجل وصفرة الوجل وقد يكون مفارقاً بطيء الزوال كالشيب والشباب. فخاصة الخاص كالضاحك
Section: [On the Accidental Universal]. As for the accidental universal, it is known by various definitions as well: such as what is said: it is predicated of many differing in species by a non-essential predication; and such as what is said: it is the predicate of two species by a non-essential predication; and such as what is said: it is an accidental universal predicated of individuals of species. So our saying 'accidental universal' excludes genus, species, and differentia; and our saying 'predicated of individuals of species' excludes differentia and property. And it cannot be said: how is this correct, given that what is predicated of individuals of two species is the accidental universal? For the plural term 'species' here is applied to two species or more, and it has already passed that it is predicated of many differing in species, both in this and in other contexts, and the discussion about it and that is the same. Then, the accidental universal is in example like 'white' not like 'whiteness'. And this accident is not the accident that is contrasted with substance, as most people think; for that one is not predicated of its subject as being it, while this is among what is predicated of it, like 'white'. For you say 'this is white', meaning a substance possessing whiteness, not an accident contrasted with substance; rather, that which is contrasted with it is whiteness, and that is other than this accident. For the meaning of 'accident' here is 'accidental', and it is opposite to 'essential' and 'substantial'; contrary to that, 'accident' is opposite to 'essence' and 'substance'; and the essential may be substance or accident, likewise the accidental may be substance or accident. And it is known by corrupt definitions, such as what is said: it is what comes to be and passes away without the subject's corruption. In it is corruption: for if by 'coming to be and passing away' is meant the state of its coming to be and passing away in existence, then the necessary accidental universal is not like that; and if by it is meant what is in existence and estimation together, then many non-essential accidental matters are such that if they are removed by estimation, it becomes impossible for that thing to remain as it was. And such as what is said: it is what can exist for one identical thing and not exist; in it is corruption also: for many accidents are perpetual, and the perpetual cannot not exist except in estimation. And such as what is said: it is what is not genus, differentia, species, or property, and is always subsistent in a subject; in it is corruption also: for every individual of accidents participates in it, since natures as natures, not as universals. For the truth regarding it is that it is a universal with this attribute being corresponding; but his saying 'it is subsistent in a subject' under the assumption that what is among the five is the accident that contrasts with substance—that is false, as has passed, because the meaning of 'accident' here is 'accidental'. And it may be general to the genus like existence, and may be general to the species without the genus like 'breathing', for it is an accidental universal for man and a property for animal. And likewise it may be necessary like 'potential existent' and may not be but rather separable. And that may be general encompassing all individuals like 'breathing', and may not be, so it is of three divisions like the example of property; since it is not necessary from property and accidental universal, so that may be quickly separable like the redness of bashfulness and the paleness of fear, and may be slowly separable like gray hair and youth. So the property of property is like 'laughing'.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage defines the accidental universal (al-'arad al-'amm), one of the five universals. The author begins by stating that the accidental universal is known through various definitions: it is predicated of many things differing in species by a non-essential predication (qawl ghayr dhati). Alternative definitions include that it is predicated of two species non-essentially, or that it is an accidental universal predicated of individuals of species. The phrase 'accidental universal' (kulli 'aradi) excludes genus, species, and differentia (since they are essential), while 'predicated of individuals of species' excludes differentia and property (al-khassa), because differentia is predicated of the species itself and property is predicated only of all individuals of one species. The author addresses a potential objection: how can the definition say 'predicated of individuals of species' in the plural? The answer is that 'species' here refers to two or more species, as established earlier. Next, the author clarifies that the accidental universal is exemplified by 'white' (abyad) not 'whiteness' (bayad), because 'white' is predicated of a substance as an attribute, whereas 'whiteness' is a substance-like accident (the accident contrasted with substance, i.e., the category of quality). Most people confuse the logical accidental universal with the metaphysical accident (the category), but the logical accidental universal is 'accidental' (aradi) meaning non-essential, not the accident that is a category. The essential (dhati) may itself be substance or accident, so the accidental universal can also be substance or accident in reality. The author then discusses corrupt definitions (ta'rifat fasida). The first: 'what comes to be and passes away without the subject's corruption'—this fails because some accidental universals are necessary (lazim) and do not pass away, and if by 'coming to be and passing away' is meant in estimation, then even essential properties could be removed in estimation, causing the subject not to remain. The second: 'what can exist for a thing and not exist'—this fails because many accidents are perpetual and cannot be absent except in estimation. The third: 'what is not genus, differentia, species, or property and always subsists in a subject'—this fails because it implicitly assumes the metaphysical accident, while the accidental universal here is the logical non-essential universal. Moreover, all individuals of accidents share in being accidents, so this definition is too broad. Finally, the author enumerates properties of the accidental universal: it may be general to the genus (like 'existence') or general to the species but not the genus (like 'breathing', which is an accidental universal for man but a property for animal). It may be necessary (like 'potential existent') or separable (mufariq). Separable types may be general to all individuals or not, and they may be quickly separable (like blush) or slowly separable (like gray hair). The passage concludes by noting that the 'property of property' (khassa al-khass) is like 'laughing', implicitly contrasting it with the accidental universal.

Key Terms
العرض العام
العرضي
الذاتي
الخاصة
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is a correct definition of the accidental universal (al-'arad al-'amm)?
A) It is what comes to be and passes away without the subject's corruption.
B) It is predicated of many differing in species by a non-essential predication.
C) It is what can exist for a thing and not exist.
D) It is always subsistent in a subject, like the accident contrasted with substance.

Key Takeaway
The accidental universal is defined as a non-essential predicate that applies to multiple species, distinguishing it from essential universals and property.

Given the five universals, take the term 'human' and determine which of the five it is. Then take the term 'white' and determine which it is, citing the definitions from this passage.

What Is Mantiq?

Distinguishing the Logical Accidental from the Metaphysical Accident
Defines the accidental universal, critiques false definitions, and enumerates its types and properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical accidental universal from the metaphysical accident (category of accident) and explain the shift in meaning of 'arad.

Core Idea
The accidental universal is about non-essential predication, not the category of accident.

A key point in the passage is the clarification that the accidental universal is not the same as the accident that is contrasted with substance (the category). Most people confuse them. The author explains that 'arad here means 'aradi (non-essential), and can apply to substances or accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of 'arad
The author distinguishes between al-'arad al-'amm (the accidental universal) and al-'arad (the category of accident). Find where he says 'the accident that is contrasted with substance'.
The category of accident is something like whiteness, which is a substance-like entity.
2
Understand the example: white vs. whiteness
The author says the accidental universal is like 'white' not 'whiteness'. 'White' is a predicate that can be said of a substance (e.g., 'this is white') non-essentially. 'Whiteness' is a quality category that is not predicated of a substance as being it.
You say 'this is white' but not 'this is whiteness'.
3
Contrast 'aradi with dhati
The author states that 'the meaning of accident here is 'aradi, and it is opposite to dhati (essential).' Essential can be substance or accident; likewise, non-essential can be substance or accident.
This shows that the logical accidental universal can apply to substances or accidents in reality.

Section: [On the Accidental Universal]. As for the accidental universal, it is known by various definitions as well: such as what is said: it is predicated of many differing in species by a non-essential predication; and such as what is said: it is the predicate of two species by a non-essential predication; and such as what is said: it is an accidental universal predicated of individuals of species. Most people confuse the logical accidental universal with the metaphysical accident (the category), but the logical accidental universal is 'accidental' (aradi) meaning non-essential, not the accident that is a category. The essential (dhati) may itself be substance or accident, so the accidental universal can also be substance or accident in reality. The author then discusses corrupt definitions (ta'rifat fasida). The first: 'what comes to be and passes away without the subject's corruption'—this fails because some accidental universals are necessary (lazim) and do not pass away, and if by 'coming to be and passing away' is meant in estimation, then even essential properties could be removed in estimation, causing the subject not to remain. The second: 'what can exist for a thing and not exist'—this fails because many accidents are perpetual and cannot be absent except in estimation. The third: 'what is not genus, differentia, species, or property and always subsists in a subject'—this fails because it implicitly assumes the metaphysical accident, while the accidental universal here is the logical non-essential universal. Moreover, all individuals of accidents share in being accidents, so this definition is too broad. Finally, the author enumerates properties of the accidental universal: it may be general to the genus (like 'existence') or general to the species but not the genus (like 'breathing', which is an accidental universal for man but a property for animal). It may be necessary (like 'potential existent') or separable (mufariq). Separable types may be general to all individuals or not, and they may be quickly separable (like blush) or slowly separable (like gray hair). The passage concludes by noting that the 'property of property' (khassa al-khass) is like 'laughing', implicitly contrasting it with the accidental universal.

Key Terms

العرضي
الجوهر
العرض

B) It is always a metaphysical accident.
C) It is non-essential predication and can apply to substances.
D) It is only predicated of accidents.

Key Takeaway
The accidental universal is a logical term meaning non-essential predication, not the category of accident.

Why These Three Definitions of the Accidental Universal Are Corrupt
Defines the accidental universal, critiques false definitions, and enumerates its types and properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three corrupt definitions of the accidental universal and explain why each fails.

Core Idea
Accidental universals can be necessary or perpetual, so definitions based on changeability or separability are flawed.

The author presents three common definitions that are corrupt (fasida). Each is refuted by considering types of accidental universals that are necessary or perpetual. Understanding these refutations deepens your grasp of the accidental universal.

Guided Path
1
Find the first corrupt definition
It says: 'it is what comes to be and passes away without the subject's corruption.' Locate this in the text.
2
Analyze the refutation
The author argues that if by 'coming to be and passing away' is meant in existence, then necessary accidental universals (like potential existent) do not pass away. If meant in estimation, then even essential matters can be removed in imagination.
This shows the definition is too narrow or too broad.
3
Find the second corrupt definition
It says: 'it is what can exist for a thing and not exist'.
4
Analyze the refutation
The author says many accidents are perpetual and cannot not exist except in estimation. So the definition fails for perpetual accidental universals.
5
Find the third corrupt definition
It says: 'it is what is not genus, differentia, species, or property and always subsists in a subject'.

أبيض أي جوهر شيء ذي بياض ليس بعرض يناظر الجوهر؛ بل الذي يناظره هو البياض، وذلك غير هذا العرض. فإن معنى العرض هنا العرضي، وإنه بإزاء الذاتي والجوهري بخلاف ذلك وهو العرض، فإنه بإزاء الذات والجوهر؛ والذاتي قد يكون جوهرًا وقد يكون عرضًا، فكذلك العرضي قد يكون جوهرًا وقد يكون عرضًا. وإنه يعرف بتعريفات فاسدة مثل ما يقال: إنه هو الذي يكون ويفسد من غير فساد الموضوع، وفيه من الفساد؛ فإنه إن عُني بالكون والفساد حال ما يكون ويفسد في الوجود فالعرض العام اللازم ليس كذلك، وإن عُني به ما يكون في الوجود والوهم جميعًا فكثيرا ما تكون الأمور العرضية غير الذاتية ما إذا رفع بالتوهم استحال أن يبقى ذلك الشيء كما كان. ومثل ما يقال هو الذي يمكن أن يوجد لشيء واحد بعينه، وأن لا يوجد، وفيه من الفساد أيضًا؛ فإن كثيرا من الأعراض ما يكون دائما، والدائم لا يمكن أن لا يوجد إلا في الوهم. ومثل ما يقال إنه الذي ليس بجنس لكن قوله «إنه قائم في موضوع على ظن أن ما يكون من الخمسة هو العرض الذي يناظر الجوهر»، فذلك باطل لما مر من قبل أن المعنى من العرض هنا هو العرضي. وإنه قد يكون عاما للجنس كالوجود وقد يكون عاما للنوع دون الجنس كالمتنفس فإنه عرض عام للإنسان وخاصة للحيوان. وكذلك قد يكون لازمًا كالموجود بالقوة وقد لا يكون بل يكون مفارقًا. وذلك قد يكون عاماً يعم جميع الأفراد كالمتنفس وقد لا يكون فيكون على ثلاثة أقسام على مثال الخاصة؛ إذ لا يكون لازما من الخاصة والعرض العام، فذلك قد يكون مفارقًا سريع الزوال كحمرة الخجل وصفرة الوجل وقد يكون مفارقاً بطيء الزوال كالشيب والشباب. فخاصة الخاص كالضاحك
Section: [On the Accidental Universal]. As for the accidental universal, it is known by various definitions as well: such as what is said: it is predicated of many differing in species by a non-essential predication; and such as what is said: it is the predicate of two species by a non-essential predication; and such as what is said: it is an accidental universal predicated of individuals of species. So our saying 'accidental universal' excludes genus, species, and differentia; and our saying 'predicated of individuals of species' excludes differentia and property. And it cannot be said: how is this correct, given that what is predicated of individuals of two species is the accidental universal? For the plural term 'species' here is applied to two species or more, and it has already passed that it is predicated of many differing in species, both in this and in other contexts, and the discussion about it and that is the same. Then, the accidental universal is in example like 'white' not like 'whiteness'. And this accident is not the accident that is contrasted with substance, as most people think; for that one is not predicated of its subject as being it, while this is among what is predicated of it, like 'white'. For you say 'this is white', meaning a substance possessing whiteness, not an accident contrasted with substance; rather, that which is contrasted with it is whiteness, and that is other than this accident. For the meaning of 'accident' here is 'accidental', and it is opposite to 'essential' and 'substantial'; contrary to that, 'accident' is opposite to 'essence' and 'substance'; and the essential may be substance or accident, likewise the accidental may be substance or accident. And it is known by corrupt definitions, such as what is said: it is what comes to be and passes away without the subject's corruption. In it is corruption: for if by 'coming to be and passing away' is meant the state of its coming to be and passing away in existence, then the necessary accidental universal is not like that; and if by it is meant what is in existence and estimation together, then many non-essential accidental matters are such that if they are removed by estimation, it becomes impossible for that thing to remain as it was. And such as what is said: it is what can exist for one identical thing and not exist; in it is corruption also: for many accidents are perpetual, and the perpetual cannot not exist except in estimation. And such as what is said: it is what is not genus, differentia, species, or property, and is always subsistent in a subject; in it is corruption also: for every individual of accidents participates in it, since natures as natures, not as universals. For the truth regarding it is that it is a universal with this attribute being corresponding; but his saying 'it is subsistent in a subject' under the assumption that what is among the five is the accident that contrasts with substance—that is false, as has passed, because the meaning of 'accident' here is 'accidental'. And it may be general to the genus like existence, and may be general to the species without the genus like 'breathing', for it is an accidental universal for man and a property for animal. And likewise it may be necessary like 'potential existent' and may not be but rather separable. And that may be general encompassing all individuals like 'breathing', and may not be, so it is of three divisions like the example of property; since it is not necessary from property and accidental universal, so that may be quickly separable like the redness of bashfulness and the paleness of fear, and may be slowly separable like gray hair and youth. So the property of property is like 'laughing'.

Commentary
This passage defines the accidental universal (al-'arad al-'amm), one of the five universals. The author begins by stating that the accidental universal is known through various definitions: it is predicated of many things differing in species by a non-essential predication (qawl ghayr dhati). Alternative definitions include that it is predicated of two species non-essentially, or that it is an accidental universal predicated of individuals of species. The phrase 'accidental universal' (kulli 'aradi) excludes genus, species, and differentia (since they are essential), while 'predicated of individuals of species' excludes differentia and property (al-khassa), because differentia is predicated of the species itself and property is predicated only of all individuals of one species. The author addresses a potential objection: how can the definition say 'predicated of individuals of species' in the plural? The answer is that 'species' here refers to two or more species, as established earlier. Next, the author clarifies that the accidental universal is exemplified by 'white' (abyad) not 'whiteness' (bayad), because 'white' is predicated of a substance as an attribute, whereas 'whiteness' is a substance-like accident (the accident contrasted with substance, i.e., the category of quality). Most people confuse the logical accidental universal with the metaphysical accident (the category), but the logical accidental universal is 'accidental' (aradi) meaning non-essential, not the accident that is a category. The essential (dhati) may itself be substance or accident, so the accidental universal can also be substance or accident in reality. The author then discusses corrupt definitions (ta'rifat fasida). The first: 'what comes to be and passes away without the subject's corruption'—this fails because some accidental universals are necessary (lazim) and do not pass away, and if by 'coming to be and passing away' is meant in estimation, then even essential properties could be removed in estimation, causing the subject not to remain. The second: 'what can exist for a thing and not exist'—this fails because many accidents are perpetual and cannot be absent except in estimation. The third: 'what is not genus, differentia, species, or property and always subsists in a subject'—this fails because it implicitly assumes the metaphysical accident, while the accidental universal here is the logical non-essential universal. Moreover, all individuals of accidents share in being accidents, so this definition is too broad. Finally, the author enumerates properties of the accidental universal: it may be general to the genus (like 'existence') or general to the species but not the genus (like 'breathing', which is an accidental universal for man but a property for animal). It may be necessary (like 'potential existent') or separable (mufariq). Separable types may be general to all individuals or not, and they may be quickly separable (like blush) or slowly separable (like gray hair). The passage concludes by noting that the 'property of property' (khassa al-khass) is like 'laughing', implicitly contrasting it with the accidental universal.

Divisions of the Accidental Universal: Necessary, Separable, General, Specific
Defines the accidental universal, critiques false definitions, and enumerates its types and properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize accidental universals as necessary or separable, and as general to genus or specific to species, and provide examples.

Core Idea
The accidental universal has multiple subtypes based on necessity and generality.

After defining the accidental universal and refuting false definitions, the author enumerates its divisions: it may be necessary (lazim) or separable (mufariq), and it may be general to the genus or specific to the species not the genus. Separable types are further divided by speed of separation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
The author says the accidental universal may be 'general to the genus' (like existence) or 'general to the species without the genus' (like breathing for man).
'Breathing' is an accidental universal for man but a property for animal.
2
Distinguish necessary vs separable
The author gives examples: 'potential existent' (necessary) vs separable types like blush (quickly separable) or gray hair (slowly separable).
Necessary accidental universals are inseparable from the subject in reality.
3
Understand the threefold division like property
The author says the accidental universal has three divisions like the property, but they relate to separability: quickly separable, slowly separable, and necessary (implied).
This parallels the division of property into necessary and non-necessary.

It may be necessary (like 'potential existent') or separable (mufariq). Separable types may be general to all individuals or not, and they may be quickly separable (like blush) or slowly separable (like gray hair). The passage concludes by noting that the 'property of property' (khassa al-khass) is like 'laughing', implicitly contrasting it with the accidental universal.

Key Terms
العرض العام
اللازم
المفارق
العام للجنس
العام للنوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an accidental universal that is general to the species but not the genus?
A) Existence
B) Breathing for man
C) Laughing
D) Whiteness

Key Takeaway
Accidental universals can be necessary or separable, and can cover a genus or just a species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Essential vs Accidental Predication
Author distinguishes two types of predication: essential and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential predication (where the subject is the predicate) from accidental predication (where the subject has the predicate but is not it) using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Predication is either essential (subject = predicate) or accidental (subject has predicate).

In logic, not all subject-predicate sentences are equal. The author explains that some predicates tell us what the subject is (essential), while others describe an attribute (accidental). Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating definitions and arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the first part: the relation of the predicate to the subject is of two types. One where the subject can be said to be the predicate (essential), and one where it cannot (accidental).
2
Analyze the essential example
The author gives 'man is animal' as essential predication. Notice that 'animal' is a genus that constitutes the essence of man. The subject is the predicate.
This is the core of definitional predication.
3
Analyze the accidental example
The author gives 'the garment is white' as accidental. The predicate 'white' is derived from whiteness, but the garment is not whiteness itself; it merely possesses it.
Accidental predicates are often derived terms (e.g., 'white' from 'whiteness').
4
Apply the distinction
Consider the statement 'Wine is forbidden' from usul al-fiqh. Is it essential or accidental? (Answer: accidental, because wine is not identical to 'forbiddenness'; it is a ruling attributed to it.)
This prepares you for real examples in Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينسب إلى الموضوع تكون نسبته إليه على وجهين أحدهما أنه بحيث يمكن أن يقال إن الموضوع هو كالحيوان الذي يمكن أن يقال إن الإنسان هو جنس يقال إن الإنسان حيوان، ومثل هذا فهو المحمول على الموضوع. وثانيهما أنه بحيث لا يمكن كالبياض الذي لا يمكن أن يقال لموضوعه الذي هو الثوب مثلا أنه هو فلا يقال إنه بياض؛ بل يقال إنه ذو بياض أو أبيض، وهذا لفظ مشتق من البياض أو مؤلف منه ومن غيره. والموضوع المجرد عن السور لا يخلو من أن يكون كليا أو جزئيًا، فإن كان جزئيًا فالمحمول عليه إن كان جزئيا لم يكن ذلك غيره فإن الجزئيين المتابينين لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر فلا يمكن أن يقال إن هذه الإنسان ذلك الإنسان. وإذا لم يكن ذلك غيره لم يكن في الحقيقة موضوعًا ومحمولًا بحسب الطبع بل بحسب القول كما يقال زيد هو أبو القاسم أو ابن عمرو، وكذلك إذا قلت هذا الأبيض هو هذا الكاتب. وإن كان كليًّا فالمحمول عليه بالحقيقة لا يكون إلا كليا فإن طبيعة الكلي لا تكون موضوعة بنفسها للشخصية من غير إلحاق السور الجزئي به وإلا لكانت الطبيعة الجزئية مستحقة في طبعها لأن يكون هذا المشار إليه، هذا إذا كان كونه على موضوع مفهوما على ما قلناه فأما إذا لم يكن ؛ بل كونه على موضوع دالا على أنه مقول على كثيرين كان هذا القول مرادفا لهذا الاسم، وكان في ترك ذلك اللفظ زيادة أمر لا فائدة فيه؛ بل الأحرى أن يسلك السبيل الذي سلكناه، ولو قلتَ جزئي وكلي لكان فيه كفاء به.
Then that which is attributed to the subject has its relation to it in two ways: one of them is that it is such that it can be said that the subject is it—like 'animal', which can be said that man is a genus—it is said 'man is an animal', and such is the predicate 'said of' the subject. The second is that it is such that it cannot, like 'whiteness', which cannot be said of its subject, e.g., the garment, that it is it—so it is not said 'it is whiteness'; rather, it is said 'it is possessor of whiteness' or 'white', and this is a word derived from whiteness or composed of it and something else. The subject stripped of quantifiers is either universal or particular. If it is particular, then the predicate, if particular, is not other than it—for two distinct particulars cannot be predicated of each other, so it cannot be said 'this man is that man'. And if it is not other than it, then it is not truly a subject and predicate by nature but only by speech, as when one says 'Zaid is Abu al-Qasim' or 'son of Amr', and likewise when you say 'this white thing is this writer'. But if it is universal, then the predicate truly is only universal, for the nature of the universal is not set as a subject for the particular without attaching a particular quantifier to it; otherwise the particular nature would be inherently worthy of being this indicated thing. This is if its being 'said of a subject' is understood as we have said. But if it is not; rather, its being 'said of a subject' indicates that it is said of many, then this expression would be synonymous with this name, and in omitting that word there would be an additional matter with no benefit; rather, it is more appropriate to follow the path we have taken, and if you said 'particular' and 'universal', it would suffice.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage defines the two ways a predicate can relate to a subject. First, essential predication: the predicate expresses what the subject is, e.g., 'Man is an animal' — here 'animal' is predicated essentially. Second, accidental predication: the predicate does not express the subject's essence but an attribute, e.g., 'The garment is white' — we do not say 'the garment is whiteness' but 'the garment is white' (derived term). The author then discusses conditions for genuine subject-predicate relation. If the subject is particular (e.g., 'Zaid'), a particular predicate like 'this man' would not be other than it, so it is not a real predication (e.g., 'Zaid is Zaid' or 'Zaid is Abu al-Qasim' is merely verbal). True predication requires a universal subject and a universal predicate. The author also critiques a view that interprets 'said of a subject' as merely meaning 'said of many', arguing that this is redundant since 'universal' already covers that meaning; his own definition is more precise.

Key Terms
المحمول على الموضوع (al-mahmul 'ala al-mawdu'): Predicate said of subject; first type: essential (subject is it), second type: accidental (subject is not it but has it). [Note: In earlier logic, this distinction is foundational; later commentators expand on essential/accidental predication.]
الجزئي (al-juz'i): Particular; an individual that cannot be predicated of another particular genuinely. [Note: No significant shift.]
الكلي (al-kulli): Universal; a term that can be predicated of many, and is required for true predication. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of accidental predication according to this passage?
A) 'Man is an animal'
B) 'The garment is white'
C) 'Zaid is Zaid'
D) 'The writer is this white thing'

Key Takeaway
The two types of predication — essential (subject = predicate) and accidental (subject has predicate) — are the basis for analyzing definitions and descriptions in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Conditions for Genuine Predication: Universals Required
Author discusses conditions for genuine predication and critiques an alternative definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given subject-predicate pair constitutes a genuine logical predication based on the universal/particular condition proposed by the author.

Core Idea
True predication requires both subject and predicate to be universals; particular pairs are merely verbal.

The author explains that for a statement to be a genuine predication, the subject must be a universal; otherwise, the predication is only verbal (e.g., 'Zaid is Abu al-Qasim'). He also critiques an alternative definition of 'said of a subject' as redundant. This lesson unpacks these conditions and their significance for logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The author states that the subject stripped of quantifiers is either universal or particular. If the subject is particular, any predicate that is also particular cannot be truly predicated.
Genuine predication requires the subject to be universal.
2
Examine particular examples
The author gives examples: 'Zaid is Abu al-Qasim' and 'this white thing is this writer'. These are not real predications because the subject and predicate are not truly distinct; they are the same particular.
Such statements are only verbally predicative.
3
Understand the universal requirement
When the subject is universal, the predicate must also be universal. The nature of a universal is that it can be said of many, so it cannot be made a subject for a particular without a quantifier.
This preserves the logical structure of predication.
4
Analyze the critique of an alternative view
The author considers a view that 'said of a subject' means 'said of many'. He argues that this is synonymous with 'universal' and adds no benefit; his own definition is more precise.
This shows the author's commitment to a definition that captures the essence of predication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Defining Substance (al-Jawhar)
Author defines substance by its quiddity not being in a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the definition of substance (jawhar) as a quiddity that is not in a subject.

Core Idea
Substance is defined by its quiddity's mode of existence, not by external existence.

The author begins by stating that the composite exists potentially, but quickly moves to the core issue: what is a substance? He defines it not by its existence in the world but by the nature of its quiddity. This distinction is crucial for understanding later arguments about universals. In this lesson, we will dissect the definition and test it on examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-jawhar' (substance). Find where the author states its meaning. The passage says: 'المعنى بالجوهر هو الشيء الذي من حق وجود الماهية له في الأعيان أن تكون لا في موضوع'.
'المعنى بالجوهر' indicates that a definition follows.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'شيء' (thing) or 'ماهية' (quiddity) – but the definition is given by a property. The differentia is that its quiddity, when existing in individuals, is not in a subject. This is a negative differentia, making the definition a rasm (description) rather than a hadd haqiqi.
In Islamic logic, a hadd uses positive essential properties; a rasm uses accidents or negative properties.
3
Test the definition with examples
Apply the definition: Is 'human' a substance? Its quiddity 'humanity' does not require being in a subject to exist; it can exist as a particular human. Therefore, it is a substance. Is 'whiteness' a substance? Whiteness exists in a subject (e.g., a white wall), so it is an accident.
4
Distinguish attachment from essentiality
The author notes that some attributes (lawāḥiq) attach to substance without destroying substantiality. These are necessary concomitants or accidents. The definition focuses on the quiddity itself, not on later attachments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحده للمركب وجود بالفعل بل بالقوة، والصورة هي ما بها يصير المركب هو ما هو بالفعل؛ وجميع ذلك إما أن يوجد كليا، وإما أن يوجد جزئيا. ولما كان المعنى بالجوهر هو الشيء الذي من حق وجود الماهية له في الأعيان أن تكون لا في موضوع وجب أن تكون ماهية الإنسان بحقيقتها جوهرا، فالإنسان مثلا إنما هو جوهر ؛ لأنه إنسان لا لأنه موجود في الأعيان نحوا من الوجود، وإنه إذا كان جوهرًا؛ لأنه إنسان مما لحقه من بعد؛ فذلك أمر يلحق جوهرًا. ولواحق الجوهر لوازم وأعراض لا تبطل معها جوهريته، فالأشخاص إذن في الأعيان جواهر؛ فما يكون من المعقولات الكلية فذلك أيضًا جوهر وإلا لما صح عليه أنه ماهية حقها في الوجود أن لا يكون في الموضوع؛ والمشارك للجوهر بماهية جوهر لا محالة. ولذلك فإن حد النوع من حيث هو طبيعة وحد للجنس أيضًا من حيث هو طبيعة ومحمولان على الأشخاص، فهي إذن جواهر؛ وما يشاركها في حدها فكذلك . ولو كانت جوهريتها لما أنها موجودة في الأعيان فكيفيته بالأعراض لكانت جوهرية الأمور عارضة لماهيتها؛ إذ صح أن الوجود عارض في هذه الماهيات ولكانت العوارض تجعل ما ليس في نفسه بجوهر جوهرًا فيكون شيء عرض له إن كان جوهرا، فتكون الجوهرية عارضة لشيء؛ وذلك محال فكليات الجواهر جواهر في ماهياتها.
The definition of the composite has existence in actuality but by potentiality, and the form is that by which the composite becomes what it is in actuality. All of that either exists universally or exists particularly. Since the meaning of substance is the thing whose mode of existence of the quiddity in individuals is that it is not in a subject, it is necessary that the quiddity of man by its reality be a substance. For man, for example, is a substance because he is man, not because he exists in individuals in a certain mode of existence. And if he is a substance because he is man, due to something that attaches to him later, then that is something that attaches to a substance. The attachments of substance are necessary concomitants and accidents that do not annul its substantiality. Therefore, individuals in individuals are substances; what is of universal concepts is also substance, otherwise it would not be true that it is a quiddity whose right in existence is not to be in a subject. And what participates with substance in quiddity is a substance without doubt. Therefore, the definition of the species insofar as it is a nature is also the definition of the genus insofar as it is a nature, and both are predicated of individuals. So they are substances; and what participates in their definition is likewise. And if their substantiality were because they exist in individuals, then their quality by accidents would cause the substantiality of things to be accidental to their quiddity. Since existence would be accidental in these quiddities, and accidents would make what is not in itself a substance into a substance, so something would be such that substantiality would be accidental to it; that is impossible. So the universals of substances are substances in their quiddities.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines substance (jawhar) and argues that universal concepts of substances are themselves substances. The author begins by discussing the composite (murakkab) in terms of matter and form, but quickly moves to the core definition: substance is that whose quiddity (mahiyya) in individuals exists not in a subject. For example, 'human' is a substance because of its essence, not because of its existence in external individuals. If something becomes a substance later, that is an accident. The attachments (lawāḥiq) of substance are either necessary concomitants or accidents that do not destroy substantiality. Then the author argues that individual substances are obviously substances, but universal concepts (like the species 'human') are also substances because they share the same quiddity that requires not being in a subject. If universals were not substances, then someone participating in that quiddity would not be a substance. The definition of species (naw') as a nature is the same as the definition of genus (jins) as a nature, and both are predicated of individuals. If substantiality were due to existence in individuals, then accidents could turn non-substances into substances, which is absurd. Thus, universals of substances are substances in their quiddities.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الماهية
الكلي
الجزئي
اللاحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, something is a substance primarily because:
A) It exists in the external world.
B) Its quiddity is not in a subject.
C) It is a particular individual.
D) It has accidents attached.

Key Takeaway
Substance is defined by the quiddity's independence from a subject, not by external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the definition of substance as 'that whose quiddity exists not in a subject', determine whether 'justice' is a substance or an accident. Explain your reasoning.
?
Take the universal 'horse' (al-faras). According to the author, is it a substance? Why? Apply the definition to both the individual horse and the universal concept.

5 min
Why Universal Concepts of Substances Are Themselves Substances
Author argues that universal concepts of substances are themselves substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that universal concepts of substances are substances in their quiddities, and evaluate the logical steps.

Core Idea
Universals share the same quiddity as individuals, so they are substances.

After defining substance, the author tackles a deeper question: Are universal concepts like 'human' or 'animal' also substances? Some might think only particular individuals are substances. The author argues that universals are substances because they participate in the same quiddity that defines substance. In this lesson, we will walk through the argument step by step and understand the reductio ad absurdum that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author asserts: 'فما يكون من المعقولات الكلية فذلك أيضًا جوهر' – 'what is of universal concepts is also substance'. This is the main claim to be proven.
2
Find the premises
Premise 1: Individuals of a substance (e.g., particular humans) are substances because their quiddity is not in a subject. Premise 2: The universal (e.g., 'human' as a species) has the same quiddity as the individuals. Premise 3: 'والمشارك للجوهر بماهية جوهر لا محالة' – 'what participates with substance in quiddity is a substance without doubt.' Therefore, the universal is a substance.
The author uses the term 'mushārik' (participant) to link universal and individual.
3
Understand the reductio
The author imagines the opposite: if universals were not substances, then substantiality would be due to external existence. That would make substantiality an accident, and accidents could then make non-substances into substances, which is absurd. This strengthens the argument that universals are substances in their quiddities.
The reductio is found in: 'ولو كانت جوهريتها لما أنها موجودة في الأعيان... وذلك محال'.
4
Summarize the argument
The argument can be summarized as: (1) Substance is defined by quiddity not in a subject. (2) Individual substances have that quiddity. (3) Universal concepts share the same quiddity (as shown by having the same definition). (4) Therefore, universals are substances. (5) If not, substantiality would be accidental, leading to contradiction.
The conclusion: 'فكليات الجواهر جواهر في ماهياتها' – 'the universals of substances are substances in their quiddities'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحده للمركب وجود بالفعل بل بالقوة، والصورة هي ما بها يصير المركب هو ما هو بالفعل؛ وجميع ذلك إما أن يوجد كليا، وإما أن يوجد جزئيا. ولما كان المعنى بالجوهر هو الشيء الذي من حق وجود الماهية له في الأعيان أن تكون لا في موضوع وجب أن تكون ماهية الإنسان بحقيقتها جوهرا، فالإنسان مثلا إنما هو جوهر ؛ لأنه إنسان لا لأنه موجود في الأعيان نحوا من الوجود، وإنه إذا كان جوهرًا؛ لأنه إنسان مما لحقه من بعد؛ فذلك أمر يلحق جوهرًا. ولواحق الجوهر لوازم وأعراض لا تبطل معها جوهريته، فالأشخاص إذن في الأعيان جواهر؛ فما يكون من المعقولات الكلية فذلك أيضًا جوهر وإلا لما صح عليه أنه ماهية حقها في الوجود أن لا يكون في الموضوع؛ والمشارك للجوهر بماهية جوهر لا محالة. ولذلك فإن حد النوع من حيث هو طبيعة وحد للجنس أيضًا من حيث هو طبيعة ومحمولان على الأشخاص، فهي إذن جواهر؛ وما يشاركها في حدها فكذلك . ولو كانت جوهريتها لما أنها موجودة في الأعيان فكيفيته بالأعراض لكانت جوهرية الأمور عارضة لماهيتها؛ إذ صح أن الوجود عارض في هذه الماهيات ولكانت العوارض تجعل ما ليس في نفسه بجوهر جوهرًا فيكون شيء عرض له إن كان جوهرا، فتكون الجوهرية عارضة لشيء؛ وذلك محال فكليات الجواهر جواهر في ماهياتها.
The definition of the composite has existence in actuality but by potentiality, and the form is that by which the composite becomes what it is in actuality. All of that either exists universally or exists particularly. Since the meaning of substance is the thing whose mode of existence of the quiddity in individuals is that it is not in a subject, it is necessary that the quiddity of man by its reality be a substance. For man, for example, is a substance because he is man, not because he exists in individuals in a certain mode of existence. And if he is a substance because he is man, due to something that attaches to him later, then that is something that attaches to a substance. The attachments of substance are necessary concomitants and accidents that do not annul its substantiality. Therefore, individuals in individuals are substances; what is of universal concepts is also substance, otherwise it would not be true that it is a quiddity whose right in existence is not to be in a subject. And what participates with substance in quiddity is a substance without doubt. Therefore, the definition of the species insofar as it is a nature is also the definition of the genus insofar as it is a nature, and both are predicated of individuals. So they are substances; and what participates in their definition is likewise. And if their substantiality were because they exist in individuals, then their quality by accidents would cause the substantiality of things to be accidental to their quiddity. Since existence would be accidental in these quiddities, and accidents would make what is not in itself a substance into a substance, so something would be such that substantiality would be accidental to it; that is impossible. So the universals of substances are substances in their quiddities.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines substance (jawhar) and argues that universal concepts of substances are themselves substances. The author begins by discussing the composite (murakkab) in terms of matter and form, but quickly moves to the core definition: substance is that whose quiddity (mahiyya) in individuals exists not in a subject. For example, 'human' is a substance because of its essence, not because of its existence in external individuals. If something becomes a substance later, that is an accident. The attachments (lawāḥiq) of substance are either necessary concomitants or accidents that do not destroy substantiality. Then the author argues that individual substances are obviously substances, but universal concepts (like the species 'human') are also substances because they share the same quiddity that requires not being in a subject. If universals were not substances, then someone participating in that quiddity would not be a substance. The definition of species (naw') as a nature is the same as the definition of genus (jins) as a nature, and both are predicated of individuals. If substantiality were due to existence in individuals, then accidents could turn non-substances into substances, which is absurd. Thus, universals of substances are substances in their quiddities.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الماهية
الكلي
الجزئي
المشارك
الجنس
النوع

A) They would be accidents.
B) The definition of species and genus would be different.
C) Accidents could turn non-substances into substances.
D) The quiddity would be in a subject.

Key Takeaway
Universals of substances are substances in their quiddities because they participate in the same definition as individuals.

Distinguishing Inherently Relative Terms from Non-Inherently Relative Terms
Author defines relative terms and distinguishes those that are inherently relational from those that are not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between terms that are inherently relative (like أكبر and أصغر) and terms that are only relative when considered with a relation (like علم and بياض), and explain the logical difference.

Core Idea
Some terms are relative by essence; others become relative only when their essence is taken together with a relation.

In this passage, the author unpacks the concept of relative terms (مضاف). He clarifies that not all things that cannot exist without another are truly relative. Rather, a term is truly relative only when its very essence is predicated in relation to another. This distinction is crucial for understanding categories in logic and for precise definition in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key concepts: relation (نسبة) vs. relative (إضافة).
Notice that the author begins by distinguishing relation (single-term) from relative (two-term). This sets the basis for the discussion.
2
List the examples of relatives provided.
The author gives examples: greater/smaller, double/half, power, ability, state, sense, knowledge, standing, sitting. Group them into two categories based on whether they are inherently relational.
3
Examine the criterion for inherent relativity.
The author says some terms are essentially predicated in relation to another, like 'big' (كبير) which cannot be conceived without 'small'. Others, like 'knowledge' (علم), have an essence that is not defined by relation, even though it exists only in relation to a knower.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example.
Consider 'whiteness' (بياض). Its essence is not relational; but when taken as 'white thing' (أبيض), it becomes relative because whiteness is predicated of the white object in relation to something else.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا المعنى من قولهم إن النسبة تكون لطرف، والإضافة لطرفين، وذلك بانعكاس النسبة فكل نسبة يوجد فيها الطرفان فهي إضافة. ثم من الأمور المضافة ما هو مثل الأكبر والأصغر والضعف والنصف ومنها ما هو مثل القوة والقدرة والحال؛ إذ القوة والقدرة قوة وقدرة لشيء على شيء؛ والحال حال لذي الحال، والحس حس حاس بمحسوس، والعلم علم عالم بمعلوم. وكذلك القيام قيام قائم والجلوس جلوس جالس؛ فهذه كلها مضافات لكنها منها ما هو مثل الكبير فإنه لا يكون في نفسه كبيرًا إلا أن يكون هناك صغير. وكذلك الشبه والمساوي. وأما القوة والقدرة والحس والعلم فنسبة أن لا يكون الأمر فيها كذلك فإن ماهية العلم إنما يقال له علم بالقياس إلى العالم وإن كان لا يوجد إلا في العالم. وفرق بين أن يكون الشيء لا يوجد إلا أن يوجد شيء وبين أن يكون مقولا بالقياس إلى شيء: فالعالم مثلا لا يوجد إلا بالباري تعالى وتقدس وليست ماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى الباري. وكذلك الثنائية لا توجد إلا بالوحدانية وليست ماهيتها مقولة بالقياس إلى الوحدانية؛ وليس الوجود والماهية شيئًا واحدا، ولا اقتران الماهية بالماهية هو قول الماهية بالقياس إلى الماهية بل كون الماهية بالقياس هو أن يكون الماهية حقيقة الكون مقارناً للمقارن. فإذن البعض من هذه الأمور ما يكون ماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى الغير مثل الكبير والصغير، والبعض منها لا يكون كذلك إلا إذا أخذت ماهية مع النسبة كالبياض مثلا فإنه إذا أخذ هو في الأبيض كان مضافاً.
This meaning comes from their saying: 'Relation is for one term, and relative is for two terms,' and that is by reciprocal relation, so every relation in which both terms exist is a relative. Then among relative things there is like the greater and the smaller, double and half, and among them there is like power, ability, and state; for power and ability are power and ability for something over something; and state is a state of the possessor of the state, sense is sense of a sensing thing by a sensed thing, and knowledge is knowledge of a knower of a known. Likewise, standing is standing of a stander, and sitting is sitting of a sitter; all these are relatives, but among them are like the big, for it is not in itself big unless there is a small. Likewise the similar and the equal. As for power, ability, sense, and knowledge, their relationship is not like that, for the essence of knowledge is only called knowledge in relation to the knower, even if it does not exist except in the knower. And there is a difference between a thing that does not exist except that another thing exists, and a thing that is predicated in relation to another thing: for example, the world does not exist except through the Creator, may He be exalted, but its essence is not predicated in relation to the Creator. Likewise, duality does not exist except through unity, but its essence is not predicated in relation to unity; and existence and essence are not one thing, nor is the conjunction of essence with essence the predication of essence in relation to essence, but rather the predication of essence in relation (to another) is that the essence is truly existing in conjunction with the conjunct. So then some of these things are essences predicated in relation to another, like big and small, and some are not like that unless the essence is taken with the relation, like whiteness for example, for if it is taken as being in the white thing, it becomes relative.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of relative terms (مضاف). The author begins by clarifying that relation (نسبة) involves one term, while relative (إضافة) involves two terms reciprocally. He then categorizes relatives into two types: those that are inherently relational, like 'greater and smaller' or 'double and half', where each term necessarily implies the other; and those that are not inherently relational but become relational when considered in a certain context, like power, ability, sense, and knowledge. For example, knowledge is called knowledge in relation to a knower, but its essence does not depend on the relation for its existence. The author emphasizes the distinction between something that cannot exist without another (like the world and its Creator) and something that is essentially predicated in relation to another. The key insight is that some things are relational in their very essence (like 'big' and 'small'), while others become relational only when their essence is taken together with a relation (like 'whiteness' when attributed to a white object). The passage ends with this classification, setting up the discussion of opposition in relatives on the next page.

Key Terms
نسبة (nisba): A relation considered from one term to another, not necessarily requiring two terms. [Note: In later logic, nisba is often used more broadly; here it distinguishes from idafa.]
مضاف (mudaf): A term that is relative to another, requiring both terms reciprocally. [Note: No significant shift—standard usage in Aristotelian logic.]
ماهية (mahiyya): Essence or quiddity of a thing; in this context, what makes a thing what it is, independent of relations. [Note: In Avicennan metaphysics, mahiyya is contrasted with existence; here it is used in the logical context of predication.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge (علم), because it requires a knower and a known.
B) Greater (أكبر), because it cannot be conceived without a smaller.
C) Whiteness (بياض), because it exists only in a white object.
D) Power (قوة), because it is power over something.

Key Takeaway
Relative terms (مضاف) are not all alike: some are essentially relative (like greater/smaller), while others become relative only when their essence is taken in relation (like knowledge and whiteness).

Defining Kalam: Two Words with Predication
Commentary clarifies the definition of speech as a composite of at least two words with predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary components of a valid kalam (speech) according to this logical commentary and distinguish it from incomplete utterances.

Core Idea
True kalam requires at least two words with predication and a complete meaning.

The definition of kalam (speech) is not trivial—it distinguishes meaningful utterances from mere words or particles. This commentary refines a standard definition by examining edge cases like proper names and conditional sentences. By parsing the conditions, you will learn how logicians and grammarians set boundaries for what counts as 'speech'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'kalam' (الكلام). Locate where the definition is stated: 'بأن الكلام ما يتضمن كلمتين بالإسناد'. What is being defined?
2
Extract the conditions
The definition has two key conditions: (1) comprises two words (كلمتين), and (2) includes predication (الإسناد). Note that a phrase like 'عبد الرحمان' has two words and predication but may not be considered complete kalam. Why?
The commentary suggests that additional completeness is required.
3
Analyze the counterexamples
The conditional sentence 'إن كان زيد في الدار' (if Zayd is in the house) has two words and predication but is not full kalam because it requires a following clause (the apodosis) for completeness. What does this tell you about the definition?
The definition may need an implicit condition of independence.
4
Understand the role of signifiers
The passage mentions three ways a word can signify its meaning: by correspondence (المطابقة), inclusion (التضمن), and implication (الالتزام). Notice how the word 'ما' (what) does not signify by any of these three? This distinguishes particles from full words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن الكلام ما يتضمن كلمتين [؟] بط [؟] عواقر؛ فذلك عشاري عندي إلا يتضمن كلمتين بالإسناد لأن عبد الرحمان جملة إسنادية تتضمن كلمتين مع زمانه مع ليس الكلام في إن كان زيد في الدار يتضمن كلمتين بالإسناد مع أنه ليس بكلام ما يليق أن يجار عن الأول والثاني أن المراد من قولنا يتضمن لفظ يتضمن، وعن الثالث المراد أن لا يتضمن أقل من كلمتين. أما من جهة الأكبر فلا يجري، وعن الرابع قدر بعد قوله بلا شيء فكلام وإلا لا يحسن السكوت عليه. وكذلك الجواب عنه والجوابات من السكوت لا بد وأن يكون لبعض المعرف وإلا على بعض المعرف إما بالمطابقة أو بالتضمن أو بالالتزام؛ ولفظ ما لا يدل على اللفظ بأحد الدلالات الثلاث؛ ولذا الكلام قال بعضهم الضمير الرفع في نفسه يوجه إليه فعلى أنه ما دل على معنى باعتباره به نفسه، والنظر إليه نفسه لا باعتبار أمر خارج عنه. ولذلك قيل لا الحرف ما دل على معنى غيره أي باعتبار متعلقة لا باعتبار ما لا لنفسه، ويمكن أن يقال تقديره ما دل على معنى مستقل بنفسه دون ذلك المتعلق.
That speech (kalam) is that which comprises two words [?] without [?]; so that is [?] according to me unless it comprises two words with predication (isnad), because 'Abd al-Rahman is a predicative sentence comprising two words with its time, while 'if Zayd is in the house' does not [in the same way] comprise two words with predication, yet it is not speech. [This is] what is appropriate to answer regarding the first and second [points]: that what is meant by our saying 'comprises' is the utterance that comprises. Regarding the third, what is meant is that it does not comprise less than two words. As for the greater [number], it does not apply. Regarding the fourth, after his saying 'without something', then it is speech, otherwise it is not proper to be silent upon it. Likewise, the answer about it and the answers from silence must be for some of the defined or else upon some of the defined either by correspondence (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadamun), or implication (iltizam). The word 'what' (ma) does not signify the utterance by one of the three signifiers. Therefore, regarding speech, some said the raised pronoun (damir raf') directs itself to it, so it is that which signifies a meaning by virtue of itself, and its consideration is itself not by virtue of something external to it. Therefore it was said 'no particle (harf) signifies a meaning other than itself', i.e., by virtue of its attachment, not by virtue of what is not for itself. And it can be said that its estimation is 'that which signifies a meaning independent in itself without that attachment.'

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definition of kalam (speech). The author appears to be commenting on a prior definition, likely from a grammarian or logician, that kalam is a composite of two words (كلمتين) with predication (إسناد). The passage examines various issues: whether the phrase 'عبد الرحمان' counts as kalam (it is a predicative phrase but may not be considered complete speech), and whether conditional clauses like 'إن كان زيد في الدار' (if Zayd is in the house) are kalam (they have two words and predication but are not considered full speech because they are incomplete without the apodosis). The author also discusses the implications of the definition regarding the number of words: at least two, but not necessarily more. There is a reference to signifiers: correspondence, inclusion, and implication (الدلالات الثلاث). The passage then discusses the nature of words and particles: some say that a particle signifies a meaning not by itself but by its attachment to another word. This is a technical grammatical-logical discussion. The OCR is very poor, so the exact argument is uncertain, but the general theme is the definition of kalam and the conditions for it to be considered a complete meaningful utterance.
